This is the image of the surgical instrument.
If the moisture is more and the swelling is larger, make two intersecting incisions in this form:.
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, its sign is that you will see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water will sink when you press it with your hand; this should not be hidden from you. It is appropriate to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this form:.
After the incision, all the moisture is drained out, then the incisions are sutured with bandages and ligatures. Then, it is covered from above with a poultice and oil until the fifth day. Afterwards, the bindings are removed and the wound is treated with lint and ointments. Do not stop binding the head moderately and nourish the patient with all dry food that is low in moisture.
Until the organ strengthens and heals. Another method for the incision is to look where the bone of the swelling and the accumulation of water appear, as it may be more towards the back of the head or the front, or on the right or left.
You aim the incision where the swelling and accumulation of water have appeared, then make the incision as much as you can manage, but be careful not to cut an artery, causing bleeding from which the patient could die along with the discharge of moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cut the two arteries behind the ears, which are known as the carotids.
If one is affected by acute ailments in the eyes or chest, and this persists without improvement from medicinal treatments, then the most effective remedy is to cut these two arteries.
If you intend to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a coarse cloth so that the artery becomes visible. Then, secure the patient’s neck with their garment. Look for where the pulse beats; their position is slightly below behind the ears, and they are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then, mark them with ink and cut them with a sharp scalpel down to the bone. This should be done at the level of the head. If you prefer, insert the scalpel beneath the artery and pull it upwards while cutting. The length of the cut should be about two fingers wide.
If the pulse is cut, blood will gush out in a pulsating manner, spurting forward continuously. If the artery is not clearly visible, you should estimate about three finger-widths from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise down to the bone. What should be done is...
You should let about six ounces of blood as a moderate measure, though you may allow more or less depending on what appears to you regarding the strength of the patient and the fullness of their arteries. Then, examine the wound; if there is any membrane remaining attached to the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, then bind the wound with a wick from linen cloth, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing the arteries in the cheeks.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks due to sharp moistures and heat in the cheek muscles, or severe chronic headache and the like, and has been treated with various medical treatments without success, we have tried cauterizing or sealing the arteries in the cheeks as described.
The method of cauterization involves shaving the hair from the cheeks of the patient and locating the visible artery in the cheek, as it can be identified by its pulse. It is rarely hidden except in some individuals.
Or, in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten their neck with a fold of their garment, then rub the area with a cloth or apply a hot water compress until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel, which has this shape. Then, gently cut the skin with it until you reach the artery, then insert a forceps and pull it upwards until you extract it from the skin and free it from the surrounding tissues on all sides. If the artery is thin, you can twist it with the tip of the forceps. Then, cut a part of it according to how far its ends spread and contract without causing bleeding. If it is not amputated or severed, the blood will not flow properly. Then, let about six to three ounces of blood flow out.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a doubled strong thread, and let the thread be either of abriresm or of the tendons of the lute so that corruption does not quickly affect it before the wound heals, causing bleeding.
Then cut what remains between the ties, do this at that time or another later. If you wish to cauterize it close to the bone with a surgical cautery until its ends fall off, it will serve the same purpose or even better as we said.
Except if the patient has a fever or an overheated temperament, for cauterization is something that aids in consuming moistures and thus is more beneficial. After excising the arteries, the area should be stuffed with old cotton, and tightly bandaged. After removal of the bandage, it should be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth and with bandaging until it heals, if God wills.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your work, immediately cut it either by cauterization or fill the area with pitch and hold tightly until the blood stops. If you do not have any of these, place your finger until the blood clots.
Cool the area with very cold water until the intensity subsides and bind it as necessary. What is lighter and easier than excising the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two points with ink.
Heat it extremely hot until it reaches the bone and cut the vein in two places so that there is distance between its ends, for they will not unite with this cauterization at all. This is the image of the cautery.
The two knives should be sharp like small points, but they are much less sharp than the knife because if they were as sharp as the knife, cold would reach them quickly and the flesh would not cut easily. If there is some thickness in them, they retain the heat of the fire.
Cut the flesh quickly, and this method is better than all others, being lighter and easier. The space between the two knives should be about the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of persistent hot tearing to the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flowing is from the veins on the outer part of the skull, and if no benefit has been gained from any medical treatment with drugs, and you see that the patient's face has reddened. He feels a crawling sensation on his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weak and moist, with his eyelids eroded and swollen due to the sharpness of the tears. Those who first treat this condition resort to this method, which they call treatment by sword, wherein you order the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, make three parallel incisions on the forehead along its length. Each incision should be about two fingers long. The first incision should be parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second slightly away from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
Be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jawbones. The distance between each incision should be about three fingers apart. Have pieces of prepared sponge or many dry cloths with you to absorb the blood.
Then insert the sharp two-edged lancet which looks like this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and peel off all the skin between the two incisions along with the fascia on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, then remove this lancet. And also insert another tool into the first incision, which is called a single-edged scalpel sharp on one side and smooth and not sharp on the other side, and this is its image:.
And make its sharp side face upward toward the flesh attached to the skin and its smooth side face the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut with it all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the outer skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the areas to remove clotted blood, then in each incision place a wick of cotton wool and put on it a dressing moistened with a decoction and oil or vinegar and oil.
To prevent a hot swelling, on the third day, loosen the bandage and use frequent washing with lukewarm water. Then treat it with a poultice of balsamicon with rose oil and other treatments used for wounds until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When there occurs in someone a constant, sharp, hot discharge that causes the eyes to appear sunken and small, with weakened vision, and the eyelids become ulcerated and the eyelashes fall out. There will be a sharp, painful headache and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and secretions only come from many deep veins. The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
There will be a sharp, painful headache and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and secretions only come from many deep veins. The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly with the incision starting from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should reach the bone. Keep your hand away from the chewing muscles that move during mastication. Once the bone is exposed, soak up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a flax thread, then secure it from above with sutures. Soak the sutures in a potion and oil to prevent a hot swelling. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of calafonia, preparing an ointment from them, and using it to promote flesh growth in such injuries.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metal object or something like stone such as iron and glass, or a plant seed like chickpeas and pits, or a liquid such as water, vinegar, and the like.
And if it is a foreign object, when a stone or something like a stone that does not grow in the ear falls into it, face the sun with the ear. If you see the stone, drip some violet or turpentine oil onto it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with pepper. Plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a band of cloth or wool around the ear and stretching the ear upwards, as this treatment often causes it to come out. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, which is illustrated here.
If it comes out with the probe, and if not, try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved blind hook. If it does not come out that way, then make a tube of copper and insert one end of the tube into the ear hole well.
Plug what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil so that there is no path for air other than the tube, then draw it out strongly with your breath; this often causes it to come out. If it does not come out by the methods described and if not, take a little of the incense of the Anbat or the bird-luring incense, and place it at the end of the probe.
After wrapping it with tight cotton, then gently insert it into the ear hole after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out by all the methods described, proceed to make an incision before a hot swelling or convulsion occurs.
The method of the incision is to bleed the patient first in a moderate amount according to their strength. Then sit the patient in front of you, turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear where it is lower near the cartilage.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it using whatever instruments you can. Then immediately suture the incision quickly and treat it until it heals.
And if what has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, try to remove it as mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a thin, delicate lancet shaped like this.
Attempt to cut that type of seed fallen in the ear with it. You should only do this if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the steam in the ear until it becomes small pieces, then remove them with a blind hook or a delicate scoop or by suction.
As mentioned, it is easy to remove them. As for water in the ear, the patient should first try sneezing with cinder while filling his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear that contains the water, if it comes out that way.
Otherwise, take many thin and smooth stones along the fingers, warm them slightly by fire, then have the patient insert one of them into his ear hole, lie down on one side, and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He should continue doing this with each stone until all the water comes out. Alternatively, take a piece of papyrus or feather, insert one end into the ear, and heat the other end until most of it burns, then repeat with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out, or draw it out with a tube as previously described for stones. As for removing animals that have entered the ear, if it is small-bodied like a flea or similar, treat it as mentioned in the section on incisions.
If his body is large and apparent to the sense, try to remove it with pitch and curettes, and its removal is easier than all that pierces the ear. As for the extraction of the worm generated in the ear if you treat it with what we mentioned in the section on fumigations and your treatment does not succeed.
You should look into the ear in the sunlight, then if any part of the worm appears to you, remove it with pitch or with delicate curettes. If nothing appears to you from it, take this tube, which is depicted here.
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suction strongly with it. Do this several times until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you in the case of the stone.
If they do not come out with the methods mentioned, use the fumigations that I have described, which the ancients have tested for killing worms. You will find that in the section on fumigations. The application of ointments and medicines to the ear is done with this instrument, and here is its depiction:
As you see, it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you prefer, the pipe inside the tube can be made of sturdy brass. If you wish, take a wire and wrap cotton tightly around one end.
Then pour the ointment or juice or any of these medicines into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the wire with the cotton from the top and squeeze it moderately with your hand so that the ointment is forced into the auditory canal and is felt.
Enter it to the patient, and let what is poured into the ear be warmed slightly by fire, and beware that the thing which drips in is not extremely cold or extremely hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of obstructive ear condition.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unperforated.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or abnormal growth of flesh. This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal and not visible to the eye.
It may also protrude slightly outside the ear canal and be visible to the eye. What occurs deep in the ear and is not visible is generally more difficult to treat, while what is visible should have the patient's ear exposed to the sun. Examine it, and if you see the obstruction clearly, open it with a delicate surgical instrument, which would look like this:.
Its tip should be slightly broad and limited at the end, with the rest of the instrument smooth on both sides so as not to harm the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it gently with a delicate forceps and cut it carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe, warm it slightly over fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient experiences hearing heaviness, gently remove it.
And be careful not to injure the earlobe, then place in the ear a wick according to the size of the hole, which has been coated with Egyptian ointment. Do this for days until you are certain that the wound will heal or take a wick and soak it in water and sprinkle ground zاج on it.
And use it, but if you see the ear has swollen with a hot swelling, it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another that has been coated in kerotie which is made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides, then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear and use the rest of the treatment that stops bleeding as mentioned in the chapter on bloodletting.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry, and it is necessary to grasp them with tweezers or a forceps and cut them off at the root with a scalpel, then apply powdered zاج to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle lens-shaped cautery. Cauterization after cutting is better. They often return if any of the roots remain, but if you cauterize with fire, it burns those roots and they do not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of transient cold in the eyelids.
Often there appears in the eyelids something that resembles frost in its severity and hardness.
And therefore it is called frost, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment for this is to examine it; if the frost protrudes on the outer part of the eyelid and can be moved in all directions, then its condition is easy, so make a horizontal incision across it.
Then peel it gently from all sides until it is removed, then hang it with the forceps and cut it. If you cannot cut it without penetrating the eyelid, that will not harm the patient at all. If it is small, then there is no harm, as the ointment will heal and bind it. If the frost leans towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid and hold the frost with forceps without needing to make an incision, and cut it from all sides.
If you penetrate the eyelid with the cut, it will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the frost with salt water and treat the area with healing remedies until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the boil that appears in the eyelid.
The boil is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it most often affects children. It weighs down their eyes, causes them to have night sweats, and they are unable to look at sunlight. In order that the tears flow to them.
And therefore, you often see them leaning on their faces and they sleep this way, with their eyelids under the eyebrows moist and swollen, and the boil is clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the boil between them. The procedure is for the patient to place his head in your lap.
Then take a piece of linen, prepare it as a wick, and make a circle from that wick according to the size needed to surround the boil on all sides. Then place it over it and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the boil in the center of the circle.
Then, with the scalpel, make a transverse incision in the center of that moisture. The incision should not be larger than that made in a vein. As for the depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the boil.
In most cases, the boil will protrude from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat. You should pull it out with a cloth wrapped between your fingers to the outside while rotating your hand right and left until it is completely removed. Be careful not to enlarge the incision so that the scalpel does not reach the eye and harm it.
If the boil does not appear at the beginning of the incision, you should slightly enlarge the incision gently until the boil protrudes. Then pull it out as we said. Then soak a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and secure it with a bandage. Some people crush salt for this purpose.
He places it in the depth of the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture, then continues treating it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing bandages until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On Types of Eyeshadow.
If excess eyelashes grow outside the natural course beneath the normal lashes and persist, they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, whiteness, and thickening until this becomes a reason for the eye's failure.
Eyeshadow application can be done in four ways: either by branding with fire, or by using a sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the cautery section, or by cutting and stitching, or by using reed, which I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, otherwise insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, close to the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
You turn it with a retractor, then incise inside the eyelid without touching the excess hair from the larger meibomian gland to the smaller one. Then thread the suture and place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
It resembles the shape of an oak leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to lift the eyelid, as this can vary among people. Some may need a suitable amount of the eyelid to be cut depending on how relaxed the eyelid is.
Some may require the cutting of less, all of this according to the relaxation of the eyelid, then cut with the scalpel on the two lines that were taught, and start from the larger opening to the smaller one, and let each incision be near the natural hair by the thickness of a needle.
Then insert the forceps into one of the corners of the skin, then peel it all off, then approximate the two lips with a needle and fine wool thread, wipe away the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substances.
If you wish to do so, and otherwise, it does not matter. Then leave the sutures and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. If you prefer, leave the wound unsutured and treat it with things that dry and constrict, as the eyelid rises when the wound is sealed and united, and suturing is better.
This method of compressing has been mentioned by the earlier authorities, except that it involves some burden on the patient; however, it is a good technique and there is no danger in it. Another method of compressing is to shape something over the eyelid like the shape of a leaf of ivy as we have described. Then raise the eyelid with three forceps, which can be spaced apart or grouped in this manner:.
Then cut what remains of the eyelid with a small scissors in this manner, cutting moderately:.
If you are unable to hold the forceps and they do not stabilize for you, take a needle with thread and insert it in the center of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger opening and a third thread near the smaller opening. Gather the threads between your fingers moderately, then lift them with your hand in a balanced manner.
Lift the skin that was incised entirely as we described, then approximate the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling appears when you cut or make an incision, alleviate that swelling with cerote or similar soothing ointments.
Sometimes the lower eyelid may also evert, and in such cases, you should use what we have mentioned of cutting, suturing, and cauterization with fire or sharp medicines. Cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, though cutting and suturing can also be skillfully performed.
Cauterization with reed is done in this manner: you turn the eyelid inside out and make an incision on the inside as described. Then, you make two thin reeds or wooden pieces whose length matches that of the eyelid and their width is less than the width of a scalpel.
You notch the ends of both pieces on each side where the threads will hold. Then, gently bring them together over what remains of the eyelid and tightly secure the two reeds on both sides. Leave it for several days; the tightened skin will die, turn black, and腐烂。
Until it falls off by itself. If it takes longer, use a punch to notch it, then treat it until it heals. Once it has healed, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes will not turn inward towards the eye. This is how the two reeds are made, fashioned in this shape and size, with the same length and width:
Chapter Twelve.
On Removing Inward-Growing Eyelashes with a Needle.
If there is a hair or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and insert into it a thread of smooth fine silk, then gather its ends and tie them in a very gentle knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another finer thread under the loop and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient place his head in your lap, and let it be near sunlight so that the work can be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the loop into the root of the extra hair.
Pierce through the eyelid quickly until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread up to the loop and insert that hair into the loop, whether it is one, two, or three hairs, not more.
Then pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the loop from the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the loop and the hair does not come out with it, pull the loop downward with the thread that you attached to it until the loop comes out of the upper hole.
Then put the hair back in and pull it out. Do this until it rises to the upper hole and you can see it clearly. At that point, remove the loop and the thread, tighten the eye, and leave it tightened for two or three days until the hair stays in place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer over time, and then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the treatment of styes that occur on the upper eyelid.
Which is affected by this sty is called arnibia, and this sty is either natural or incidental. The incidental one results from the healing of a wound, split, burn, or the like.
The procedure is to cut open that healed area, separate its edges, and place a wick of linen between them, then bind it until it heals. It is not advisable to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
If you do that, the sty will return worse than before. Instead, use things that relax, such as fenugreek and turpeth with water in which alum and linseed have been boiled, and apply a diachylon ointment dissolved with some fat to the wick and treat it thus.
The principle of treating it is to strive by every means that it does not heal in the same condition as before. The procedure we mentioned is part of correcting the sty, not restoring the area completely to its original state.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of styes that occur on the lower eyelid.
This sty that occurs on the lower part is truly called a sty, and it can be natural or incidental. The incidental one results from a wound, split, burn, or the like.
The method for treating it is to take a needle with a doubled thread, pierce the flesh with it, and pass it from one side to the other until the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then pull the flesh upward with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper form, otherwise take a needle and place it at the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it, and make two cuts on the inner side of the eyelid so that the ends of the two cuts are from the corners of the cut you made until they meet, thus forming a corner. Until when they come together, its form becomes similar to this shape:.
It is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is lower than what adjoins the eyelid, then join the separated parts with two stitches sewn with wool thread, then treat it with the relaxing and bandaging medications we mentioned until it heals.
If the eyelid has been affected by a cut, stitching, or cauterization, it should be incised with a slight cut also without hair of the eyelashes as previously mentioned, then separate the two edges with bandaging as we described.
In summary, the treatment of ptosis, whether from above or below, should be carried out according to what appears suitable for the form of the ptosis, as it may vary greatly in appearance, and the skilled practitioner will devise a method by whichever means is possible for him.

Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may affect many people by the upper eyelid adhering to the white of the eye, preventing it from moving easily and hindering its natural functions; therefore, it is necessary to insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or extend it with a forceps, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel that is not as sharp as other scalpels but slightly blunt so as not to alarm the patient during the procedure, which could cause the scalpel to slip and harm the eye with its sharpness. Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed. Then, pour salty water into the eye to wash it, or drip a solution of zingiber officinale dissolved in water into it. Finally, separate the eyelid from the eye with a cotton swab.
Place wool soaked in egg white over the eye, and after the third day, use pounded ginger until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the removal of the ingrown nail and excess flesh around the nails.
The ingrown nail can be of two types: either it is neural, resembling a hard, thin membrane, or it is non-neural, resembling a white, solid moisture that breaks apart when touched by iron or pulled with forceps.
No forceps were used in it, and both types begin from the larger aperture until they are inserted little by little until they cover the pupil and prevent light and eye movement. The method of cutting them is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the cataract with slightly curved forceps and pull it upwards. Then take a needle, insert a hair from horse or cattle hair and a strong thread into it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce it into the center of the cataract.
Then you thread it with the needle and tie the cataract with the thread, pull it upwards, and use the hair to peel off the side of the cataract that is next to the iris as if you are spreading it out with the hair completely, then cut the remaining part at the base of the aperture with a delicate scalpel or a small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the aperture.
To prevent the constant flowing of tears from cutting it, the cataract can be pulled with forceps alone or with a thread as we said, then peel it off with a smooth-edged scalpel that looks like this:.
Be careful that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane, causing a tear and damaging the iris. After cutting, put some powdered salt or zingiber officinale in the eye and leave it for another day, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the cataract is not adherent and you cannot insert a needle into it or secure forceps to it, this type of cataract is often treated with medication as described in the classification section. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate, smooth, and weak-edged scalpel in this form:. Shave the nail from above gently with it. If you see that it becomes loose and crumbles due to the shaving, then instill a drop from your vial into the eye of Zanjari eyewash or red eyewash or powdered salt, and bind the eye for another day.
Then repeat the procedure on it until it is all gone, unless you encounter a hot swelling in the eye; in that case, leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals. Then repeat the shaving procedure on it until it heals. As for the flesh growth in the meibomian gland...
If the growth severely irritates the eye, attach that growth to a hook and cut some of it, but do not overcut lest it cause tearing. Then instill into the meibomian glands red eyewash or Zanjari eyewash until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the removal of warding and any excess flesh that grows in the eye.
Red flesh may grow in the eyes of some people, accumulating until it covers the pupil or approaches it or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outward. It resembles the rose of jelenar.
The treatment involves having the patient place their head in your lap, then open their eyes, and remove all of that red flesh using tweezers suitable for this purpose.
Or hold it with a forceps or pincers, then cut the flesh bit by bit until all of it is removed by cutting, and be careful of the eye so as not to harm it while working. Your cutting should be done either with the scalpel we described for cutting nails or with a small scissors. Like those with which you pick the threads, as its image will come in the next chapter.
Once the cutting is complete and all the inflammation has gone, fill the eye with ground salt or drip in red saffron or similar caustic medicines. Apply cotton coated with egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent hot swelling.
If any inflammation remains and the blood overpowers you and you fear hot swelling, then leave the eye alone and treat it with what calms the swelling. Then return to it gradually until it heals. Do the same for any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this kind.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye, whether naturally or due to an illness, with iron instruments. Do not engage in any of these procedures until you are certain that the organ can withstand such treatment.
Chapter Eighteen.
On removing threads from the eye.
Threads are red veins that form over the eye, hindering vision and weakening the eye over time. You should first examine if the eye containing these threads is strong and free of any other illness besides the threads.
Then, perform the leeching; that is to say, instruct the patient to place his head in your lap. Then attach those veins with one or two hooks according to your skill, and the hooks should be gently curved in this manner:.
or they could be double hooks in one body as follows:.
Then, carefully cut those veins with a delicate scissors and wipe the blood from time to time until you see that the veins have departed and the blood has coagulated. Be careful not to harm the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done in the morning facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work so as not to cut any other veins. When you are finished, drip green or red sheyaf into the eye to let its sharpness consume what remains of the spittle. If you cannot leech it all at that time, bandage the eye with something that soothes its pain.
Leave it for days until its pain subsides and you are safe from the hot swelling, then repeat the procedure on her in exactly the same manner until it heals. This is the image of the scissors to use for this.
Leave it [with the patient's head in the student's lap, another one drying the tears and ensuring clear vision at the front and back of the eye while you] begin the procedure by opening the eye with fixed or triangular speculum that a student holds.
Be precise, with the patient's head in the student's lap, another one drying the tears and ensuring clear vision at the front and back of the eye, and you may begin the leeching from the top of the eye or from the bottom, but it is preferable to dry two hooks on the upper side, one at the end of the crown and the other at the beginning.
Hold it between the middle finger and the index finger, then hang weights underneath opposite them. Then hold it between the little finger and the ring finger and lift your hand lightly until you see all of the vein raised. Cut from the smaller side if you want with scissors or with a delicate knife.
Insert the needle with wax and stretch the entire vein until it rises on the flesh, and do not cut the dried part, for tears and blood will make it easy (soft) for you.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the crown from above and below until nothing protrudes except in the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea, peel it off with wax, which is better, though it may be difficult for some people.
And when you cut it, the area will swell for days, and the pain and the heat of the swelling will subside, then repeat the procedure on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On restoring the septum to the nose.
Physicians call the septum a polypus, and if you treat it with cauterization or with sharp, burning medicine as previously described and it does not heal, there is no remedy except to incise the swelling when it has ripened and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed, and if the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument that looks like this:
It is called the rough head, made from Indian iron, with its head rounded like a button, engraved with fine engravings similar to those of a file or rasp. Place it on the site of corruption in the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly pressing your hand until you know that the corruption has been scraped off.
And do this several times, then treat the area with drying and contracting medicines. If the area seals and flesh grows and the discharge stops, and if it remains for forty days without recurrence or inflammation and no incident occurs in it.
Know that he is healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the polyp into the nasal opening in this manner: reveal the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine, once the bone is revealed, take it out in this form:
The tip of the iron should be triangular and its end conical, drawn to the tip as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, which is near the nostril, and keep your hand slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the instrument until the bone is penetrated and the patient feels it.
If one finds air coming out when he holds his hand over the nose, then treat the area with contracting and drying medicines as I have informed you. If flesh grows and the place hardens, then the material that was protruding outward will return into the nose, which will be less severe for the patient, if God wills.
The Twentieth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Eye Growths.
If the entire eye has a growth and it does not cause any harm or deficiency to the sight, it is appropriate to ease the patient, then place them in a basin. Then put a leech on the back without restriction and let it suck gently.
Then place on the eye a poultice made from acacia, frankincense, gum, and cinnamon. Then bind the eye over the dressing with many bandages. The method of the bandages is to take soft linen in abundance, fold each piece into four folds to make them thick.
Then place them one on top of the other according to what the eye requires, then bind them tightly with a strong cloth. Repeat the cupping by suction without restriction, then remove the cup and leave the eye bound for a day and night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened well. Then remove the bandage after a day and night as we said. If you see that the eye has recovered, otherwise repeat the dressing, padding, binding, and cupping until it recovers.
The Twenty-First Chapter.
On Cutting the Umbilical Cord.
If a hernia appears in the umbilical layer, then protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids resembling an umbilicus, and this spoils the appearance of the person, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It involves inserting a needle into the base of the umbilicus from bottom to top, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread on the side of the orifice and passing it through, leaving the first needle as is.
Then cut the place where the doubled thread bends, tie it around part of the umbilicus near the needle and tighten it well. Then remove the needle and apply wool soaked in egg white to the eye, leave it until the threads fall off with the umbilicus, then treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, you may bind the umbilicus from all sides with a circle of cloth until the eye becomes more protruding. Then, use a thin scalpel that reaches the depth of the eye to let the egg-like moisture flow out, causing the eye to drop and wither in place, then bind it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of scabies.
This ailment which is called scabies is a discharge that gathers in the eye, resembling flowing water but there is none. The method of treatment is to seat the patient on a chair upright, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
Move it until you see the discharge move downward with your eye, then it stabilizes and does not move, and the light is released, and the patient sees things as he used to see them. If it does not go down, we know that it is water. If the discharge does not descend as we mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient in front of you, then take a thin scalpel and make an incision slightly above the cornea at the point where the conjunctiva meets the cornea in the limbus until the material is released.
Once it is released, drip warm water mixed with honey or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled into the eye. Then continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which is suitable for draining with clear and explanatory words, so take that information as accurate. At this point, you should seat the patient in front of you, cross-legged, facing a light source near the sun.
His healthy eye is tied and pulled tightly, then you raise his eyelid with your left hand if the affected eye is the left one or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the probe near the cornea with a thickness of a morat in the white part of the eye from the side of the smaller aperture, then push the probe forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates into the white part of the eye and you feel that the probe has reached an empty space.
The depth to which the probe should go should be equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the iris, for through the clarity of the cornea, you will see the copper clearly in the eye. Then direct the probe upwards to the place where the fluid is.
Then press it down repeatedly; if the fluid descends immediately, the patient will see what his sight has been opened to right away, with the probe still in his eye. Then let it rest for a little; if the fluid rises again, lower it once more without removing the probe.
When it stabilizes and does not rise, gently remove the probe while slightly turning it with your hand little by little. Then dissolve some pure Andranian salt in water and wash the eye internally with it. Afterwards, apply moist cotton or wool externally over the entire eye.
With rose oil and egg white, you bind it along with the healthy eye. As for us and our time, we place ground cumin with egg white on it. If the probe does not enter the eye due to its hardness, because there are those whose eyes are extremely hard. It should take the scalpel which is called the brid which has this form:.
Pierce only the area of the wound with it and do not deepen the piercing, but rather create a subtle opening for the probe, then insert the probe as mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare a secure bed for the patient to lie on his back in a dark room.
He should abstain from all movements and coughing, and his food should be what alleviates his condition. He should not move his head to the right or left at all, and the bandage should remain in place until the third day. Then, remove it in that dark room and test his vision.
Show him things and then reapply the bandage until the seventh day. You should not do this during treatment or after cauterization at your time, but you should avoid it before because water rises quickly with intense viewing.
If a hot swelling occurs, you should remove the bandage before the seventh day and treat it with what calms the swelling until it subsides. Once it has subsided, then release his vision and place a veil over his face through which he can gradually train his sight while in that dark room for several days.

Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of flesh grown in the nose.
Fleshy growths of various kinds may appear in the nose, some resembling many-legged scorpions, others being hard and cancerous with a dark color, and some being soft and not dark in color.
As for the soft growths that are not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit before you facing the sun, and you should open his nostril, place the forceps on those growths, then pull them out, and cut what has been reached with a sharp, delicate instrument from one side.
To ensure that all the flesh has been removed, if any part remains that you cannot cut, scrape it off with a delicate instrument gently until nothing remains. If bleeding overwhelms you or inflammation occurs suddenly.
Treat it as appropriate, or if it is a malignant swelling, immediately cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is removed. Then, after cutting, apply vinegar and water or a liquid to the nose. If the nose opens and moisture flows into the throat.
Know that it has healed if the moisture flows as it should. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that there is growing flesh inside on top of the loose bones which the instrument could not reach to cut. In this case, you should take a linen thread that is somewhat thick.
And make many knots in it, and place between each knot the length of a finger or less. The patient then inserts one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or as much as he can after making it like a button and pulling its thread until it reaches the nasal cavity.
And it comes out at the throat. This action is often done by children in school, and it is easy for anyone who wants to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, one end that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the thread with knots to cut the flesh. Do this until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread, and in the nose, after wiping the blood, place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then place a lead tube in the nose for several days until it heals. If drying treatment is needed, use that method.
And this is an image of the device used to drip oils and medicines into the nose:.

Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing at the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow at the tip of the nose, increasing in size over time until their appearance becomes ugly.
Therefore, it should be cut off at the first sign of its appearance and completely removed. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that can replace cauterization to the area. If it is not cut off until it grows large, examine it; if it is hard, solid, uniform in color, and has little sensation.
Do not treat it with iron for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen that cutting this tumor has led to great harm for the patient. If the tumor feels soft upon touch and is not uniform in color, and you find that it can be completely cut out. Purge the patient, and cut it without hesitation. Treat the area with substances that dry and constrict until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If the connection is severed due to a wound or the like, know that if there is a separation in any of these cartilages, it seldom responds well to treatment except in some people.
It is advisable that when someone presents with such an issue, if the wound is fresh and bloody, you should bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the separation has caused the edges to part and each side has become healthy, it is best to peel back each side to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then the lips are sewn together and tightened, and powdered sulfur and frankincense are sprinkled on it. A plaster of unguent or another type of healing ointment is placed over it. It is left tightened for two or three days, then it is released.
And the medicine is changed until the threads fall off by themselves. Then it is treated with ointment until it heals. The method of sewing is to join what has been separated, either with needles as described in stitching the abdomen or with thread as previously explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing knots that appear in the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling morsels of corns and others smaller or larger. It is advisable to turn the lip, incise each knot, and grasp it with forceps.
Then cut it from all sides. After cutting, fill the area with powdered zaj and let it stop the bleeding. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On removing excess flesh from the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called aphtosa. It is advisable to grasp it with forceps or hold it with a probe and cut it at its base. Allow the blood to flow out, then apply powdered zaj to the area.
Or one of the astringent and drying suppuratives, then if that flesh returns after treatment, it often returns; cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On scraping teeth with iron.
Rough and ugly crusts may form on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums. These crusts may blacken, yellow, or green until they cause damage to the gums and make the teeth look unpleasant.
It is advisable that the patient sit before you with his head in your lap, and you should scrape the molar and tooth where the crusts and sand-like substances appear until nothing remains. Do the same for the black, green, yellow, and other discolorations until it is clean.
If what is inside disappears with the first scraping, otherwise repeat the scraping on another day, a second time, and a third time until you achieve your goal. Know that the molar requires various types of scrapers with different shapes according to what is suitable for your work.
For the scraper used to scrape the molar from the inside is different from the one used for the outside, and the one used between molars has another form. Here are several forms of scrapers that you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On extracting teeth.
It is advisable to treat the molar for pain with every means possible and to hesitate before extracting it, for there is no replacement if it is extracted, as it is a noble essence. Even if extraction becomes necessary, it should be done cautiously.
It is necessary if the patient decides to extract it that you ensure until you find the aching tooth sound. For often the pain deceives the patient, and he thinks it is in the healthy tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted; we have seen barbers do this many times.
When you have identified the aching tooth with certainty, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong enough surgical instrument until you separate the gum from all sides, then move it with your fingers or with delicate forceps gradually little by little until you loosen it.
Then take hold of it firmly with the larger forceps, and have the patient's head between your knees so that he is secured and does not move. Then pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, insert it under the tooth from all sides gently, and rock it as you did before.
If the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill that hole with a piece of cloth and secure it well with a thin strip of wax so that it does not crumble when you pull on it with the forceps. You should also carefully incise around the gum from all sides thoroughly.
Be cautious with your effort so as not to break the tooth, leaving part of it behind, which would cause the patient greater harm than his original pain. Beware of doing what ignorant barbers do in their hastiness and eagerness to extract it without using the methods we have described.
For often great calamities occur to people, the least of which is that a tooth may break and some or all of its roots remain. Or it may be extracted along with some of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times. Then, after its extraction, he rinses his mouth with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, which often happens, then grind some zadj and pack the area with it.
Otherwise, use it if the zadj does not help you. The image of the delicate forceps that are used to move the tooth first should have long ends, a short handle, and be thick so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth with them. This is the image of the large forceps:
It should be as you see, with thick handles so that when you grasp them they do not yield or bend. The ends should be short and made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel, with teeth at the tips that interlock to grip firmly and tightly.
The ends may also be made like the shape of a file, which also makes them strong and precise in grip.
