Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the Extraction of Teeth that Have Grown in the Wrong Position.
If the teeth grow outside their natural path, they spoil the appearance, especially if this happens in women and slaves. It is advisable to examine; if a tooth has grown behind another tooth.
If it cannot be extracted or pushed back, then extract it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, which is depicted as follows:.
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron. Your cutting should be done over many days due to the hardness of the tooth, so as not to disturb the other teeth. If it protrudes firmly due to its coldness, cool it with a file that looks like this:.
It should be entirely made of Indian steel, with a very fine edge like that used for making needles. Cool the tooth gradually over many days with gentle application so as not to disturb the tooth and cause it to fall out. Finally, smooth it and remove it with some chisels.
If a tooth has broken and is bothering the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed down until the roughness of the break is smoothed out and does not harm the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On securing loose teeth with silver threads or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything edible for fear they will fall out, and treating them with constricting medicines has not been effective.
The solution is to secure them with a gold or silver thread, and gold is better because silver can corrode and rot after a few days, while gold remains in its state forever without such issues. The thread should be of moderate thickness to fit well between the teeth.
The method of securing is to take the thread and insert its bend between two healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread among the loose teeth, whether one or more, until the weaving reaches another healthy tooth on the other side.
Then, you return the weaving to the side where you started and pull gently and wisely so that it does not move at all. Make sure your thread is tight at the roots of the teeth so it does not come loose. Then, cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them with wax, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not irritated, then leave it tightened in this way permanently. If it loosens or breaks, secure it again with another thread, and thus it will serve its purpose for a long time. This is the image of the teeth and the method of securing two loose teeth between two healthy ones as you see.
And it may happen that one or two molars return to their place and fit in as we have described and remain, and this is done by a skilled craftsman. And he may carve bone from the bones of an ox and make it in the shape of a molar and put it in the place where the molar has gone. And it is fixed as we have said, so it remains and one enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue and hinders speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may either be natural, with which a person is born, or it may be accidental from a wound that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, placing their head in your lap, lifting the tongue, then cutting the ligament transversely until the tongue is freed from its restraint. If there is some stiffness or knotting and this is due to a healed wound, make an incision with forceps and cut it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is resolved.
Be cautious that the incision is not too deep in the flesh so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding. After the cutting, rinse with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a cotton wick under the tongue for the patient to keep each night.
To prevent it from healing again, if bleeding occurs, apply powdered zajah to the area. If the bleeding persists, cauterize the area with a suitable lens-shaped cautery tool. Then treat it with all treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On Extracting the Frog That Develops Under the Tongue.
A swelling that resembles a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing it from its natural function, and it might grow until it fills the mouth. The procedure for this is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and examine the swelling.
If you see it as dark or black and hard, and the patient feels nothing of it, do not treat it for it is cancer. If it tends towards white and has moisture, then pierce it with a lancet and cut it open with a delicate knife and remove it from all sides.
If the blood overpowers you while working, apply powdered zajah until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until you remove it completely. Then rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all the appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the Treatment of Tonsil Swelling.
And what grows in the throat from other swellings may present glands inside the throat that resemble those appearing externally, known as tonsils. If treating them with what was mentioned in the section does not heal them.
If the swelling is hard, dark in color, and has little sensation, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in color and its base is thick, also do not treat it with iron for fear of bleeding. Instead, leave it until it ripens, either to be softened or to burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut. The procedure is to look before the operation to see if its hot swelling has completely subsided or decreased somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, with his head in your lap, open his mouth, and take an assistant who will hold his tongue down with an instrument that looks like this:.
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue with it and clearly see the swelling, take a forceps, pierce it into the tonsil, and pull it out as much as possible without bringing any of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument that looks like this: it resembles scissors, but its ends are bent so that the mouth of each one faces the other, both being very sharp. It is made from Indian iron or Damascus steel:.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a lancet that looks like this:.
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. After the cutting, the patient should gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or oak leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then continue to treat the patient until he recovers. Sometimes other swellings may appear in the throat besides the tonsils; these should be cut in the same manner as described for cutting the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that grew inside her throat, extending towards the larynx, barely sensitive, which was almost blocking the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and it had prevented her from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death had she remained another day or two, and the tumor had two branches that extended until they came out through her nostrils. So I acted quickly, inserting a clamp into one of them and pulling it, thus extracting a piece successfully.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril hole, and then did the same for what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pushed her tongue aside, and inserted the clamp into the tumor itself, cutting off a part of it, from which only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, then took some food. We continued to cut parts of that tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor would regenerate what was cut off, so this went on for a long time with both of us.
I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat, which stopped its growth. Then I left that place and did not know what happened to her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On Cutting the Tumor of the Tonsil.
Which is called a cluster if a phlegm descends to the tonsil and swells, and if it is white and rectangular, it is called a pillar. If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a cluster. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment is not effective.
I have seen the hot swelling subside from it and become thin, so it should be cut. What was of it round and collected and had no length, and was blood-colored or dark-colored or black or without feeling, then it should be avoided from being cut.
In it, there is a need to operate on the patient; therefore, if you see it as described, white and long, you should sit the patient facing the sun, press his tongue with the tool that was previously described, then insert the probe into the growth.
And pull it down and cut it with one of the two tools I mentioned for cutting the tonsils. It is best to only cut what has exceeded the natural limit without adding more, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting, use what we described for cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is reluctant to have it cut, you should employ a method to cauterize it without fear or hesitation. The purpose of cauterization is with a sharp remedy, which involves placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then press his tongue with the tool we mentioned, then take some of the sharp water I described in the section on cauterization and mix it with unextinguished lime, making it neither too thick nor too thin. Fill this mixture into the recess of this tool, and this is its shape:
The end of the tool where you place the medicine should have a recess like that of a spoon. Place the tool with the medicine directly on the growth itself while the patient is lying on their side so that saliva flows out of his mouth from inside his throat, so that nothing goes down into his throat and harms him.
Then hold the medicine in your hand and squeeze it on the tooth for about half an hour until you see that it has turned black and the pain of the medicine's bite has subsided, and if you wish, take some cotton, wrap it around the end of a stick, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the stick with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton sticks to the tooth. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired effect of cauterizing the gum. Then leave it, for it will wilt and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After cauterization, wipe around the gum with cotton soaked in fat and dry the area around it from the medicine. Then rinse with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and more reassuring.
Teeth can also be treated with a milder method than cauterization and cutting; they can be treated with fumigation as follows: take frankincense, zaufa, thyme, saffron, sage, chamomile, cypress, and similar herbs. Gather all of them or some of them in a pot and submerge them in vinegar.
Boil the pot, which is sealed with clay from a wise person, and make a hole in the center of the lid. Attach a hollow instrument to this hole, made as follows: it can be crafted from silver or brass.
Insert the end with the funnel into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the tooth through the tube until the gum swells well. Then repeat this several times until it wilts. Do not perform this treatment at the first sign of swelling.
For often it increases the swelling, and it should only be done when her hot swelling subsides. If this tool is not available to you, take a reed and attach a chicken eggshell to one end so that the mouth of the patient does not burn.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is good for treatment with safety.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On the removal of thorns and other objects that pierce the throat.
Often a bone or fish spine, or something similar, pierces the throat. You should extract what is visible to the eye after pressing the tongue with an instrument towards the sun.
So that you may clearly see what is in the throat. For what does not appear and is hidden in the throat, it is advisable to induce vomiting before the food is digested in the stomach, as the piercing object might come out with the vomit, or the patient can swallow a piece of lettuce or the root of chicory.
Or swallow a bite of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, then swallow it. When it reaches the spot of the thorn, pull the string quickly. Do this several times.
For the thorn or bone often sticks to it and comes out. If it does not come out as described, then use a lead instrument of this shape:.
It should be slightly thicker than a needle and have a curved end. The patient should gently insert it into their throat while raising their head upwards, being careful not to touch the larynx to avoid causing coughing, and use it to push out the bone or thorn, or the doctor can insert it with their hand.
And inserting the patient into it is better for his knowledge of the location of the embedded object and to push downwards or pull his hand upwards with the tool, all of that according to what is suitable until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On Removing Thorns Embedded in the Throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the division regarding treatment with medications and it is not effective, then examine the patient's throat in the sunlight after you press his tongue with the tool that was described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a fine, firm tweezers. If you cannot do so, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then introduce an iron rod heated by fire into the tube.
Do this several times and the patient should abstain from water throughout the day. Then, take a vessel filled with cold water, open his mouth over it, and let him rinse with it without swallowing a drop, moving the water around with his hand from time to time.
The leech will fall off when it senses the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and alum using the tool described for fumigating nasal passages. Do this several times, and it will fall off.
The method of fumigation involves taking a pot with heated charcoal, covered with a lid that has a hole in the center. You attach one end of the tool to this hole, then add the fumigating substance. The patient places his mouth at the other end of the tube and seals it so the fumes do not escape until he feels the fumes reach his throat.
For the leech falls from its place, and if it does not fall, then the incense is repeated several times and the patient endures thirst and eats salty food and garlic without drinking water; surely it will come out with this management. And this is a figure of an instrument that attracts the leech from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles forceps as you see, except that it has this bent part which enters the throat and its ends resemble a bird's mouth with the roughness of a file; so when it clamps onto something, it does not leave it.
And another instrument used to press the tongue, it is a wide iron ring as wide as to fit inside the mouth over the tongue. It enters the mouth standing on its side, keeping the mouth open against the will of the patient and he cannot remove it at all until it is taken away.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth in exactly this same measure.
Chapter Forty.
In it, there is discussion about softening swellings and incising them.
Swellings are of many diverse kinds, as will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in softening and incising in two aspects: one is the type of swelling itself and what moistures it contains.
The second aspect is due to the body parts where they occur, because a swelling occurring in the head is different from a swelling occurring in the buttocks, and a swelling occurring in a fleshy area is different from a swelling occurring in a joint; each has its own specific treatment method.
There are swellings that should not be lanced until the pus within them is ripe and complete, and there are those that should be lanced when they are just beginning and have not fully ripened, like the swellings that occur near the joints.
For if a swelling occurs near a joint and persists until the surrounding area becomes putrefied, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, which could result in the disability of that limb, or the swelling may occur near an important organ.
For if you delay lancing it until it ripens, you will harm the important organ, or if it is near the anus, lance it when it is just beginning and not fully ripe. You must lance the swelling that is near the anus when it is just beginning and not fully ripe so that the pus does not penetrate inside the anus and cause an fistula.
And it becomes to a point where it does not heal. You should know the time for lancing swellings that have fully ripened, which is when the pain of the swelling subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and inflammation decrease, and the head of the swelling becomes defined, along with other signs.
You should place the incision at the lowest part of the swelling if possible, so that it is easier for the pus to drain downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the swelling. The incision should be made lengthwise on the body if the swellings are on areas like the hands and legs.
Places where muscles, tendons, nerves, and blood vessels are located, generally in all flat areas without bends. As for those that bend, the incision should follow the contour of that area. If the swelling is in fleshy areas, it is best to avoid lancing it.
Until it fully matures as we have said, for if you cut it before that, the pus will continue to flow from it for a long time and will be much more harmful and dirty, and its lips and depths might become hardened, and some swellings may spread across the body when necessary or according to what the limb requires.
It is appropriate to use one incision for small swellings and a wide incision or many slits according to the size of the large swelling. There may be some swellings where it is necessary to puncture the skin and cut if it has become like a rag.
And it has happened to some extent that it has died, similar to what occurs in many abscesses and hidings. Some are split into a three-cornered slit, and some are cut in the shape of a plant leaf, like the Arabian swelling.
Some require a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and the like. For those that do not have a head, such as flat smooth swellings, it is appropriate to use only a simple incision. If the swelling is large and has gathered for a long period, after you have made an incision, do not hurry to extract all the pus at once.
Rather, extract some of it, then allow the swelling to subside for another day, and then extract some more pus. Do this gradually several times until all of it is extracted.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, as the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden release of pus, and the patient may die without you realizing it. Beware of this matter greatly.
After you have lanced these tumors, it is necessary to clean the wound and examine it. If the tumor's incision is small or if there is only one simple slit, use linen or old cotton for bandaging. If the tumor is large and has many slits from the lancing.
It is necessary to insert a wick into each slit until they all reach each other. If part of the skin has been cut from the tumor or excised, it should be filled with old cotton or the fuzz of linen without moisture and secured until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your procedure, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of linen in them, and apply it to the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding persists, it is necessary to employ the procedures and treatments, including evacuations as described in many places in this book and from the division. If you are in winter and the tumor site has many nerves, it is advisable to soak the dressings with a hot drink and oil.
Place it on the affected area. If you are in summer and the areas are fleshy, it is advisable to apply dressings soaked with cold water and oil or a cold drink and oil. On the third day, as we have said, you should clean the tumor and wash it.
Use similar methods for its treatment until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the details, I have described each tumor and the way to treat it separately in a summarized form.
Chapter Forty-One.
On incising tumors that appear on the scalp.
Tumors of small size appear on the scalp, and they are a type of boil contained in membranes that have sacs like those of a chicken's egg. There are many types; some are fatty, and others contain moisture similar to mud.
Some contain moisture resembling pus and broth, and there are those that are hard and solid. All of these pose no danger in incision and extraction unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to probe them first with the instrument that will be shown after this, called the lancet, to determine their contents. If they contain moisture, make an incision along the length in a simple manner as follows:
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. Once the moisture has drained, peel and cut away the entire sac that contained the moisture, leaving nothing behind, for it often returns if any part remains.
Then, soak a cotton ball in the strong ointment if available, or in saltwater, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, for it will dissolve any remaining sac. Repeat this process by applying the cotton ball with the strong ointment again and a third time if necessary.
Continue until you are certain that no part of the sac remains. At this point, treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains fatty matter, incise it in this manner:.
Make a firm incision and place the retractors at the lips of the wound and peel it from all sides and strive in extracting the peritoneum which contains it, if an artery impedes you then do what was described to you after and likewise do with the swelling if it is hardened from the cut.
And the treatment is as mentioned, and incising the hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people have no moisture at all. This is because I incised a swelling in.
The head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like a hard, rough, white stone that could not be broken, and if someone threw it, it would injure them. None of the other swellings occurring in the head were like this, such as the swellings that appear on the heads of children.
And at the base of the ears, make a slight incision all over them and turn their lining downwards so that the discharge easily flows down. Then treat it with appropriate treatment.
The Forty-Second Chapter.
On Incising Swellings in the Neck: These swellings often appear in the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins, and may occur throughout the body. Those that appear in the neck can be singular or multiple, and some may develop from others.
Each of these swellings is inside its own peritoneum, similar to tumors and head swellings as described. There are many types of these swellings; some are hardened, some contain moisture, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
I have not seen any of them in good condition to touch, and its surface is close to the color of the skin; it moves in all directions and is not attached to the neck nerve, nor to the thyroid, nor to an artery, and it is not sunken. It should be cut with a slight incision from top to bottom of the body in this form, from line ~G~ to line ~B~:.
And peel it from all sides and stretch the edges of the skin with forceps or with two forceps or with many forceps if you need to, as we said in the case of head tumors. Extract it little by little, and make sure not to cut a vein or a nerve. The scalpel should not be too sharp.
So that your hand does not overcut or disturb the patient, causing you to cut what does not need to be cut. If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders you from working, put in the wound powdered zaj or some of the styptics that stop the blood.
And suture the wound and leave it until the severity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to show signs of putrefaction, for the blood will stop then. Return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if any more small growths remain, cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the growth, you should not cut the growth from its root but should tie it with a double tight thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm. Then fill the wound with old cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment and treat it.
And if the entire sow's [teat] is cut off, it is necessary to bring together the lips of the wound and suture it immediately after ensuring that no fragment remains. If you find that a fragment of the sow's bone remains.
It is advisable to aim for cutting at its root and base, and use suturing as we have mentioned. For those swine that contain moisture, lightly bandage it where you see the signs of its maturation, and make the bandage towards the lower side as we said.
Then after the bandage, use winding with Egyptian ointment or similar to consume any remaining corruption until you see the wound is clean, then treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On incising the throat for a swelling that occurs inside the neck.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it, and this is the order of their words. As for those with goiter, they should avoid incising the throat as they would not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and lungs are diseased, as for those who have a hot swelling in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no issue with the trachea, it is necessary to use incising the throat to avoid the damage that results from suffocation.
If we intend to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the trachea with a small transverse cut between two rings, similar to the incision in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage. This location is appropriate for the incision.
For it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is timid, he should stretch the skin of the throat with a speculum then cut the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he cuts the peritoneum that we described, and determines the incision of the trachea from the phlegm that comes out with what is ejected, and the cessation of sound. The wound is left open for a while, and once the time when choking was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are sutured together from the skin.
And he sutures it alone without the cartilage, then uses medications that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book says, the explanation of this statement we have narrated is that if they see the patient has one of these tumors mentioned blocking his throat.
When the patient is near death and his breath is about to cease, they proceed to incise the larynx so that the patient can breathe through the wound and be saved from death. The wound is left open until the course of the disease ends, which usually lasts for about three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it heals. What I witnessed with my own eyes is that a servant took a knife and directed it at her throat, cutting part of the trachea. I was called to treat her and found her gasping like a slaughtered animal, so I exposed the wound.
I found that the blood coming out of the wound was minimal, so I was certain that no artery or vein had been cut. The air was escaping from the wound, so I immediately sutured it and treated it until it healed. The servant did not suffer anything except a hoarseness in the voice, nothing more.
And after days, it returned to its best condition, so from this we say that incising the larynx has no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On incising the tumor that appears in the pharynx from the outside, which is called pharyngeal elephantiasis.
This tumor, which is called pharyngeal elephantiasis, is a large tumor of the body's color and it is common in women. It comes in two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural one, there is no remedy for it. As for the accidental one, it comes in two types: one similar to fatty tumors, and the other similar to the tumor that occurs from the entanglement of the artery, and incising it is dangerous. One should not touch it with iron at all, except for the small ones. If you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not attached to any veins, then incise it as you would a fatty tumor. Extract it along with the cyst if it is in a cyst, otherwise remove all of it, then treat the area as appropriate with the necessary treatment.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On incising various types of tumors.
Tumors have many types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it can be confusing. I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and alarming pains until the inflammation subsides. The infection completes, then the fever and severity subside, and the swelling does not have any heat, fever, or pains. It is contained in a serous sac which is its special container, and it takes on the color of the body. Its beginning is like a lentil and becomes like a fig, larger and smaller.
It is of two types, either fatty or it contains moisture. The colors of the moisture are diverse as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you proceed to treat the swelling, you should first probe and examine it with an instrument called the mads, whose image will come in the section after this one.
Measure the size of the swelling, then remove the mads and look at what comes out in its trace. If moisture flows out, regardless of its color, make a slight incision as mentioned in the other swellings. If no moisture comes out after the mads, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this form as I have informed you, suspend it with forceps, and gently peel the skin from all sides while being cautious with the sac so that you can remove it intact along with the swelling.
If the sac is ruptured during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, then it often happens that way. Remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it, for if any part of it remains, whether little or much, the swelling will return most of the time.
If it overcomes you and a little of it remains, then enlarge the incision at the end with some sharp corrosive agents, suture the wound, and apply something to reduce the hot swelling above it. Treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, sew its edges and treat it with methods that promote healing.
If a vein, whether prominent or not, is encountered and bleeding occurs, immediately apply powdered zadj to the area and leave it tightly for two or three days until the wound becomes putrid and the blood's boiling subsides. Then return to cut what remains of the tendon.

Chapter Forty-Nine.
On incising the tumor that appears near the artery or vein, which is called aneurysm.
If the artery is cut and the skin above it heals, a tumor often appears as a result. Similarly, the vein may also swell and become tumorous.
The signs by which one can determine if the tumor and swelling are due to an artery or a vein: if the tumor is due to an artery, it will be elongated and concentrated deep within the body. If you press the tumor with your finger, you will feel a rustling sensation.

Chapter Sixty.
On the Removal of Stones.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and I have described the difference between stones generated in the kidney and those generated in the bladder, and the signs of all of this. I say that the stone generated in the bladder most often affects young boys, and one of its signs is that urine comes out of the bladder resembling water in its thinness.
Sand appears in it, and it irritates the penis, causing discomfort and fidgeting. It often hangs down and spreads, and diarrhea commonly accompanies it. Young boys are more susceptible to this until they reach fourteen years of age, while it is more difficult for the elderly. As for young adults, their condition is somewhere in between.
Those who have larger stones tend to have easier treatment, while smaller stones are the opposite. When it comes to treatment, the patient should first be given an enema to expel all waste in his intestines, as its presence may hinder the detection of the stone.
Then, the patient should be taken by his legs and shaken downwards to move the stone to the neck of the bladder. Alternatively, he can jump from a height several times. Then, sit him upright between your hands with his hands under his thighs so that the bladder tilts downward entirely, and then examine it.
You should examine it from the outside; if you feel the stone in the space, then immediately incise over it. If it does not fall under your touch at all, it is appropriate to smear the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a child or the middle finger.
If he is a mature boy, insert it into his perineum and search for the stone until it falls under your finger, then gradually move it towards the neck of the bladder. Then press on it with your finger and push it outwards toward the place where you want to make an incision.
You order a servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand and command another servant to lift the testicles upward with his right hand and pull the skin below the testicles away from the incision site with his other hand. Then you take the scalpel which looks like this:.
You make the incision between the perineum and the testicles, not in the middle but towards the left side. The incision should be made directly over the stone, with your finger in the perineum pressing it outward, ensuring that the incision is wider externally and narrower internally to allow the stone to pass through.
Do not make it larger, for the finger in the perineum may press the stone during the incision, causing it to exit without difficulty. Know that some stones have angles and edges, which can make their removal difficult.
Some are smooth, resembling a chestnut, and round, making them easy to extract. For those that have angles and edges, you should slightly enlarge the incision. If it still does not come out, you must use a trick, either by grasping it with a tightly held instrument whose end is like a file.
To secure the stone so it does not slip from it, or insert a delicate curved instrument beneath it; if you cannot manage that, widen the incision slightly; should any blood overpower you, cut it with the lancet.
If there is more than one, first push the larger one to the mouth of the bladder, then incise for it; afterwards, push the smaller one. Do this similarly if there are more than two. If it is extremely large, it would be ignorant to make a large incision for it, as it exposes the patient to one of two dangers.
Either he dies or he suffers continuous dripping of urine because the place does not heal at all. But try to push it out or use forceps to break it and remove it in pieces.
When you are done with your work, cleanse the wound with camphor, storax, and turpentine, then bandage it tightly. Insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will lead to corruption and infection of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type accordingly with similar treatments until it heals.
It is necessary at all times of treatment to bind the thighs and bring them together to stabilize the medicines that are placed on the area. If the stone is small and has entered the urethra and stuck there, preventing urine from passing, treat it with what I will describe before it necessitates incision.
For often I have dispensed with incision using this treatment, as I have tried it. It involves taking a bifurcated iron instrument, which looks like this:
Sharpen the tip to a triangle and fix it on a handle. Then take a thread and tie the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder, then gently insert the bifurcated iron into the urethra until the forks reach the stone itself.
Rotate the fork with your hand around the stone little by little, aiming to pierce it until you penetrate from the other side, at which point the urine will flow. Then grasp what remains of the stone outside the penis with your hand; it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will be healed.
If this treatment is not possible due to some obstacle, tie a thread below the stone and another above it. Then make an incision on the stone in the penis between the two threads and remove it.
Then loosen the tie and clean the clotted blood that has formed in the wound. The thread is tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other above it so that when the thread is loosened after the stone's removal, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, you should, when you tie the upper suture, raise the skin upwards so that it returns upon your completion and covers the wound as we have said.
Chapter Sixty-One.
On Removing Stones from Women.
Gallstones rarely develop in women. If one of them does have gallstones, treating it is difficult and often impossible for various reasons: one being that the woman may be unmarried, and another being that you may not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a doctor.
If she is chaste or related by kinship, and thirdly, you may not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially the manual work. The fourth reason is that the incision site for the stone in women is distant from the stone's location, so it requires a deep incision.
This carries a risk. If necessity dictates otherwise, you should seek a skilled female doctor, though they are rare. If one cannot be found, then ask for a chaste and gentle male doctor, or have a skilled midwife present, or a woman who can offer some guidance in this craft.
You should have her present and command her to perform all the examinations required for the stone, starting with checking if the woman is a virgin, in which case you should insert a finger into her rectum to examine the stone. If she finds it, she should press it under her finger.
Then you should order her to make an incision. If she is not a virgin but married, you should instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina to examine the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If you find it, you should insert it from the mouth of the bladder downwards to its fullest extent until it reaches the root of the thigh. Then make an incision opposite the middle of the genitals at the root of the thigh from whichever side is most accessible and feel for the stone in that area.
And her finger should not move from the stone, pressing under it, and let the incision be small at first, then insert the forceps through that small incision. If she feels the stone, increase the size of the incision according to what you know the stone can come out from, and know that types of stones are many.
Some are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round and branched, so recognize their types to guide you in what you wish to do. If the blood overpowers you, sprinkle powdered zadj in the area and hold it for a while until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone is removed, prepare the instruments I mentioned for removing stones from men to assist you in your work. If bleeding overpowers you and you know by the pulsation of blood that an artery has been cut, apply the cautery to the area.
Tie it tightly with bandages and leave it without repeating the procedure, and leave the stone without removing it as the patient might die. Then treat the wound; if the intensity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes clean, return to your work until you remove the stone.
The Sixty-Second Chapter.
On Incising the Watery Adrenal Gland.
Hydrocele is the accumulation of moisture in the white membrane that lies under the skin surrounding the testicle, which is called the scrotum. It can also be enclosed in a special sac prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
Until it seems like another testicle, and it is located between the scrotal skin and the white membrane we mentioned. This does not occur except rarely. This hydrocele results from a weakness affecting the testicles, causing this material to form, or it may result from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture can have various colors; it may be yellowish, or reddish like blood, or blackish dark, or whitish like water, and the last is most common. These are the signs by which it is recognized.
Where water accumulates, if it is in the white membrane we mentioned, the swelling will be round and slightly elongated like the shape of a testicle, and the testicle will not be visible because the moisture surrounds it completely from all sides.
If the moisture is in a special sac for it, then the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, a person may imagine that it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotal skin and the white membrane, it can be felt. If you want to know the color of the moisture, press the swelling with the square probe previously described. What comes out in the probe's impression will indicate its contents.
When we come to treatment with iron, you should order the patient to undergo bloodletting if possible and if his body appears full. Then he should lie on his back on something slightly elevated, and place many cloths under him. You should sit to his left.
He orders a servant to sit to his right, extending his penis to one side of the scrotum skin and towards the flank of the abdomen, then he takes a wide scalpel and cuts the scrotum skin lengthwise from the middle nearly to the groin.
The incision should be straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum skin into two halves until it reaches the white covering, then you peel it off and be careful not to cut it. Your peeling should be more on the side attached to the testicle and extend the peeling as much as you can.
Then, you widen and stretch the fluid-filled sac and remove all the water. Then, you separate the edges of the incision with forceps and pull the sac upward without touching the scrotal skin covering it, and cut the sac as much as possible, either in one piece or in segments.
Especially the thin side, for if you do not cut sufficiently, you cannot prevent the fluid from returning. If the testicle protrudes out of its skin while you are working, then when you finish cutting the sac, replace it and缝合阴囊皮肤的切口。
Then treat it as you would any other surgery until it heals. If the testicle is found to be diseased from another illness, you should tie the vessels in the spermatic cord to prevent bleeding, then cut the scrotum and remove the testicle, and treat it as previously mentioned.
If the fluid has collected on both sides, know that both are affected. Incise the other side in the same manner as the first. If it is feasible to perform the procedure in one operation, do so, then clean the blood.
You insert into the incisions wool that has been soaked in oil or in rose fat and you cover from outside with other wool that has been soaked in wine and oil and you spread it over the testicles and the abdominal fluids and place above it folded cloths which are the dressings. And you tie it from above with the six-ended band in this manner:.
Then, two cloths are taken, sewn, and filled with fluffed wool as in this example, and the ends which are to be tied are made of cotton or wet wool, gathered by pulling into the belt of the pants, and one end is placed on the buttocks towards the back.
The second is taken towards the groin, with the two ends on the thighs below and the remaining two ends on the buttocks, and all the pulling is gathered into the belt of the pants. This bandage can also be made by ironing instead of cutting with iron.
It involves taking a slender iron that is smooth and cutting with it the skin of the scrotum while it is hot, as described, until the white membrane containing the fluid is exposed. Take another iron in this manner:.
It resembles a Greek eye, then you press it against that membrane while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then stretch the membrane with probes and peel off with the iron the layer containing it, cutting as much as possible until you excise all of it, being careful not to let the fire touch the testicle.
Know that this procedure is safer, involves less bleeding, and is better than incision. If during the process of cutting or ironing, a hot swelling or bleeding or anything else obstructs you, then you should treat whatever impedes you with the appropriate treatment as previously mentioned.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure due to its ugliness, then use the cautery as I will describe it, if it is valid for you based on what we have provided of evidence.
The urine is watery, so the patient should sit on a high chair, then cauterize the swelling at its bottom with a wide instrument and make the cautery along the body. The incision should be wide enough for all the water to flow out, then put cotton on the area and tie it up.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out when you cauterize it, this is due to the white membrane blocking the mouth of the wound, preventing the water from coming out.
At that time, it is appropriate to insert a goose feather or the instrument used to extract water from the blisters, which has been described earlier, or slightly enlarge the incision.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after about six months. If it accumulates, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should endure for a few days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
On Incising the Fleshy Urinary Stone and Its Treatment.
Know that incising this type of stone is very risky and often leads to death. Therefore, we advise leaving it alone for safety. I will mention the procedure and types of swelling related to it.
I say that many swellings may occur in the parts that make up the testicles, and this can be due to various causes: either from hot residue that settles in the testicles or from a blow. The color of the swelling is like the color of the body, and it does not contain water.
The swelling is spreading, and sometimes the swelling is hard in color like a scar and has no sensation. It may be due to the complication of arteries and their inflammation as previously mentioned or from the inflammation of veins. What is due to the inflammation of arteries is known by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be subjected to it at all. As for what is due to the inflammation of veins, nothing disperses when pressed with fingers, and this type may affect it and other muscles.
It involves incising the scrotal skin, then elevating the testicle and extracting it from the white tunica vaginalis, freeing the spermatic cord from vessels, ligating the vessels, and cutting the spermatic cord after freeing it from all sides of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to the surrounding tissues.
It is necessary to remove and cut the testicle. If there is adhesion between any of the tunics or among the vessels, it is necessary to free all such adhesions and cut them in a circular manner.
If the growth of flesh is in the area of adhesion that is at the back, it is necessary to cut all of it and remove the testicle as we have said. Once your work is complete, clean the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and drink, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Four.
On the Treatment of Adrena with Dalia.
Dalia is a twisted swelling, somewhat like a cluster, with relaxation of the thighs, making it difficult for the patient to move, exercise, or walk. The treatment of this condition is similar to what has been previously mentioned.
But it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that you should make the patient sit on a high chair, then push the testicles downward with forceps, and hold the skin of the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels near the penis.
And another servant holds them and stretches them tightly, then you cut with a wide sharp scalpel close to the vessels until they are exposed, then peel from each side as I mentioned to you in the sectioning of the arteries in the thighs, then sew them with a needle containing a double thread.
Tie it at the first places where the ligament is exposed, and also tie it at the end. Then cut it in the middle with a vertical incision along the length of the body, and remove what has gathered in it of corrupt, impure moisture.
Then treat the wound as you would other wounds, allowing a period for healing so that the vessels nourishing one of the testicles may fall off. If the ligament is exposed to all the vessels, it is necessary to remove one of the testicles along with the vessels so that the sperm does not lack nutrition. If the vessels are cut beforehand, they will wither and be of no use.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
On the Treatment of Urinary Incontinence.
This incontinence occurs due to a rupture that appears in the abdominal fascia extending towards the groin from the abdomen's rings, causing urine to leak through the hernia into one of the testicles.
This hernia is either from cutting the fascia or from its extension, and these two types occur due to many causes, either a blow or a jump or a shout or lifting something heavy and so on. Its sign, if it is from the extension of the fascia, is that it occurs little by little over a long period of time.
And it does not happen suddenly, the swelling is even to a certain depth because the fascia compresses the intestine. Its sign, if it is from cutting the fascia, is that it causes great pain all at once from the beginning, and the swelling appears differently under the skin nearby.
That is the intestine coming out and moving outside the fascia. Sometimes the bladder may come out with the intestine, hence this hernia is called intestinal and vesical, and it might occur with gas. Excrement may also flow into the intestine and be trapped there, leading to the patient's death.
For it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when compressed. Treating these conditions with iron is dangerous, so you should be wary of it. The method involves ordering the patient to push the intestine back into his body if returning it is possible.
Then he lies on his back in front of you and raises his legs. You then stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and cut the skin of the scrotum entirely lengthwise, then insert retractors into the edges of the incision as needed to open it up.
The incision should be large enough for the testicle to come out. Then peel back the fascia under the scrotal skin until the hard white fascia is exposed on both sides. At this point, insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white membrane under the scrotal skin and the second membrane, the adhesion behind the testicle is separated, then the right hand folds it inward toward the scrotal skin, while the left hand pulls the white membrane upward and lifts the testicle with the membrane towards the incision.
You order the servant to pull the testicle upward and you completely separate the adhesion behind it, then examine with your finger to ensure there is no twisted intestine in the tough white membrane; if you find any, push it into the abdomen below.
Then take a needle with a thick thread that is made from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the membrane under the scrotal skin that adjoins the incision, then cut the ends of the twisted thread until there are four threads. Then you arrange them one over the other in a solid manner and tie the membrane that we mentioned is under the scrotal skin tightly from two sides, then also wrap the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. To prevent a hot swelling from occurring, and also to create a second tie separate from the first by less than two fingers. After these two ties, leave of the membrane under the scrotal skin an amount equal to the size of a finger bone.
To prevent a hot swelling from occurring, and also to create a second tie separate from the first by less than two fingers. After these two ties, leave of the membrane under the scrotal skin an amount equal to the size of a finger bone. Then cut all the rest in a circular manner and remove the testicle with it. Then make an incision on the lower part of the scrotal skin from which blood and pus will flow as described previously, then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and apply the bandage as described.
And the white membrane that we mentioned is cauterized after cutting it to prevent bleeding, for that often occurs. The ligament is left until it falls off by itself; if its falling is delayed, wash it with hot water and apply something that makes it sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
As for the windy hernia, I have not seen anyone dare to treat it with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be treated as we described for the hernia with a dartos muscle, which involves tying the vessels gently below after making an incision.
Then it is cut in the middle, and the swelling is treated with what opens it until the vessels fall off. The wound is treated as mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
On the hernia that occurs in the armpit.
A hernia may occur in the armpit as we have said, where the area bulges but nothing of the intestine descends to the thighs; if it does descend, it is minimal and returns at all times.
However, if the condition persists over time, the tear in the membrane may increase until the intestine and scrotum descend into the thighs. This occurs due to the extension of the membrane that is in the armpit, as we have said. The membrane extends, then relaxes and bulges in the area. Its treatment is by cauterization, as I have described previously.
And it is treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on his back before you, then you incise the site of the swollen hernia transversely to the width of three fingers, then you spread out the fasciae that are beneath the skin.
Until the white fascia beneath is exposed, then you take a tube and place it on the protruding part of the fascia, pressing it to the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts at the end of the tube from the fascia, attaching one to the other with sutures.
Then insert the end of the tube without cutting the fascia at all, and do not touch the testicle or anything else as I informed you in the treatment of urinary retention, then treat it with the treatment for wounds.
When the sutures are removed, clean the wound and keep it until it heals, for the fascia will contract and the hernia swelling will not increase.
And cauterization is better in this area because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
On the Relaxation of the Scrotal Skin.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the inner flesh relaxing, which makes its appearance unattractive. It is appropriate for one who wishes to treat it to have the patient lie on their back.
Then cut all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, and then bring the edges together. If you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four sutures and secure the sutures tightly, then cut what is between the sutures.
Then treat it with the treatment of other surgeries until it heals and the stitches fall off.
Chapter Sixty-Nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our Sharia.
For this reason, I should not have mentioned it in this book of mine. However, I mentioned it for two reasons: the first is so that it may be known to the doctor if asked about it and to know how to treat someone who has undergone it. The second reason is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Like lambs, goats, cats, and other such animals. I say that castration is of two types: either by compression or by cutting. The one done by compression is performed by placing the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then compress them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer discernible to the touch. As for castration by cutting, you should hold the animal, grasp the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the foreskin and make one incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles protrude, then cut them after peeling them and do not leave any membranes on them except for the thin membrane that covers the blood vessels. This method of castration is better than the one done by compression.
Because compression may leave some of the testicles, causing the animal to desire mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two types. The first is when, below the pubic area or on the scrotal skin between the two testicles, a form resembling a female genital appears, with hair, and urine may flow from that which is on the scrotal skin.
As for women, there is one type, and it appears above the vagina on the pubic hair area, resembling small male genitals coming out externally, one of which resembles a man's penis and the other two resemble the female genitals. The treatment for the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women should have the excess flesh cut until its trace is removed, then treat it like other surgeries until it heals. As for the second type in men from which urine flows and is located on the scrotal skin, no action is needed, and it will not heal at all.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the removal of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from women's genitals.
The clitoris may sometimes increase in size beyond the natural state until it becomes ugly and its appearance is displeasing. It may also enlarge in some women to the point that it spreads like a male's and becomes suitable for intercourse.
It should be grasped with your hand or a forceps, and the excess clitoris should be cut. Do not cut excessively, especially at the depth of the base, to avoid causing bleeding.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals, and as for the grown flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And it may come out to the outside like a tail. And for this reason, some of the early scholars named it the tail disease; you should cut it off as you would the clitoris and treat it until it heals.
The Seventy-Second Chapter.
On the treatment of hernia.
Hernia is when the woman's vagina is not perforated or the hole is small, and this can either be natural, with which she is born, or acquired. The acquired condition results from a previous illness and may be due to excess grown flesh.
Or it may be thin or thick membrane and this can either be in the depth of the womb, its sides, its top, or bottom, preventing intercourse, conception, childbirth, and sometimes menstruation. Knowledge of this is determined by the examination of a midwife.
If the obstruction is visible and close, if it is not visible, examine it with your fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from thin membrane and is close to the lips, promptly pierce it by placing something like forceps on the lips, then place your thumbs of both hands together.
The woman should lie on her back with her legs apart, then stretch the lips forcefully until you pierce the thin membrane and open the obstruction. Then take some wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. Have intercourse with the woman every day so that the area does not close again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, you should cut it with a wide scalpel resembling an iris leaf. If the obstruction is from grown flesh, fix it with retractors and cut it off; have medicines that stop bleeding without stinging such as oak bark, shian, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube to prevent the wound from closing quickly, keep it in place for days and use a wick of dry linen, then treat it with all treatments for inflammation until it heals. Sometimes other fleshy growths may appear in the uterus; they should be cut according to this method.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, for a cancerous tumor in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red blisters that appear in women's genital areas.
As for hemorrhoids, they are the swelling of vein openings until much blood flows from them continuously. When hemorrhoids worsen, they become warts. I have mentioned their types and signs in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What can be treated and what cannot be treated: I say that if the hemorrhoids and warts are deep inside the uterus and not perceptible, they should not be treated with iron. What is perceptible in the opening of the uterus and can be felt is what should be treated.
It is advisable for the woman to enter a cool room, then stretch the warts with a tweezers or a coarse cloth and cut them from their roots. Then, apply one of the blood-stopping agents that do not cause irritation when bleeding occurs.
Like scammony and sycamore fig and mastic and the like, then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then let them hang in cold water. If bleeding persists, sit her in a decoction of buckthorn and pomegranate peel and colocynth and the like, then take barley flour and knead it with honey and vinegar.
And dress it on her back, if the blood stops, otherwise persist with strong cupping of the breasts without restriction. Then take a wad of wool and soak it in the juice of bindweed or the juice of bistort or the juice of heather branches mixed with colocynth drink and apply it to the area.
When the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red boil, it resembles the heads of herpes, rough in appearance. It should be cut off as much as appears, according to what was mentioned regarding warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
On the growth that occurs in the uterus.
Various types of growths may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, leucorrhea, corrosive sores, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors. I have mentioned all these diseases, their types, and their symptoms in the classification.
It is appropriate to mention in this article the hot tumor that occurs in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors that gather over a period, how it should be cauterized with iron. One should observe if the pain of the tumor at its onset is sharp with palpitations, inflammation, and fever.
I have seen it red when sensation falls upon it; do not hurry to bandage it, treat it with what aids maturation until these symptoms subside. Then it is appropriate for the woman to sit on a chair with two seats and lean on her back.
She should bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and place her arms under her knees and bind them with a suitable strap.
Therefore, the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument that opens the mouth of the womb, whose image will be shown later. If you wish to use this instrument, you should estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's womb so that the part of the instrument that enters is not deeper than the womb.
It injures the patient if the instrument extends beyond the depth, so pads should be placed on the edge of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the womb. The spiral that runs through the instrument should be from the upper side.
You hold the instrument and the servant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened. When the pus appears and you touch it with your hand and find it soft and thin, you should cut it at the head with a broad scalpel. Once all the discharge has been expelled, you stop.
A soft wick should be placed in the wound, soaked in rose oil or green oil that has some astringent properties. The wick should extend out from the incision in the cervix. Outside, on the womb and pubic area, clean wool soaked in water boiled with alum should be applied.
Then treat it with the ointment suitable for that after two days until it heals.
The womb and the wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then with water in which the root of sus or long zeraunjan has been boiled, then return to the ointment. If the pus is hidden inside the womb.
It is necessary to abstain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated with what we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter Seventy-Five.
On instructing midwives how to treat live fetuses if they come out in an unnatural position.
The midwife should first know the form of a natural birth. Among its signs are when you see the woman bending downward and longing to breathe in air, and her labor becomes easier for her.
The fetus will come out quickly, so know that this birth is in the natural form and it comes out head-first with the afterbirth attached or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, you should press her abdomen so that the fetus comes out quickly.
For if it descends head-first, the afterbirth comes with it and is cleansed well of those superfluous materials. Any birth that does not follow this pattern is unnatural and undesirable, for the fetus may come out feet-first or hands before head and feet.
Or one hand or one leg may come out, or its head and hand, or it may come out folded, or it may turn onto its back, and other such undesirable forms. The midwife should be skilled and delicate in all these matters and beware of mistakes and errors.
And I clarify each of its forms and how to handle it so that you may understand and be aware. When the fetus comes out head-first in the natural manner, and this becomes difficult for the woman, and delivery is hard on her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and have women hold her steady.
Bandage her womb with cooked fenugreek and moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small forceps between her fingers to cut the placenta or she cuts it with her fingernail until the moisture inside flows out and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus is delivered.
If it does not come out, the woman should be irrigated with fenugreek juice mixed with ceratum ointment. Then after the irrigation, she should be told to bear down and sneeze with wild celery, and her mouth and nose should be held so that the fetus is delivered immediately. If the hands of the fetus come out first, they should be gently pushed back little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground while the woman holds on so she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus has died, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them gently, for it will come out. The fetus coming out feet-first: if the fetus comes out feet-first, you should push them upwards then turn the fetus little by little until it is aligned, then take one of its legs and stretch it little by little.
When they emerge to the thighs, order her to bear down and tickle her with hellebore, for it will come out. If it does not come out as described, then gently return the fetus little by little until you restore it to its natural shape, for it will then come out smoothly. If all of the above fails and it still resists.
Take the saliva of the chickpea and the saliva of the fenugreek, and the fat of the styrax, and a dissolved gum, then pound all together in a mortar well. Then apply it to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the navel.
When you see that the lower part has softened, make her a suppository of honey and have her hold it. If she retains the suppository for an hour, then seat her on a chair, tickle her, close her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen; the fetus will then be born at once, coming out on its knees and hands.
If it comes out in this manner, gradually insert your hands little by little. Then the woman should lie on her back with her legs hanging down below while lying on a bed, clasp her hands, and then gently press above her abdomen little by little. Then remove the pillows under her legs until the lower part is raised. If the fetus does not come out, take both of her legs and move them with a strong motion, then gently press above the navel little by little until the fetus rises upward, then the midwife should insert her hand and gradually adjust the fetus.
And order the woman to strain until the fetus comes out, the fetus coming out with one of its hands extended, hold onto withdrawing its hand, if she cannot withdraw it then raise the woman and make her walk, if she cannot walk then lay her down.
And shake her legs heavily, then use the levers which I described from the leashes. If labor seizes her, sit her on a chair, then treat by withdrawing the hand and aligning the fetus to its natural form, make the woman sneeze and order her to strain until it comes out.
The fetus coming out on its back, extending its hands, with its face towards the mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus' hand then turn it little by little and align it gently. Once aligned, apply what we described of oils and leashes to its front.
Then have her move little by little, once she does that, sit her on the chair and order her to lean to the right side, then have her strain and make her sneeze with cundus, for it will come out, the fetus coming out standing on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is drained of moisture. If it clings to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel so that it does not suffocate and die. Then push the fetus inside until it is positioned as it should be.
And order the woman to strain several times, for it will come out easily. The birth of twins or multiple fetuses: know that twins are often born, and three or four may be born and survive, though this is rare. As for five, it is something against nature and they do not live at all.
And they are surrounded by a single placenta, and they separate from each other with membranes between them attached to the navel of each one of them, and they may be born just as a single fetus is born in a natural and easy delivery, or they may be born in an unwanted manner as we have described.
If some of them are difficult to deliver and become in the unnatural forms we mentioned, treat them as described. Let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with tenderness and caution, and adapt to each form with the cunning that leads to safety.
What may be conceived in the womb and then abort, there may be conceived in the womb one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten. It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another aborted fifteen, all of them having been conceived.
By the wisdom of the All-Knowing, all of this should be within the knowledge of the midwife, for such occurrences may fall upon her one day, and she should meet them as is appropriate.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
On the Extraction of a Stillborn Fetus.
If you have treated the stillborn fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment and it has not been expelled, and circumstances compel you to use manual intervention, then you should assess whether the woman is strong and free from any symptoms that might cause harm. If so, the woman should lie down on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downward and her legs raised. A servant should hold her on both sides or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not move when extracting the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of her womb with moist ointments, along with the saliva of Al-Khutami and Al-Hulbah and flaxseed. Then the midwife anoints her hand with these ointments and salivas, then she inserts it into the womb gently, then she seeks a place to pierce with the instruments from the fetus, and the appropriate places for that.
If the fetus is in a position to descend head first, then pierce with the instruments in its eyes or at the back of its head or in its mouth or in its palate or under its chin or in its nape or in places close to the ribs or below the cartilages.
And if it descends feet first, then pierce in the groin and the middle ribs. It is advisable to hold the instrument with the right hand and place its bend between the fingers of the left hand, then gently insert the hand and pierce with the instrument in some of those places we mentioned.
Until you reach an empty space, then pierce another or a third instrument opposite it if needed so that the pulling is even and does not lean to one side. Then pull steadily, and the pull should not be straight only but moved in every direction.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth when extracted. Between these actions, the pulling should be relaxed. If one part is held up, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to rotate what is stuck. If part of the fetus comes out, then move the instruments to other positions.
She does this, raising it slightly, until the fetus is completely out. If a hand comes out first and cannot be returned due to compression, it should be wrapped with a cloth, then pulled until it is all out, then cut off.
Either in the wrist joint or in the elbow, and so it should be done in the other hand and in both legs. If the fetus's head is large or faces pressure during delivery or if there is collected water in its head, you should insert between the fingers a spiky forceps.
With it, you cut the head so that the water comes out or you crack it with an instrument called the mashdakh, whose image comes with the images of other instruments in the chapter that follows this.
Likewise, if the fetus naturally has a large head, you should split the skull or crush it with the mashdakh as mentioned, then extract the bones with forceps. If the head comes out but is compressed at the clavicle, cut it until the moisture in the chest flows out.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and delivery is not possible, cut it as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or has a tumor, you should puncture it so that the moisture flows out.
If the fetus is positioned head-down, pulling it out will be easy and aligning it to the mouth of the womb will be simple. If it gets compressed at the abdomen or chest, you should pull it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and cut the abdomen or chest until what is inside flows out.
If you have extracted all the limbs but the head has retracted and is stuck, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix, search for the head with her fingers, and pull it to the mouth of the womb. Then she should insert forceps or two forceps and pull it out with them.
If the mouth of the uterus has closed due to a hot tumor, it should not be forced. Instead, at that time, the application of moist and fatty substances should be used, and bandages should be applied, and she should sit in waters that relax and moisturize.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if it can be aligned, use what we mentioned for a living fetus. If that cannot be done, then it should be cut and removed. It is essential that nothing of the afterbirth be left inside at all. Once you have finished your treatment, apply what we have mentioned for treating hot tumors that occur in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, she should bathe in constricting waters and use the rest of the treatment. I have witnessed a woman who had been pregnant, then the fetus died inside her, and afterwards, she became pregnant again.
Then the other fetus also died. After a long time, a tumor appeared in her lower abdomen, swelling until it burst and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound would not heal, so I applied some of the strong pulling ointments to it.
A bone came out of the site, then after some days, another bone came out. I was amazed by this because the abdomen is a place without bones, so I concluded that they were bones from the dead fetus. I examined the wound and extracted many bones from the fetus's head.
I continued this process until I extracted many bones, and the woman was in the best of health. She lived for a long time with only a slight discharge of pus from the site. I relate this rarity here.
For it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to treat with his hands.

And their width is about the size of a finger, and their length is one and a half fingers. In the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides made of the same type of wood, firmly attached to them. Their length should be about half a finger or slightly more, and their width should be around two fingers or slightly more. These two guides.
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the dilators. An image of another tool for the same purpose, finer and lighter:.

So seat the woman on a bed with her legs dangling and spread apart between her thighs. Then insert these two guides together into the cervix, while you hold the end of the tool between her thighs. Then open your hand with the tool as you would with forceps, to the extent you desire to open the cervix.
