Chapter Seventy-Eight.
On the Removal of the Afterbirth.
If the afterbirth is retained after delivery, it is advisable to instruct the patient to hold herself and then tickle her nose with cinders. Place your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out by this method, otherwise take a quantity [of another substance].
Make a hole in the lid and place there herbs that open the uterus, such as fennel, hyssop, dill, chamomile, thyme, southernwood, and centaury. Use all of these medicines or some of them, immerse them in water, and heat it over fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole of the pot lid with the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then sneeze as we said, for the afterbirth will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in bear's fat or assafoetida's juice.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; when you find it, grasp it and pull it out slowly. If it is attached deep in the uterus, insert the hand as described until you find the afterbirth, then pull it out little by little gently.
So that the uterus does not fall out due to strong pulling, it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then increase the amount of pulling, for it will respond at that time and become detached. If the opening of the uterus is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the speculum and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that has been described, do not be forceful in attempting to pull it out again. Instead, tie what has come out to the opening of the uterus and to the woman's thigh, then inject her with the quadruple ointment, for it will cause it to detach after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, but if it becomes putrefied, a bad smell will rise from it to the stomach and head, which can harm the patient. Therefore, appropriate fumigation should be used.
For this reason, some of the early practitioners have tried the fumigation of colocynth and dried figs. Here is the description of the apparatus used to fumigate a woman when menstruation or afterbirth is retained, and so on:
It is made from lead, resembling a funnel, or it can be made from brass. The thin end is placed in the wound and the wide end over fire with incense prepared on embers. Hold it until that incense disappears and repeat with more.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the treatment of unperforated hemorrhoids.
Many boys may be born with their anal passages unperforated, blocked by a thin membrane. The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger; otherwise, she should use a sharp lancet. She must be careful not to touch the muscle.
Then place wool soaked in the decoction and oil on it, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear it may close up, insert a lead tube into the opening for many days, removing it only when the child needs to defecate.
The anus may also become blocked due to healing of a wound or swelling. It should be incised where it has healed, then treated with the treatments we have mentioned, including the use of lead.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of abscesses that occur below.
Abscesses that occur below are a complex and thickening that happens near the anus externally or in the space from one of the sides, and the abscess can be singular or multiple.
If that complex persists for a long time, it may open and discharge a white watery fluid or thin pus. Some of these abscesses may penetrate to the rectum and intestines, while others do not penetrate. The penetrating ones can be identified by the stool and gas that may come out when the patient defecates.
And perhaps worms may emerge from it, and abscesses may form if they are in the space penetrating to the bladder and the urethra, and some may penetrate to the hip joint and to the tailbone. What distinguishes the penetrating abscess reaching the buttocks is also different from those that do not penetrate.
Insert your index finger into the buttock and insert a thin probe made of copper or iron into the abscess if there is no bend in it. If there is a bend, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing comes out of the hole, no feces, no smell, nor worms as we said, then know that it is not penetrating, so proceed with the treatment.
And if the abscess penetrates to the bladder or the urinary tract, the evidence is the discharge of urine from it and its resistance to healing with medications. As for if it penetrates to the hip joint or the tailbone, its sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no bend toward the bone, and pain persists in one area, and pus continually discharges from it, and no treatment works, nor does it heal with ointment, then know that these penetrating abscesses will never fully heal.
Treating them is a vain effort for those physicians who improperly attempt to deal with it. As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, there is hope for their cure with iron as I will describe and what I have tested, which is to lay the patient down in front of you on their back.
He should lift his legs upwards and his thighs inclined towards his abdomen, then insert a copper or lead probe if there is a stricture, gently until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe pass through on its own, clearly visible without sensing any intervening fascia or flesh between your finger and it, then be certain that it has passed through. Do not exert further effort as there is no healing benefit from it, as we have said.
They have said that it may heal in some people, though rarely. Among the treatments that may be beneficial is to heat a thin iron on according to the size of the stricture as was mentioned before, and insert it hot into the stricture until it reaches near the anus, then repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all the excess flesh that has formed, resembling a bird feather tube, has been burned off, then treat it with a cloth soaked in fat until the burned flesh is removed, then treat it with healing ointments, and if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. If you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick barrier of flesh or fascia between them, and you see the stricture near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin from the beginning of the stricture at that time. You should pass the probe through the incision while it is in the stricture until the incision reaches where the tip of the probe ends, and then the probe is removed and falls out. Then clean the excess flesh that has formed, resembling a bird feather tube, leaving nothing of it behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the blood overpowers you and prevents you from completing your task of cutting those flesh pieces, then the best thing to do and what is most beneficial is cauterization with fire or a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire combines two good conditions: burning off that excess flesh.
And stop the bleeding and dry out the moisture, then treat it with linen soaked in fat or sulfur powdered with oil until pus forms and the burned flesh comes out with the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of solid flesh.
These are medicines that constrict and dry. For if it heals, it is cured, and there is no fear of recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is away from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel with the probe that there is a barrier of membrane or flesh and it is close to being penetrated, then the treatment should only be attempted with hope and optimism. This can be done in one of three ways: either by cauterizing it as we mentioned.
Or you may cut it open until you reach its base so that you can effectively introduce linen and treatment close up, but do not overextend the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus, causing the patient to involuntarily release feces. Then treat it as we have mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or you may penetrate that barrier and pierce it after cutting it close to the anus, as we mentioned, either with a probe or another sharp instrument. Then thoroughly clean all the clotted flesh in the incision as much as you can.
Then, set the fracture of the entire wound with the incision and the upper opening until it is sealed, and leave the fistula open inside the rectum to be lighter on the patient, and sometimes the fistula may perforate in this manner.
It is when the probe is inserted into the fistula and it is on one side of the rectum towards the surface of the body with the skin and the anus ring, then take at that time a perforated tip probe like a cobbler's needle in this form:.
And insert a braided thread of five threads or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches the bottom, if it is perforated on the edge of the rectum from the inside nearby.
Then, pull the thread out from that hole by inserting your finger into the rectum and pulling out the end of the thread, gather both ends together, and tighten them. Leave it for a day or two; every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the mouth of the fistula is not perforated, perforate it however you can, except if it is very deep, in which case you should not do so to avoid cutting the muscle. Then treat it as mentioned until it heals.
A picture of the saw-shaped surgical instrument used to split fistulas, where the cutting edge is very sharp and the other side is not sharp so as not to cut what is not needed, as you see:.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids that bleed and their removal and the healing of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red blossoms or like grape bunches, and they vary in size, small and large, and blood flows from them constantly.
They can be singular or numerous, and they can be outside the rectum at its edges. However, those that are outside the rectum are usually less moist, with yellow water or a little blood flowing from them constantly, and they are of the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the rectum is to order the patient to defecate and strain until the rectum protrudes, and the warts appear before you. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or hold them with your fingernail and cut them at their bases. If they do not hold in the forceps due to their moisture and laxity.
So scrape them with a coarse cloth or a piece of rough bread, or pull them out with your fingers, then cut them. Then sprinkle some sharp powders on them after cutting to serve as cauterization, or cauterize them as previously described in the section on cauterization.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatments until it heals. If the rectum does not come out, administer an enema with a slight irritant to wash out its contents and make it easier to protrude when the patient strains. As for the warts that protrude from the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
It is that you take it with your fingernail or hook it with a forceps and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And for those who dislike cutting with iron, it is advisable to use puncturing in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and pierce it with a needle at its base from the other side, and wrap the ends of the thread under the needle while it crosses and tighten the wart firmly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all warts except leave one that you do not puncture.
Let the blood residue flow out, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerotic on the hemorrhoid and put it over the hemorrhoid and instruct the patient to rest, then leave it until it falls off. Once the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often result from dryness and constipation. If it persists and medication does not work, it should be shaved with a scalpel or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer that prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the procedure with a more severe shaving until it bleeds well and peels off, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the treatment of reversed and unreversed nails, dry warts, and molluscum contagiosum.
The nail is a round knot on the color of the body, resembling a nail head. It occurs all over the body, especially in the soles of the feet and the fingers, and walking causes pain from it.
It should be cut around the nail, held with forceps or a pincette, and removed from its root. Then treat it; if you wish, cauterize it as previously described in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, covered, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
Warts often appear on children's hands, and their treatment is to stretch the wart, cut it, or prick it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it as previously described with fire or a sharp medicine.
Inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument I mention in the treatment of the ant hill. As for the ant hill, it is also a small, covered, thick protrusion on the surface of the body that goes very deep, and if it gets cold, one feels a sting similar to that of an ant there.
It appears all over the body, mostly on the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from a duck feather or a strong eagle feather and place that tube over the ant hill until the tube's circle surrounds it completely. Then rotate your hand with the tube until you cut around the ant hill.
Insert it into the depth of the flesh and remove the ant hill from its root. If you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:.
The top of the tube to the thinness is sealed and twisted to make it easy for the fingers to control and twist it, and if you want, cauterize the wart as was mentioned in cauterizing warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the forms of the instruments used for injections in diseases of constipation, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or of china, or of drawn or beaten brass, and this instrument may be made in small and large sizes according to the users. Those used for treating small children should be small, and for those with narrow or painful bowels, their syringes should be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:.
The upper tube where the nozzle is attached should be wide, with a barrier where the nozzle is tied as you see. Its lower end, which enters the rectum, should be sealed and very smooth to thinness. On one side there are two holes, and on the other side, there is one hole.
The size of the hole should be as thick as a needle or slightly thicker. The nozzle where the medicine is placed should be made from an animal bladder or lamb skin, shaped like a small funnel, with a diameter of one finger-breadth.
It should be pierced with many holes as it turns, with each hole spaced a finger's thickness apart. Then, a thin thread made of ten or so twisted threads is inserted into those holes to tie the nozzle together like a funnel. Then place the medicine in it, tightly secure the tip of the syringe between two stones, then inject with it. This is the image of the circle of the flask and the hole:.
The method of instillation is to fill the bladder or pouch with medicine while it is warmed to the patient's tolerance; be cautious that it is not too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on their back and lifts their legs up.
It is placed on a pillow or cloth, then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil or egg white or fenugreek juice or similar. Then it is gently inserted into the anus. The servant then takes the flask in both hands and squeezes it forcefully until all contents of the syringe are emptied into the intestines.
Then the syringe is removed and the patient lifts his legs against the wall. If the medicine induces a need to expel, he should hold it back as much as possible; even if he sleeps all night, it will be more effective for retention. If used for diarrhea conditions and he hastens its exit, then repeat the enema until he is healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the anus, then the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, like those used for children. This is the image of a gentle syringe with its skin tightly stretched:.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the division the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and here I will discuss their treatment by hand and with medicines in completeness. I say that wounds differ according to the thing by which the wound is caused and according to the location where the cut occurs.
The things with which surgeries are performed are numerous, such as a stone strike or sword cut or knife slash or lance, stick, or arrow puncture and other similar numerous things.
As for surgeries according to the body parts, it is like a wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and other similar organs.
And I describe the treatment of some wounds to set a standard and rule for all other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds in particular because the complex ones will be mentioned in the third chapter of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no bone fracture, I examine it. If it was caused by a stone strike or something similar and only the skin has been cut and the wound is large and I fear swelling in the patient, then immediately treat it with a scalpel on the spot. Do not delay this, and let blood according to the patient's strength if there is no reason preventing leeching, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is minimal. The leeching should be done on the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against overeating and drinking, and against heavy foods. If there is something preventing leeching, reduce their food intake, avoid meats and drinks. If a hot swelling occurs on the wound, apply cotton soaked in rose oil alone or mixed with an astringent beverage.
If there is no fear of hot swelling, apply to the fresh wound its own blood if it has not been altered by the atmosphere, which I will describe to you shortly. If the air has altered it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until the pus flows out, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or similar and its edges do not come together with bandages, sew it up as I will describe in缝合腹部伤口。
If the skin is exposed to the bone and hangs without attachment except for a small piece, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines that promote the growth of strong flesh instead of the skin, and advise the patient to nourish themselves with foods that are strengthening, such as heads and trotters and the like.
If gangrene occurs in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut off all the gangrenous part and discard it, then treat it. If there is an artery or vein that bleeds in these wounds and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, search for the artery.
If you hit it and it does not stop, cut it with a lancet and tie it off. If necessity calls and what we mentioned does not help, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy; it suffices to sprinkle this powder on them while they are still bloody.
Before the air alters it, the recipe for the powder is to take one part of gum, two parts of scamony, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime. Crush everything, sieve it, and pack the wound with it, binding it tightly so that it adheres well.
It凝固后，用绷带从上面绑紧，如果不肿胀就不要解开，直到完全愈合。如果手中没有乳香和 scamony，单用石灰也能达到同样的效果。
And this action can also be done with two plasters in many major surgeries if he masters placing them and tightening them, and the wounds are fresh with their blood, unless the air has changed it; however, if the air has slightly changed it, especially during summer time.
There is no effective meaning in placing this cautery; treat it as we mentioned, by applying some mature ointments to it or apply to it a porridge made from barley flour with water and honey until the pus discharges, then continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If a fracture occurs in the bone along with the wound and it is minor, treat it with a splint. I have described the treatment of skull fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between a simple neck wound and a head wound.
And if a nerve or an artery in the neck has been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with things that cause severe constriction, such as cantharides and zinc, and the like, because they harm the nerve and constrict it.
Nothing cold should be applied to the wound at all because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs, should be treated with soft medications such as washed cotton soaked in fresh water several times, moistened with oil or rose and tuta oil.
It should also be washed with fresh water and other metallic stones of this description, and moist ointments. If the wound is large, use sutures or bring its edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and pus has gathered in a hidden place at its bottom, line it with a drain in the lowest part of it. If an artery was cut off in the wound and you see blood flowing out, then sever it or tie it up or cauterize it if necessity demands that.
If the wound has cut some or all of the rings of the trachea but the vocal cords are intact, then suture the skin edges over a tracheal tube. Do not touch the trachea itself, but align it and return it to its natural shape, then secure it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone fragments in it, remove all of them or as much as possible, and leave what cannot be removed until the wound becomes septic and heals, making their extraction easier.
If the wound is suppurative, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If bone becomes septic in the wound and turns into a sinus, treat it with the methods that will be mentioned in its section, in the surgery of the chest and what is between the shoulders.
If it is a stab from a lance or knife and you see that it has depth, examine it. If air comes out when the wounded person breathes, know that it is a fatal wound. If it does not have depth and is suppurating with blood, do not apply powder to it at first and do not bandage it tightly so as not to trap the blood in its depth.
It can lead to the heart and kill the patient. However, apply an attractive ointment to it and change it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, place old cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb any moisture that comes out, and let the patient sleep on the side of the wound.
To liquefy what accumulates therein, even if three days or more have passed since the wound occurred and there has been no spasming in the patient nor poor fluttering nor constriction in the breath, and you see that all his conditions are sound, then know that the wound is healthy, so treat it with lint and the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If it is difficult to see and the discharge continues constantly, then know that it has become a fistula; treat it from its opening. If there is a wound on the chest or back, treat it with what was previously mentioned of suturing to liquefy what accumulates therein.
If it is large, cauterize it; if it is small, ligate it. If it has affected the bone and broken fragments from it, examine the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until they become so.
If the wound putrefies, it becomes easier to remove [the debris]. As for other wounds occurring in other organs, their treatment follows the same principles as what we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On abdominal surgery, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound that occurs in the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. A large wound may cause one or several intestines to protrude, making their reinsertion and suturing more difficult and challenging.
A small wound may also present other difficulties, as returning it to its place can be more challenging. If you do not promptly return the intestine to its position, it may swell and become thick, making reinsertion difficult. For this reason, a medium-sized wound is often preferable because returning the intestine to its place is less difficult compared to the other two types.
And know that stitching the abdomen has four aspects. The two general methods are suitable for stitching the abdomen and for stitching all other body wounds, while the other two specific methods apply to all injuries but are more particularly specialized for stitching the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. So, I say that if the wound is small and part of the intestine protrudes and it is difficult to replace it, this is due to one of two reasons: either the incision is too small as we said, or the intestine has been inflated due to the cold air.
If that is the case, it should be warmed by soaking a sponge or a wet cloth in lukewarm water alone or in which aqyl, sa'di, and sunbul have been boiled, and then apply it to the intestine until the inflation subsides.
This can also be done with a decoction that has constrictive properties, which is better than water alone for reducing inflation. When you see the inflation has subsided, moisten the intestine with water in which hameham or khubbaz has been boiled, as this will facilitate its easier replacement.
If it is still impossible for it to return after this treatment, slightly enlarge the incision with the instrument used for abscesses. The first treatment is preferable if possible rather than making an incision. Incision is only resorted to as a last necessity when other methods fail. This is the image of the instrument:
One of its curved ends is limited while the other end is not limited. The thin edge should not be as sharp as a scalpel but slightly blunted. This instrument resembles a small probe, as you can see. If the wound expands and the intestine enters, it should be replaced in its natural position.
And if you can place it in its specific location, that is best. And if the incision is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back and raise his legs above his head; and if it is in the upper abdomen, he should raise his head and chest above his lower body.
And similarly, if the incision is on one side of the abdomen, your constant aim and purpose should be to have the side with the surgery higher than the other side. This should thus be applied in major surgeries and in moderate surgeries.
And as for minor surgeries, place him according to what is feasible for you. Then have a gentle servant ready who holds the entire incision with both hands and brings its edges together, then gradually reveals it bit by bit to the person doing the suturing.
And this is the description of a common suturing method, which is to take one needle or several needles according to the width of the wound. Then leave a length equal to the thickness of the little finger from the edge of the incision and pierce one needle without inserting thread into the edges of the skin. And pierce through both edges of the peritoneum underneath the skin from inside until it comes out from that side, having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then tighten with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound come together firmly.
Then leave a length equal to the thickness of the finger again and pierce another needle, then secure it with thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with however many needles are needed until the entire wound is sutured. The needles should be of moderate thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
For the very thin needles quickly cut the flesh and the thick ones also have difficulty entering the skin; therefore, they should be of moderate thickness. The needle should not be inserted at the edge of the skin too closely.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not open before healing, nor should the suturing be too loose so that the wound cannot close. Then, cut the ends of the needles so they do not harm the patient while sleeping, and make loops from cotton fabric on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is most suitable for small surgeries, as they may only require one or two needles or so.
As for the method of common suturing, it involves bringing together the four edges, that is, the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum, in one stitch with a needle threaded with moderately thick twine. Once this needle has passed through the four edges, return the needle from the side where you started.
So that the thread crosses over the top of the wound, making the suturing similar to the way a sack is sewn to hold belongings. Leave between each stitch a space as wide as the thickness of the little finger. This type of suturing is known by all people.
With this suturing method, a surgery was performed on a man's abdomen that had been cut by a knife. The wound in the surgery was more than a handspan wide, and about two handspans of the intestines had protruded. The tear was in the middle of the abdomen.
I turned it after its moisture had exited the wound for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days. I treated him until he recovered, and he lived many years afterward, managing all his affairs. The physicians had ruled that he would never recover at all.
It is surprising that I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where there were no medicines at all. I would put old cotton on the wound twice a day and attend to washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the types of special suturing, I mention it as per Galen's words: you start suturing from the skin, and insert the needle from outside to inside. Once the needle is passed through the skin and the muscle going straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle and passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside on the other edge of the intestine. Once you pass it, pass it again in this same edge of the intestine from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside. As you pass it through the peritoneum, go to the edge of the intestine on its side until you pass through all of it.
Then begin again from this same side and suture it with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and exit the needle from the skin near it. Then reinsert it into that skin and suture the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the intestine.
Extract it from the skin on its side, and do this once again and do it repeatedly until you sew the entire surgery in that manner, and the description of this sewing is to sew like sewing fur for skins.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leave it once from the other side until you finish, and as for the second type of special suturing that Galen also mentioned, this is his exact words.
It is that you sew it in the manner that some healers sew it, by bringing each part to its corresponding similar part naturally, joining one edge of the peritoneum to the other edge and one edge of the diaphragm to the other edge, which is done as I describe to you.
You should prick the needle into the edge of the diaphragm that is nearest to you from the outside and insert it inside by itself, then leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and insert it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum, and then return it again.
And insert it from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the diaphragm which is on the opposite side, and this type of suturing is better than the general simple suturing which was mentioned earlier. This is Galen's exact words. Some have said that the abdomen can be sutured with two needles. This is done by inserting a single thread into both and beginning by piercing the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side and passing it back to your side, just like the suturing used for garments.
And know that if the wound is in the center of the abdomen, then缝合它比腹部其他部位更难。至于伤口的治疗，其处理方式与其他创伤相同。即，如果你在血液未被空气改变前及时接触到新鲜伤口，复位内脏并妥善缝合，然后使用促进愈合的药物。
If the wound has been altered by air, then apply some ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the threads fall off, and the peritoneum and intestines heal. Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply old cotton when it begins to suppurate and change it twice a day as I have informed you until it heals. For in most cases, you will not need other treatments if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound may cause pain to the major organs.
It is advisable to dip a soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose fat and place it around the areas between the armpit and the groin. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, which often happens, inject him with a mild, lukewarm astringent decoction.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and become a perforating wound to its cavity. Know that what is thick among the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin among them is harder to heal.
As for the intestine known as the duodenum, it does not heal at all from any surgery due to the abundance and thickness of its vessels, the delicacy of its wall, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. However, if what protrudes from the wound is fat and you encounter it fresh, then replace it as you would with the intestine itself.
If time has passed and it has turned green or black, you should tie a thread above the place where it has turned black to prevent bleeding, for there are veins and arteries in the swelling, then cut what is below that ligature.
You should make the ends of the thread hang down from below the surgery, protruding out to make it easier for you to pull and remove them when the swelling subsides and the wound discharges pus. Regarding the wound that occurs in the intestine, if there is a small tear in the intestine.
It may heal in some people, for I have seen a person who was stabbed in the abdomen with a spear, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. The wound became chronic and turned into an opening from which feces and gas were discharged. I treated him, although I did not hope for his recovery. I continued to treat him until he healed and the area closed. When I saw that the area had closed, I feared for the patient that some misfortune might occur in his interior, but no such misfortune ever happened to him, and he remained in the best of health. He is healthy, eating, drinking, engaging in intercourse, taking baths, and serving in his duties. Some experienced people have mentioned that if there is a small wound in the intestine, it should be sutured in this manner.
It involves taking large-headed ants, then bringing the edges of the wound together, and placing one of these ants, with its mouth open, on the edges of the wound. When it bites down and tightens its mouth, cut off its head; it will stick and not come undone. Then place another ant close to the first one.
And continue doing that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then return them and suture the wound, for those heads will remain attached to the intestine until it swells and heals, and it does not cause any harm to the patient at all; the intestine can also be sewn with fine thread.
That which is used from the viscous animal substance after it enters into a needle is to take one end of this thread from the substance and make it smooth, then tie a thin, twisted cotton thread at its end, then insert that thread into the needle. And in it, the thread of the adhesive substance is used to suture the intestine, then it is returned to the body cavity. This method of suturing with ants and adhesive substance is done out of hope and expectation.
But if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy for it and it will not heal at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the Treatment of Cold and Nasal Polyps.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and prolonged and turns into an ulcer and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus without ceasing, is generally called a naysoura in any part of the body, and we call it zakama, the naysoura.
It is truly a hardened, white mass that forms without pain and has a hollow like the hollow of a bird's feather.
For this reason, some have called it a feather. It can be moist at times and continuously discharge pus; sometimes the moisture may cease. This moisture can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
I have presented in the section on incision when mentioning surgeries that every ulcer which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes: one of them is either a lack of blood in the body or its poor quality, or there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or that it is abundant in pus and dirt, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material supplying it is of poor quality, or the treatment used for it is inappropriate, or due to corruption that has occurred in the locality similar to an epidemic, or due to a specific characteristic of the locality.
Or that it is abundant in pus and dirt, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material supplying it is of poor quality, or the treatment used for it is inappropriate, or due to corruption that has occurred in the locality similar to an epidemic, or due to a specific characteristic of the locality. As observed in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, or because there is one bone or several bones [involved]. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the section on incision.
It is fitting for me here to inform you of the treatment of fistulas and rhinitis which is performed through manual manipulation. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some may extend to large veins or arteries, or to nerves.
or to the peritoneum or to the intestines or to the bladder or to a rib or to a vertebra of the spine or to a joint of the complex joints such as the hand and foot, and some extend near a major organ and so on.
Those fistulas that have such characteristics are among the difficult-to-heal diseases that do not respond to treatment, and working on them is laborious and requires knowledge. If there are fistulas that have not reached the areas I mentioned and you hope and have confidence in treating them.
And if you wish to ascertain that, take a probe of copper or iron if the sinus runs straight and examine it with it. If there is a bend in the sinus then examine it with a lead probe because lead softens its body.
It narrows at the entrance and bends towards the curve. If the sinus has many mouths that you cannot determine with a probe, inject one of its mouths. The moisture you inject will go towards the other mouths and flow out.
Then thoroughly examine in whatever way you can to determine if there is any bone or nerve, or whether the depth of the sinus is far or near, or if it is a single sinus with many mouths. Ascertain all this to the best of your ability through careful inspection and the pain felt when you gently touch the area with your hand.
And similarly regarding the signs and causes leading to the swelling. If you have ascertained all of this accurately, then proceed with confidence to the treatment by considering whether it is.
If the sinus is clearly close to the surface or in a safe location far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places mentioned to you, then incise the sinus as described previously and remove what is inside it of pus, rotten flesh, and excess tissue.
And what grows in its mouth of flesh or polyps and the like, treat it until it heals. If the sinus is deep seated and runs straight, you should incise it as deeply as possible and then clean it thoroughly of all rotten flesh.
Then use the soaked lint in sharp medicines and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you reach with iron, do that to it several times until that sharp medicine eats all the corruption remaining at the bottom of the fistula.
Then treat it with ointments that grow healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal thereby, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone and this is confirmed to you, then incise and split it as I have informed you, unless there is a hindrance preventing you.
If a vein, nerve, or major organ, as we have said, is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackening in the bone, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what heals it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues to flow as before.
Know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption, so reveal all that is on it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort, then treat it. If it heals, otherwise know that this corruption is in a deep and distant place, so reveal all that is on it again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is a small bone and you can extract it, then pull it out with delicate forceps that are suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, then thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any behind to the extent of your effort. If something like ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and flesh if there is any, and free the bones.
If you are unable to extract the bones at your time, apply to the wound what prevents putrefaction and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones rot. Protect the wound so that the incision which you made during your work does not heal.
Rather, if you fear that, place cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For if those ligaments rot and the bones are cleaned, extracting and pulling them out will be easy.
If it is a single large bone, such as the leg bone or thigh bone or the like, and only its surface has rotted, then scrape it thoroughly until that black decay disappears, then set the wound. If a large part of it has rotted and the decay has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to spread it open.
Cut it all until the decay ends. Then treat it until it heals. I inform you of a case of gangrene that affected a young man's leg to serve as an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young in age.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause that moved within the body until it reached the leg, causing severe swelling and a large tumor, with no external cause. Then time progressed along with the mistakes of the physicians until the tumor ruptured.
A lot of material flowed out of it, and his treatment was mismanaged until gangrene set in the leg, and many openings developed, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgical practice.
Until he came to me and I saw his leg, and the materials flowing from those openings in great abundance, and the man had become emaciated and his color turned yellow; so I inserted the probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone, then I examined all the openings.
And I found them communicating with each other from all sides of the leg, so I decided to incise one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone, and I found it rotten, decayed, blackened, putrefied, and perforated until the holes extended to the marrow. I then spread out what was revealed to me.
And I removed the decayed bone, and I assume that there is no other decay in the bone besides what I have cut and spread out, and that I have completely excised it. Then I proceeded to close the wound with healing medications for a longer period, but it did not heal. So I returned and exposed the bone again beyond the first exposure.
And I found the decay connected to the bone, so I spread out what of that decay was revealed to me as well. Then I tried to close it, but it did not heal or join. So I exposed it again and kept cutting the bone piece by piece and attempted to close it, but it would not heal until I had cut off about a handbreadth of the bone.
And I removed it along with its marrow, then I closed it with medications and it healed quickly and was cured. The repeated operations and incisions were necessary due to the patient's weakness, his low tolerance, and my fear for him of death, as he would often faint from excessive vomiting. He was completely cured.
And flesh hardened in the place of the bone and its condition improved in his body and its strength decreased and it functioned in its states and no harm that could hurt him at all was encountered in his walking, then if a bone protruded in a part of the body it had fractured.
It should be treated in this manner, which is to take a bandage and tie it at the end of the protruding bone and have someone pull it upwards, then make another bandage from coarser wool than the first one.
Then bind it to the flesh beneath the bone and extend its end so that the flesh is pulled down, and you expose the flesh from the area you want to spread so that the saw does not harm the flesh, and place a piece of wood or board tightly under the bone from below.
For if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the rotten bone, and it should be spread slightly above the place of decay so that there is no corruption inside the bone cavity that does not appear on its surface to the senses, thus forcing you to spread it again.
If the bone is rotten but not protruding, and instead is connected to each other with the decay in its center or in part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides, then place the board from below, then spread it from the first side where the decay is.
When you are finished spreading it, then spread it from the other side, and let the spreading be slightly away from the decay as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the rotten joint itself and spread the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the corruption is in the palm of the hand or the sole of the foot, treating it is extremely difficult, but you should peel off the corruption as you see fit and cleanse it thoroughly.
And by what means can you proceed when a vein or nerve is not in your way? Know that the saws and cutting implements for severing these bones are numerous according to the position, setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and looseness of the bones. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument suitable for that work.
Know that the works themselves may guide you to the type of instrument you need if you have long practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for skill in the craft comes from seeing various kinds of diseases. For one may devise for themselves instruments suitable for each disease, and I have illustrated several instruments for you at the end of this chapter.
Make them examples to follow and standards to measure others by, if God wills. Image of a saw:.
