Chapter Seventy-Eight.
On Amputating Limbs and Spreading Bones.
Limb tissues may become gangrenous either due to an external cause or an internal one. If you treat this corruption with medication and the treatment is ineffective, and you see that the decay is spreading throughout the organ without being stopped by anything,.
you should amputate the limb where the corruption has reached to save the patient from death or a greater calamity that is worse than the loss of the limb. A sign of this condition is that the limb turns black until it seems as though fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after blackening until that putrefaction spreads to the adjacent parts of the body and affects the whole body; hence, you should promptly amputate it. The same applies if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some venomous creatures such as the sea scorpion or snake or lizard or the like.
If the corruption or bite is at the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay lest the corruption spreads to the hand. And if it has affected the hand, cut it from the base of the wrist and do not delay until the corruption spreads to the arm's wrist.
And if it has affected the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the corruption spreads further and you see it approaching the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder under any circumstances, for that would lead to the death of the patient; instead, use other treatments as much as possible.
You should do the same for the foot; if the corruption affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of the phalangeal joints. If it affects the instep, amputate the entire foot. If it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has spread above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and resign the patient to death. The method of amputating or incising a limb is to bind a strap below the place you intend to cut and another strap above that place.
One servant extends the lower strap downward, and another servant extends the upper strap upward. You should peel away the flesh between the straps with a broad lancet until all the flesh is exposed, then proceed to cut or incise. You should apply linen cloths from all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place and cause additional pain and hot swelling to the patient, if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some hemostatic agents, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bandage the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I inform you of an example that occurred to a man in his leg, this very condition I am describing to you, which is that there appeared on his leg a blackening accompanied by a burning sensation resembling fire.
That corruption first occurred in his toe until it affected the entire leg. The man amputated his leg himself when he saw the corruption spreading through the limb, along with the intense pain and burning sensation he was experiencing. He cut it at the joint and healed.
After a long time, that same corruption appeared in his index finger. He came to me, and I treated it with medications applied to the hand after cleaning his body, but the corruption did not stop and began to spread to the other fingers.
The corruption spread to his hand, so he asked me to amputate it, but I refused, hoping to stop the corruption. I also feared for him that cutting off his hand might lead to death since the man's condition was on the decline. When he despaired of me, he returned to his hometown.
I was informed that he amputated his hand entirely and healed. I relate this story to assist in dealing with cases of this disease and to provide a reference for guidance and action.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medicines.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling extends until it gathers pus, then it bursts or breaks and all the pus inside comes out, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin on it is like a cloth that has become thin and was not so corrupted as to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament. From this, it deserves to be called an abscess and would not be named a fistula unless its duration extends until the corruption affects any of these organs.
Then it is called a fistula or sinus. Its treatment is to cut all of that skin around it, especially if it has become thin and like a cloth, and you are sure that it will not adhere to the area due to its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and the corruption has not reached that extent, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This means you should examine the abscess; if it is large and the pus coming from it has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
It is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and zincir in equal parts, combined in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine curdles and becomes as thick as honey. Then take what you need, dilute it with water and honey, inject it into the abscess, close its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract it at sunset. Do this for days until the wound is clean and the stench disappears. It may be irrigated with lye water if this medicine is not available, which is to take the ashes of vine wood or oak wood and pour water over it.
Then filter it and irrigate with it until you are certain that the wound has been cleaned. If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or lye water, then irrigate it with a mixture of water and honey in which some powdered zanjabeel has been mixed.
Or irrigate it with a mixture of honey and wine, for honey has the property of cleansing and washing, and wine adheres to the wound, especially if the wine has some astringent and drying qualities. If you do this several times and are certain that the wound is no longer corrupted, then irrigate it with something that promotes flesh growth.
For example, take some of the dough ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine, or irrigate it with other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the wound is narrow and does not accommodate the irrigation tube.
Then widen it slightly with iron or place a wick dipped in Egyptian ointment or sericium ointment until it widens. Similarly, if the opening is also wide, then bring its edges together with sutures and leave enough space for the irrigation tube to enter without excess.
And if the opening from which pus flows is raised upward, then cut it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downward. For if the pus accumulates in the depths of the wound, it prevents flesh from growing there.
And if you cannot make an incision in the abscess as desired towards its lower part, arrange the limb in a position where pus can flow out easily according to what is feasible for you, ensuring that no pus remains in it at all.
And as for the adhesive medications that are applied to the abscess from the outside and how to apply them, it is to take one of the adhesive ointments similar to what you desire and spread it on a piece of linen cloth, and let the cloth be the size needed to cover the entire abscess. Then, with scissors, cut a hole opposite the mouth of the abscess, slightly wider than the mouth of the abscess. Then spread one of the soft ointments on another piece of cloth according to the size of the hole and place it over it, spaced apart from the mouth of the abscess to facilitate the outflow of pus. Do not remove the large cloth until afterwards.
For several days, and as for the one on the mouth of the abscess, it is to be removed every time to let out what has accumulated in it of pus and to know how much or how little, or whether what comes out of it is ripe or not.
And also, examine the site of the abscess itself to see if its owner feels pain there or not, and whether there is swelling or no swelling. If your treatment continues this way and you see that the material coming out is minimal at the mouth of the abscess.
Know then that the abscess has adhered or is close to adhering. In this case, release it the next day or on the third day. Whenever you release the wound, replace the cloth and renew the ointment. If thin pus comes out of the abscess after a long period, do not despair of its adhesion.
Its healing is swift, for there are those among people in whom the growth of flesh in their surgery is slow, and there are others to the contrary. If after many days pus that is not ripe comes out from the wound, know that the wound has not adhered; if the adhesion of the wound is delayed.
If his condition persists, increase the drying of the medications. The medicines should be moist in their consistency but dry in their strength, like the palm ointment if an excess amount of colocynth has been added. What is most effective for treatment is to take the palm ointment.
It can be treated with rose oil, and sprinkle on it a moderate decoction that is balanced in its consistency. Then knead it with it and use it. This can also be done if the palm ointment is not available: take honey, boil it until it becomes irritating, and use it. Or you can take pepper, aconite, and costus, grind them all together, and sprinkle the powder on the honey while it is over fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it over the wound, or apply the honey that you boiled until it thickened, sprinkle the medicines on it, place the cloth over it, and bind it tight.
Zaranj, long susan root, asmonian lily base, carrot seed flour, and hellebore can be used individually or in combination. Grind them, sieve them, and sprinkle the powder on the honey described and use it. If the wound persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption deep within it.
If it affects the bones or other solid nerves or ligaments, treat it as you would treat a fistula as previously described.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On the treatment of corns and ingrown nails and the amputation of excess fingers and the splitting of webbed fingers.
A corn is an excess of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or toe and may grow on other fingers as well. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot swelling.
Or it becomes infected until it eats away the root of the nail and may spoil it entirely, and the infection might reach the bone causing a foul smell, and the tip of the finger becomes widened with a bruised color. If you treat it with what we mentioned in the section on incision and your treatment does not work.
You should cut with iron all the excess that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound. Cauterization is very beneficial in this case. If the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy.
And if the corner of the nail has pressed into the flesh, causing it to become inflamed and painful, you should place a thin wedge under the corner of the nail that is pressing into the flesh, lift it up, and gently remove that part of the flesh.
You should apply caustic and corrosive medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all removed, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the infection has affected the bone, you should cut out and remove that bone, for it will not heal as long as there is infected bone present.
If you see erosion and decay spreading in the finger, cut it at one of the joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is hit or crushed and severe pain occurs, it should be slit open first.
Then, cut the nail with a sharp instrument diagonally from top to bottom and be careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail with your cut, for that will cause severe pain to the patient and may lead to excessive flesh growth in the area. Then treat the area with what soothes the pain.
As for the extra finger that develops in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in part of it, and it might also have a nail. Its growth often occurs at the base joint of one of the fingers.
Or its growth may occur in some joints of the finger. Those that grow in some joints of the finger do not move, while those that grow at the joint of the finger might move. What is entirely fleshy is easy to cut; you should cut it at its base with a broad instrument.
As for those that grow at the base of the joint, their treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting them. As for those that grow on the finger at one of the joints, you should first cut its flesh in a circular manner down to the bone, then use one of those appropriate saws to remove the bone.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the webbing that occurs between fingers, this often happens and can be something a person is born with or can occur after the healing of a wound or a burn from fire or similar. You should slit open this webbing.
Until the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them felt or patches soaked in rose oil so that it does not heal quickly and separate them, or put a thin sheet of lead between them until the area heals properly.
And if the fingers adhere to the palm, then cut that adhesion according to what seems appropriate and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On Cutting Boils.
Varicose veins are twisted, thick vessels filled with black superfluous blood; they occur in most parts of the body but more frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of farmers, carpenters, and carriers. Firstly, it is appropriate to use friction on the body with black salt several times with strong friction.
Then, let the patient fast for a while. As for treating it with iron, it is done in two ways: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other is to extract the vein entirely. If you are going to cut it, first wash the leg thoroughly with hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves, then bind the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a cloth, then cut the vein in one place or in two places or in three wide cuts.
Then, squeeze the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards and from above downwards until the amount of blood that you deem sufficient or what the patient's strength can tolerate is removed. Then bind it and order him to avoid foods that generate black bile, and repeat vomiting and bleeding.
When the veins are filled to the point that it harms the patient, as for sweating, it should be done in this manner: shave the leg of the patient if there is a lot of hair, then immerse him in the bath and wash his leg with hot water until it reddens and sweat pours out, or he exercises vigorously.
If a bath is not available, warm the limb, then make an incision in the skin opposite the vein longitudinally, either at its end near the knee or at its bottom near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and peel off the vein from all sides until it becomes apparent to touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and prominent. Once freed from the skin, it resembles a tendon. Then insert a thread under it until it is raised and protrudes from the skin, then secure it with a smooth, blind clamp. Then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers wide.
Then peel the skin off the vein until it appears, then raise it with thread as you did before and secure it with another clamp as you did first. Then make another incision or several incisions if needed.
Then sweat it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it out and sweat it until it comes out from the second incision. Then pull it to the upper incision and do this until you pull it out from the third incision, the highest of all, until it is completely out, then cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and sweating, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Insert thread under it and twist it with your hand in all directions until it comes out, being careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, extracting it will be very difficult for you.
It is harmful to the patient, so if you remove it all, place on the site of the wounds wool soaked in a drink of rose oil or olive oil and treat it until it heals. If the hernia is contracted with folds that twist in various directions.
And if it is not straight as we said, it should be cut at every direction of its folds and places where it twists, then hang it with clips until you remove all of it. Be careful when cutting not to sever or injure the vein, for it will be difficult for you to remove it, so be cautious in your efforts.
Image of the instrument with which you cut the hernia:.
Image of the blind forceps:.
It should not have a curvature like other forceps and the ends should not be sharp so as not to injure the vein. It should be thick and smooth in its bend because if it is thin, it will cut the vein with its thinness. Rather, it should have the advantage of thickness as we said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
On the removal of the civic vein.
This vein develops in the legs in hot regions such as Hijaz and the lands of Arabs, and in hot, dry, less fertile countries. It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to corruption that occurs under the skin.
It occurs inside the bodies as worms and other parasites, and like the maggots that develop between the skin and the flesh. The sign of the beginning of this vein is that there is a severe inflammation in the leg, then the area suppurates, and then the vein begins to emerge from where the suppuration occurred.
It is as if it were the root of a plant or animal, and when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams, tied up, and the lead left hanging from the stalk.
Every time something comes out from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut part of it and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it from its root before all of it has come out because if you cut it, it will retract into the flesh and cause a tumor and putrefaction in the area and an ugly ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and drawn out little by little until it is completely out and none remains in the body. Sometimes from this duct there may come out in some people something that can be five or ten cubits long.
I have been told that for a man, it extended to twenty cubits. If it breaks while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole and pack it with a long plug along the body until all its material is discharged, and try to clear any putrefaction in the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This duct may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing it to have many openings and a branch coming out of each mouth. Treat it as we mentioned in the division and with what has been previously stated.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On incising the worm generated under the jaw, which is called the cattle disease.
This disease is called in some countries among us the cattle ailment because it often afflicts cattle; it is a small single worm that breeds between the skin and the flesh and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It becomes apparent to the sense when it moves from one organ to another until it breaks through the skin where it penetrates, forming due to the putrefaction of some mixtures, as is common with worms and snakes and melon seeds in the abdomen.
It is anticipated that her harm occurs when she moves within the body, rises to the head, and reaches the eye; she may open it and come out, thus destroying the eye, which happens frequently. If you wish to treat her and remove her, it can only be done when she moves and becomes apparent to the senses.
It is necessary to bind what is above and below her tightly with a band, then cut over her and remove her. If she sinks into the flesh and you do not find her, apply cauterization with fire to the area until you burn her. The greatest damage she can cause is to the eye, as we have said.
If you see her in the head near the eye, bind tightly below her on the forehead, then cut over her and remove her. The patient should be treated by purifying the body with laxative medicines that clear the putrefied mixtures and by avoiding foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On Incising the Disease Known as Nafir.
This disease which is called in our country naffir is a pain that occurs in some organs then moves from one organ to another and I have seen it as I describe it to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some villages. She uncovered her arm and showed me slight swelling in the vein of the arm. After staying for an hour, I saw that swelling moving towards the elbow like a worm climbs, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
If it flows from one place to another, the pain leaves that place and settles in the shoulder. Then, after sitting for an hour, it spreads throughout the body until it reaches the other arm. She told me that it moves through her entire body as I witnessed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one organ to another, and I had not seen this disease with my own eyes in such a manner before. However, I have seen groups of people who feel pain moving from one organ to another, but I have not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I could only attribute this to the fact that the woman was from the countryside, with a dry body and visible veins. This is why such movement was perceptible, whereas it would not be so apparent in people of affluence with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, and if it appears visibly as we mentioned, then bind above and below it tightly, incise it until that congested air comes out, and cauterize the area.
And if you do not see it with your eye, treat it with body powdering and what expels gases and releases them, such as stinking seed and spikenard seed and the like of medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places where they enter the body. As for their difference according to their types, some are large and some are small, and among them are hollow glass arrows and solid glass arrows, and there are those that have three angles and four angles.
And there are those that have barbs and those that have splinters. As for those that occur according to the organs they enter, they are of two types: either the organs are major hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. When an arrow falls into one of these organs and signs of death, which I will describe to you later, become apparent, you should avoid extracting that arrow, for death usually follows the owner of such a condition. And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of an arrow piercing the brain, penetrating the bone, and injuring the membrane covering the brain is severe headache, fainting, and dizziness.
And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of an arrow piercing the brain, penetrating the bone, and injuring the membrane covering the brain is severe headache, fainting, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes, a red tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, and sometimes blood coming from the nostrils or ears, speech becoming interrupted, loss of voice, and a white moist substance resembling porridge oozing from the site of the wound, similar to the fluid of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, stop treating the patient and do not remove the arrow if it has not been removed. As for the signs of the arrow when it affects the heart and is close to the left breast, he feels as though it has embedded into something solid rather than something hollow.
And perhaps the arrow may move in a way that resembles the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and loss of consciousness; then know that death is inevitable. The sign of the arrow if it wounds the lung is the流出伤口的血呈泡沫状。
And the vessels near the neck swell, the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking to inhale cool air. If the arrow reaches the membrane in the chest, it is close to the lower ribs, and breathing becomes labored with severe pain.
He sighs, and all the parts of the shoulders move. If the arrow reaches the liver, severe pain follows, and blood that resembles the color of the liver comes out of the wound. If the arrow reaches the stomach, undigested food may come out of the wound.
His condition is evident. If the arrow pierces the abdomen and protrudes, and feces or pus or intestines come out of the wound, there is no hope for treatment or removing the arrow. If the arrow reaches the bladder and urine comes out.
And if something protrudes from the wound and the pain intensifies for the patient, then know that he is doomed. As for other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar parts, they are often spared.
When the arrow has not encountered a vein or nerve and is not poisoned, and I inform you of some of what I have witnessed regarding these arrows so that you may infer from it your treatment, which is that an arrow had struck a man in the nasal root near his eye socket.
I extracted it from the other side under the earlobe and he recovered without any harm to his eye. I also extracted another arrow for a Jew that had struck below his lower eyelid into the eye socket, where the arrow was concealed and I only reached its small end that attaches to the wooden shaft.
It was a large composite arrow with a smooth, square iron head without barbs, and the Jew recovered without any mishap to his eye. I also extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and this arrow was Arabic.
It had barbs, so I made an incision between his jaws as it had penetrated his throat. I gently manipulated it until I extracted it, and the Christian was saved and recovered. I also extracted another arrow for a man who had been struck in his abdomen, and we feared he would die from it.
After he remained for about thirty days without any change in his condition, I made an incision to reach the arrow and carefully extracted it. He recovered without any complications. I also treated a man who had been struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound had healed around the arrow.
After seven years, the arrow emerged at the base of her thigh. I also treated a woman who had an arrow in her abdomen; the wound had healed around it, and the arrow remained without any change in her condition or causing any harm to her natural functions.
I saw another man who had been struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound had healed, leaving him with little pain, and there are many similar cases. I extracted an arrow from a commander of the sultan's army; it had penetrated the middle of his nose, slightly leaning to the right side.
The arrow had entirely disappeared, and I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound extremely narrow, so I probed it with a thin probe but could not feel anything. He was experiencing swelling and pain under his ear on the right side.
I suspected that the swelling might be due to the arrow's tip, so I dressed the area with a bandage containing a drawing force and left it, hoping that the area would swell and reveal the arrow's mark. However, nothing happened at the site to indicate that the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued to apply the bandage for many days, but nothing happened. The wound eventually closed during that time, and the patient despaired of its extraction for a long period until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me. I then applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened up, and I probed it, feeling the thin tip of the arrow that was embedded in the bone. Then I continued to widen the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow became visible to us. It took me about four months to do so.
Then, when the wound widened and allowed me to insert forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to being removed. I continued to manipulate it and try various tools until one day I grasped it firmly with a pair of forceps, as depicted at the end of this chapter.
And the doctors ruled that his nasal cartilage would not set, but I set it and the wound healed and the patient recovered completely, without any harm at all. I inform you of the method of extracting some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor can a book encompass it. Rather, the skilled craftsman measures the little with the much and what is present with what is absent, and deduces new work and new tools for strange occurrences.
If I speak of this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the parts in which they have penetrated in two ways: either by pulling them out from the place where they entered or from the opposite side, and those that are extracted come out from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy area, it should be pulled out. If it does not come out at the time it was embedded, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, making it easier to pull out. Similarly, if it has lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for days and attempt to pull and move it every day until it comes out. If it still does not come out after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from all sides with a fine drill until you widen the area for the arrow, then pull it out.
If the arrow has penetrated into one of the ventricles of the brain and some of those symptoms I have mentioned appear in the patient, refrain from pulling out the arrow and leave it until the condition improves after days.
Be careful in pulling out the arrow, for if it is deeply embedded and does not yield to pulling, use drills around the arrow as described to you, then treat the area until it heals. If the arrow has become hidden in a part of the body. And if it is not felt, probe for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some tools suitable for pulling. If you cannot do so due to the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in that place.
Make an incision to widen the wound and gain control of the arrow until you can remove it. If it has two wings, grasp them and free the flesh embedded in them from all sides with any means possible. Be cautious if you cannot free the flesh by breaking and twisting the wings to release it.
And if you attempt to remove the arrow from any location, use your fingers to twist it in all directions until you free it, being as gentle as possible so that the arrow does not break, which would make it difficult for you to pull and extract it. If you cannot manage it at the time.
Leave it for days until the flesh around it becomes putrefied, then try again as it will be easier at that time. If bleeding obstructs you, use the treatments we have mentioned. Be careful not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon and use all your ingenuity.
In every way, you can extract the arrowhead, and it should be done with gentleness, patience, and steadiness as I have described to you. It is advisable that when you pull out the arrow, you position the patient in the same manner he was in when the arrow struck him, for this is most appropriate.
If you cannot do that, use whatever shapes you can. As for the arrow that exits from the opposite side, either some part of it has protruded outward or you will find the tip of the arrow by touch above the skin nearby and you will see it protruding.
Make an incision over it, and let the incision be large enough to accommodate forceps. Then pull it out, for it will come out easily. If it gets stuck in the bone, rotate your hand around until the arrow affects the bone and widens its path, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for days then return to it until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is in it, push it out with it. If the shaft has fallen and you want to use pushing, insert a hollow instrument into it so that its hollow part enters the tail of the arrow, then push it out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that can enter that hollow part, for this will make the arrow come out easily. If the arrow is poisoned, you should excise all the flesh into which the poison has penetrated if you can, then treat it with what is appropriate for such a condition.
If the arrow is in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank and is close enough to be examined with a probe and you can make an incision over it, then do so. Be careful not to cut any artery or nerve, and remove it.
Then draw the incision if it needs suturing, then treat it until it heals, image of the forceps which you use to extract arrows:.
Its ends are like a bird's beak, made as if it were a file; when it grasps the arrow or anything else, it does not let go. Varieties of large, small, and medium sizes can be made according to the size of the arrow and the width and narrowness of the wound.

Chapter Ninety-Five.
On bloodletting from veins.
The veins that are commonly bled from in the body are thirty veins, including sixteen veins in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the hassasin, and the two visible arteries on the temples, and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the nazeerin.
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the two veins on the sides of the neck, the two veins on the upper lip, and the two veins on the lower lip, these known as al-jaharik, and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are bled from in the arm and hand, they are five veins. One of them is the cephalic vein, which is on the ulnar side, and the common people call it the head vein. The median vein, known as the axillary vein, is composed of branches of the basilic and cephalic veins, and the common people call it the body vein.
And the basilic vein, which is located on the inner side of the arm and is also called the axillary; the common people call it the abdominal sweat or the arm rope. And the cubital vein, which is located above the elbow joint, to which bloodletting adhesions occur, and it clearly appears above the thumb. And theasilim.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, which has two branches; in the leg and foot, there are three veins: one under the patella on the outer side, the second is the saphenous, located at the ankle on the inner side, and the sciatic vein, located at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg, there are three similar veins. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit lies in tying them for chronic catarrhs, migraines, tinea, and chronic bad sores on the head. The method of tying them is as I describe.
It is to shave the head of the patient and rub its back where the two veins are with a coarse cloth well. Then, have the patient constrict his neck with a turban until the veins appear and their location behind the ears in the two lower areas of the head. Grasp them with your finger where you feel their pulse under your finger, and mark that spot with ink.
Then take a lancet, which is known as the nashl. Insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut off the vein along with the skin in one amputated piece. The length of the cut should be about two fingers clasped together.
Or the amount of blood to be let is determined, then they are tied with bandages until healed; their cutting and cauterization was mentioned earlier in the book. As for the two arteries in the temples, their compression is beneficial for chronic migraine, difficult headache, persistent inflammation, and acute tearing discharge directed towards the eyes.
And the way to compress them as I describe to you is for the patient to tighten his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly, and their pulse becomes evident under your finger. Then mark with ink, and then lift the skin above the vein slightly with your index finger.
You insert the scalpel from below, lift the vein upwards, and cut it as you did with the other veins. Let blood according to your need, then loosen the patient's neck and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then place cotton and a bandage on it and tightly secure it from above, leaving it until healed. Their use and cutting was mentioned earlier in the book. As for compressing the forehead vein, its benefit is after compressing the temporal veins for chronic facial ailments such as psoriasis, sores, and thick redness.
And the way to compress it as I describe to you is for the patient to tighten his neck with a turban until the vein appears. Then take the tool called the axe, and this is its image:.
Place the protruding spike at the end of the axe on the vein and strike from above with a comb or something similar. Let the blood flow in the amount you desire, then loosen the patient's neck and bind it until healed.
And it may be opened with a broad lancet, except that the lancet should not be sharp-pointed like other lancets, but rather should have a slightly wide point, and it is sharpened obliquely because the bone is close, so the lancet might break if it were thin.
As for the two veins at the inner corners of the eyes, their benefit is for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, weeping, and facial diseases. As for how to lancet them, the patient should tighten his neck with a headscarf, then you lance them while standing at his head, and let the lancing be oblique towards length slightly with a small, somewhat broad lancet.
For if there is no flesh in the area, then if the lancet is thin at the tip it might break. Then let out as much blood as you need, put cotton on them, and bind them for one night, then remove the bindings. As for lancing the nasal vein, it is beneficial for acute fever.
And severe headache and facial diseases such as the red splay that appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic. The way to lance it is to have the patient tighten his neck, then hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin long lancet.
And pierce it in the center of the nasal tip itself between the nostrils straight because the vein does not appear there to the touch, then blood will flow immediately. You should keep your hand with the lancet steady for a little while, let out as much blood as you need, then bind it for one night, for it heals quickly.
As for the cheeks, lancing them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and black ailments that appear on the surface of the skin, such as black leprosy, smallpox, and bad sores and ulcers. The way to lance them is to have the patient tighten below them in his neck with a bandage, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
The patient sits on a chair, then the vein is incised lengthwise with a relatively wide incision. Then, let blood flow until a moderate amount has been released or as much as you deem necessary for the condition. Do the same to the other vein and then loosen the bandage.
The two veins are tied moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and leave it until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the jaw, their incision after the temple’s vein is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, decay of the gums, bad sores, splitting of the lips, and severe sores.
Those that are in the nose and around it, and how to incise them: have the patient sit in front of you and tie his neck with a turban, then pull his lips apart and look at the two veins which you see one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, identifying them by their darkness.
Also, around them are thin black veins which you should cut off short. If it is difficult for you and you do not know which they are, aim to cut the larger and clearer ones. Do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, their incision after the temple’s vein is beneficial for tumors in the throat due to scrofula and mouth diseases. To incise them, have the patient sit before you facing the sun, lift his tongue, and look underneath it where you will find one vein on one side and another on the other side, both of a dark color.
Incise them but be careful not to cut deeply as there are arteries underneath, and excessive cutting might cause bleeding from those arteries. As for the three veins that are incised in the elbow, these are the ones commonly incised in all people, and their incision is done in two ways.
Either with a wide, basil-like scalpel or olive-shaped to thinness, or by incision with a knife-like scalpel, which is the nishl [and this is its image], the image of the wide basil-like scalpel:.
It should be wide as you see, suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, visible vessels that contain thick blood like tar, and this is the image of the olive-shaped scalpel:.
This scalpel is narrower and has a sharper tip, suitable for incising thin vessels that contain thin, bile-like blood, and this is the image of the nishl scalpel:.
This nishl, which is suitable for incision, comes in various types, wide and thin, according to the width and narrowness of the vessels. This can be inferred for other uses as well, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic, which is one of these three vessels, the benefit of incising it is that it draws blood from ailments that are under the throat and neck, towards the chest and abdomen. The person performing the incision should be cautious and hold steady, for beneath it are arteries; if he errs and advances the scalpel too deeply, he may cut those arteries.
Bleeding may occur, so it is advisable not to puncture it with a stitching scalpel but to incise it with the nishl. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, it should be avoided and another vessel sought or one of its branches should be found.
Or incise the arm tendon in its place, as it is distinct and should be cut with the nishl scalpel as mentioned. If you wish to incise it specifically, before stretching the arm, you should palpate the area until you identify the pulse location, then mark it with ink.
Then bind the arm and make a transverse incision along the vein with the lancet as we said, and be careful that the cut falls away from the position of the artery. And when you see swelling in the place where you marked with ink while tightening the bandage, know that this swelling is the distention of the artery; so avoid it.
If you see the blood when bleeding spurting as a boy's urine would spurt and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery. In this case, quickly place your finger on it for a long moment, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, it often stops; then tighten the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it. It should be on the neck and not moved for days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and overpowers you and there is no medicine at hand, then cut the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take the shell of a pistachio, split it, and take one half. Bind it tightly to the site of the vein with bandages and ligatures until the next day. If the blood stops, otherwise treat it as previously mentioned by applying hemostatic agents. Stopping its flow is not difficult in most cases.
This is due to the small size of the wound and the effectiveness of the bandage on the arm, so be aware. As for the blue vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower body because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic as we said.
It is necessary for the operator to be on the neck of the one being circumcised, for underneath are nerves. If he increases in piercing with the lancet and touches the nerve bundle, numbness occurs which is difficult to heal, and sometimes does not heal at all, and this nerve bundle often becomes noticeable by sensation.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not noticeable, then you should make his circumcision a split with the knife and avoid the nerve bundle as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerve bundles, then cut the vein longitudinally. As for the temporal vein, the benefit of its circumcision is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein especially, if you want to circumcise it; you should use an olive-shaped lancet or a broad basil-shaped lancet for stitching because it is safer than all other veins as there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet when circumcising and seek a soft spot. It does no harm if it is not hit in the first attempt and circumcision is repeated, except that it may swell in some people if it is not cut in the first attempt.
But that swelling does not harm it at all. As for the manner of circumcision and its consequences, and what should precede its correction, the first thing you should know is that circumcision is either used solely to preserve health.
Or it is used for diseases. What is used to preserve and sustain health and to prevent the occurrence of diseases is that circumcision be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the temporal, the ulnar, and the brachial, and that circumcision be done at the beginning of spring when the signs of fullness appear.
Bleeding should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since daybreak. As for bleeding that is used for treating illnesses, it does not have a specific time limit but rather is performed whenever necessity and need arise, whether at night or during the day.
And at every hour and at all times, children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have exceeded sixty years be bled. When anyone intends to bleed for any reason, it is as such.
It is advisable to cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a mild enema if there is a large amount of retained feces, so that impurities from the intestines do not enter the blood vessels during bleeding and harm the vital organs. One should not bleed someone who is pregnant, drunk, or intoxicated until those conditions have passed.
Bleeding should also be avoided after sexual intercourse, vomiting, defecation, excessive sexual activity, fatigue, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and anything that weakens the body or mind. Then, one should consider thinning the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
Through foods, drinks, and medications if possible, then he should take a bath if there is no impediment or exercise somewhat to thin the blood. His bleeding should be in the middle of the day as mentioned, and he should aim to clear his mind that day from all bad mental disturbances.
Such as worries, anger, fear, and all physical disturbances like excessive fatigue, overexertion, sexual intercourse, and the like. The things that people are accustomed to using, such as perfumes, fragrances, and entertainment, should be present according to each person's ability.
Then the surgeon sits on a pillow that is higher than the pillow on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and the surgeon scratches it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with the clamp and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
Because when the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is too loose it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after tightening, the patient should rub both hands together.
Until the veins swell and become visible to the touch, then the surgeon should wipe the lancet with a little of old oil, particularly, then he places his left index finger on the same vein under the spot where he intends to cut slightly so that the vein does not escape and avoids the strike. For there are veins that are like tendons and escape when cut, and some are filled with air so that when the lancet is placed on them, they lower under the lancet and deceive the surgeon, and the lancet does not open the vein; even if it opens it, the opening would be narrow.
Therefore, the surgeon should be certain and take his time with all these matters, then he should lower the lancet; if it opens the vein on that attempt, otherwise he should repeat it slightly below or above that spot quickly if the area does not swell.
If it swells or the patient is distressed, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, as it may cause a hot swelling and he should not enter the bathhouse. Then, if desired, the bloodletting can be repeated, but if the lancet penetrates slightly and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin.
I fear that the amount of blood you wish to let will not come out, so prepare the lancet in the same hole and be gentle on straightness, and increase the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the area swells, for in many people the area may swell at even a small incision.
If you see that it has swollen, do not return to it at all, for it will not help you. Place something with vinegar on it, as it facilitates blood flow and is better in this case than oil itself and other ointments.
Use vinegar in all your bloodletting from veins when the flow of blood is difficult. The Farroq antidote and schizonepetalum can also achieve this if applied to the area; the blood will become thinner and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of bloodletting, it often happens, especially for those who have not been bled before or if the vein was cut too small. Act quickly and place a sponge soaked in warm salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, as it will dissipate.
You should do this after the blood has fully come out from the vein itself or another vein. If there remains any blackness or greenness in the area after a few days, it will not cause harm. If you wish, apply some dissolved aloes and bitterness to it.
Or apply some juice of fuddlej or the like. Swelling and nodules often occur after bleeding the basilic vein, so place your hand on it. If you find it softens when you press it, then it is a bad nodule; be careful not to apply anything we mentioned to it.
For sometimes blood from an artery may flow from it, but dress it with what constricts to solidify the area, then treat him with all treatments until he recovers. It is appropriate to let out blood from every person according to his strength and what appears dominant in the color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red. Similarly, if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin. Likewise, if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides. It is appropriate for one who is full and strong and needs to have blood drawn all at once.
One should widen the bloodletting vein and the lancet should be wide. For those who are weak, it is the opposite. It is appropriate to draw the blood in several instances and for the hole to be narrow. The best method for bloodletting is to make a slanted, twisted cut, not stitched.
This method of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and from severing a nerve, and it is better and more secure than bloodletting across the width or length. For those who faint during bloodletting, it is appropriate to give them something to eat before bloodletting, such as bread soaked in pomegranate juice or honey.
If he is feverish, let the blood in three or four times. If his temperament is cold, let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in hyacinth drink or in fragranced honey drink with afavieh or in a good-smelling herbaceous drink.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause is excessive loss of blood, it is appropriate to give him meat broth and a light herbaceous drink, use fragrant camphor on him, and rub his chest with it. Use all that we have mentioned in the section on fainting.
That which results from bloodletting, and as for he who wishes to refresh his arm and release blood again, it is suitable for him whose vein was opened for much bloodletting and whose strength is weak that the blood be released little by little according to his strength over consecutive days.
And as for he who wishes to refresh his arm and release blood again and whose body is strong, let him do so after seven or nine hours from the first opening. And as for he who wants to attract blood from his body to the opposite side to which it has leaned, it is suitable that he be refreshed on the second or third day.
And as for he in whom the blood is abundant, having become hot and intense and caused fever, it is suitable that the blood be removed all at once and a large quantity be taken out and the incision widened until fainting occurs after you have attended to all the conditions of bloodletting. And place your hand on his pulse while the blood is flowing to prevent death instead of fainting, for that often happens if the bloodletter is ignorant and neglect occurs. And it is not suitable when you want to release the arm and let blood again.
And if the vein's mouth has closed and it is difficult for blood to come out, do not poke it forcefully or twist strongly, as that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you bleed him again, or use the scalpel blade to remove what of the blood has congealed in the vein's mouth.
Or apply to it some salt dissolved in water, or apply to it some Farouk antidote or chickpea, and poke it gently until the blood flows. If the vein has become swollen, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides, but if necessity calls for bleeding again and it is imperative, then either incise above that place or incise in the other arm or in another vein.
As for incising the arm vein, it is used instead of the basilic and cephalic veins if they are not found or are hidden, as it is composed of both. The method of incision is to have the patient immerse their hand in hot water until the joint turns red and the vein becomes clearly visible, then bind above it slightly with a moderate tightness.
Then incise the vein slightly obliquely, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the cut be wide. Your incision should be slightly above the wrist joint. If the blood does not flow out easily, immerse the hand again in a container of hot water and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Until you reach the amount you need. If you are in the summer days, you may not need to reimmerse the hand in hot water. Most often, making the blood flow into hot water is done in winter. Incising this vein is safer than all other veins. Because there is no harmful vein or nerve beneath it. As for incising the basilic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of incision is to bind the wrist with a bandage or hold it with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no harmful vein or nerve beneath it. As for incising the basilic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of incision is to bind the wrist with a bandage or hold it with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. It then becomes very apparent to the touch. Incise it slightly obliquely, and even if you completely cut it off, it will not harm it at all. Be careful not to delve too deeply with the lancet as there is the nerve of the fingers beneath it, and the area is devoid of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave the blood to flow through it, for if you do not immerse it in hot water, the blood will coagulate at the mouth of the vein and prevent its flow. When you have extracted enough blood, apply fat and salt to the vein so that it does not close quickly.
And you should do the same with every narrow branch. As for tying off the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments. Do the same in its ligation as you did with the second one equally. As for ligating the superficial vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body, such as uterus ailments.
And for the retention of menstruation, kidney diseases, chronic leg and calf ulcers, and similar ailments, the method of ligating is to have him immerse his leg in hot water and apply massage until the veins dilate, then bind above the ankle joint with a tourniquet, placing it on the vein located towards the big toe.
And it branches out in the foot into many branches, so ligate it in its widest branch or at the ankle where they converge, which is better and safer. If you ligate it in the front of the foot, be cautious of the nerves beneath it in the sole of the foot.
Make the ligation in a way that it is offset as if you were going to amputate it, and let the incision be oblique. If the blood does not flow out easily, have him immerse his leg in hot water and leave the blood to flow until it is drained. If the ligature misses the vein on the first attempt, try again.
Let him try the ligation a little higher up, for the area is safe and nothing harmful should be feared if you are cautious of the nerve as we said. Do the same with the superficial vein of the other leg equally. As for the sciatic vein, its location is as we have said, at the heel on the outer side.
The benefit of cupping for hip pain if it is due to hot blood, and the method of cupping is that the patient enters the bath and hastens, and ties his leg from the hip to above the instep with four fingers' width using a thin long headband, for it will not be apparent otherwise.
When it appears, cup it in whatever condition you find it, either by displacement which is better, or cut it off entirely or split it open, for its location is healthy. It is very slight in most people, so if you do not find it.
If it does not appear at all to the sense, then cup some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot towards the little toe and the ring toe, and be careful of the nerves, and let out as much blood as you desire.
Then loosen the tie and place cotton on the cupping site, and bind the area tightly, for it will heal quickly.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On cupping and how to use it.
Cupping vessels may be made of horns, wood, copper, or glass. Cupping can be performed in two ways: one is cupping with bleeding and the other is cupping without bleeding.
And this cupping without bleeding can be performed in two ways.
The leeches for the two cheeks, which are both sides of the neck; the leeches for the chin, which is below the lower jaw of the mouth; the leeches for the shoulders; the leeches for the heels at the end of the buttocks; the leeches for the hips, which are the middle of the arms; the leeches for the legs; the leeches for the calves.
Cupping only attracts blood from the thin veins spread in the flesh, and for this reason, it does not weaken as bleeding does. It is not appropriate to use cupping, whether with fire or without, in any of the diseases that result from fullness until the body has been completely purged.
If there is a need for cupping due to illness or as a routine practice, we use it at any time, whether at the beginning of the month, at the end, in the middle, or at any other time.
This is because there are people for whom when the blood increases to the point where it needs to be released through cupping, they feel heaviness and headache in their head. Some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Others feel itching on their face and sides, and darkness and irritation in their eyes. There are also those who scratch the area that was cupped.
Some laugh excessively, some find the taste of blood in their mouth and their gums bleed, spitting out blood. Others sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, killings, wounds, and similar things in their dreams. Whenever we observe any of these symptoms...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we recommend cupping after two or three hours have passed since noon. As for the benefit of spot cupping, it helps with heaviness in the head and fluid running into the eyes.
But it should be done after completely purging the body. This cupping can substitute for bloodletting from the temple. It is warned against using it by one who has a cold brain or has had an apoplexy, as it would greatly harm him.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly and those who have cold diseases in their heads, and those who are addicted to it may develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, it is advisable to instruct the cupper to lower the cup slightly downward to avoid causing forgetfulness. As for the cupping of the shoulder area, it can substitute for bloodletting from the pterygoid and basilic veins.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, respiratory distress, coughing, and fullness. The cupping of the shoulder area should be slightly raised because if it is placed lower, it may cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for the cupping of the axillae, it is beneficial for headaches, eye diseases, migraines, throat constriction, and toothache at the roots of the teeth. It can substitute for bloodletting from the basilic vein. You should instruct the cupper not to insert his hand deeply with the lancet so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for the cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for mouth ulcers, gum diseases, and other similar ailments in the mouth. It can substitute for bloodletting from the veins in the lips. As for the cupping of the shoulders, it is helpful for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for the cupping of the two sides below the ribs, it is beneficial for what bloodletting from the three veins—basilic, pterygoid, and temporal—can treat, because it draws blood from all those thin veins in the flesh, which in turn draw from thicker veins until the suction reaches the three major veins.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper not to be excessive in cupping because the area is devoid of flesh and beneath it are nerves and blood vessels. As for the single cupping which is performed on the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the rectum and sores below.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper that the cup should be large and made of brass because the area requires strong suction and perhaps a glass cup might break and require much cupping. As for the cups on the legs, they notably reduce swelling.
Because it attracts blood from all over the body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, it induces menstruation and helps with boils and abscesses, and serves as a substitute for bloodletting from the veins and arteries. However, it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. The benefit of cupping the veins is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of placing the cups is to first place the cup empty and suction moderately. Do not keep the cups in place for long; instead, place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the mixtures can be evenly applied to the area. Continue repeating this process until you see the area turn red and open up.
When the redness of the blood appears, then you should perform cupping and gradually suction again. Then observe the condition of the bodies; for those whose flesh is delicate and pores are loose, it is advisable to perform cupping once only so that the area does not become ulcerated.
You should instruct the cupper to widen the incision slightly and deepen it a little, and to adjust the suction gently with a delicate movement. If there is thickness in the blood, it is advisable to perform cupping twice: in the first instance, to open a path for the thin blood and its fluid, and in the second, to thoroughly extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, a third leeching should be repeated to reach the limit. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one leeching suffices; if we wish to remove more blood, we use more leechings. If we determine that the blood is thick.
It is necessary to perform a deep bloodletting, and the moderate limit for bloodletting is only the depth of the skin. What should be used with cupping are ointments, water, and what the person undergoing cupping should avoid. As for those whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and has narrow pores.
It is advisable to anoint the places for cupping with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments. If it is summer, then use ointments like that of khirya, or violet ointment, or sweet almond ointment, or pumpkin seed ointment.
If it is winter, then use ointments like narcissus ointment, lily ointment, chamomile ointment, or colchicum ointment, and the like. If the humors are thick and cold, then the ointment should be mastic ointment, sparrow ointment, cow ointment, cyperus ointment, or similar.
If the person undergoing cupping has wide pores and tender flesh, it is advisable to avoid using ointments. These individuals should wash the cupping marks after cupping with rose water or cold water or wolf grape water or pumpkin water or senna water, and the like. As for those whose blood is very moist.
They should wash the cupping marks with vinegar or water of sage and buckthorn, and the like. As for those whose humors are thick, they should wash the cupping marks with old wine or mastic water or cooked cyperus or chamomile, and the like. It is advisable to avoid cupping in the bathhouse and immediately after bathing.
It should be used after exiting the bath by one or two hours and it is not appropriate for anyone to sleep after cupping. It is appropriate to manage the cupped and bled person before and after cupping.
You should first look if the cupped or bled person is bilious and if his blood tends to be sharp and inflamed, then it is appropriate for him to take cooling things like pomegranate, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, sage, and sherbet, and the like. His foods should be light dishes, sheep meat soups, stews, and similar items.
And if his temperament is cold, it is appropriate to give him honey syrup or sage seed drink and he should take moderate aromatic wine that is between old and new. He should be advised to eat less and his food should be light dishes, quail, skylarks, and young pigeon stews. It is appropriate for the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding to be more than food.
It may be appropriate to give some people in certain times the Farouq antidote or musk medicine or saltha before cupping and bleeding or after it to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood. It should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for the cupping that is done without scarification, it is the cupping placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and hip joint because these organs cannot tolerate scarification. It is intended either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Like placing the cups on the breasts for the condition of nasal congestion, or using them to expel a cold air that has settled in an organ, such as placing the cups on the abdomen and navel. This loosens the organ, warms it, and relieves pain by expelling that air.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone, for by its strength in drawing, it may open the obstruction or remove the stone from its place. It does the same when placed on the liver and spleen at a gas that causes distress within them, and these cupping vessels may be used empty with suction only.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water for ailments of the eyes. This is done by filling the cupping vessel, let it be large, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for this have been boiled.
Then it is placed filled on the area, held in place, removed, and repeated as needed, and this is a picture of the cupping vessel used with fire:.
Its mouth should be two fingers wide when opened as we have depicted, and its depth should be half a finger-breadth. There should be a small hole on its side, about halfway, the size of what a needle can enter. It is made from Chinese brass or yellow brass with thick edges.
It should be smooth, flat, and polished so as not to harm the organ when placed upon it. There should be a transverse tube of brass or iron in its center where the candle for fire is placed. This cupping vessel can also be made larger than described or smaller according to the diseases.
And we will describe its use, for the cupping vessels used for children and thin individuals are different from those used for men and obese bodies. As for how to place this cupping vessel with fire on the organ, light a wick from cotton or a small candle made of wax, and place it on the center of the tube that is in the middle of the cupping vessel.
Let the fire rise upwards towards the bottom of the cupping vessel so that the patient's body does not burn, then place it on the limb with a finger over the hole we mentioned until when we hold the cupping vessel no longer needed, remove the finger.
The steam comes out through that hole and the cupping vessel is dissolved in place, then light the wick as described and repeat if necessary. As for the cupping vessel used for treating hemorrhoids with water, it does not have a solid rod or a hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it on the limb only, and this is its shape:.
And this cupping vessel is better when it is large to hold a lot of water.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On Attaching Leeches.
Leeches are mostly used in parts where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are too small like the lip and gum, or because the limb is devoid of flesh like the fingers and nose and the like.
The way to use them is to select leeches from clean fresh water free from contamination, then leave them in fresh water for a day and night until they become hungry and there is nothing left in their insides. Then the body is first purged by venesection or cupping, then the affected limb is wiped.
Until it turns red, then place the leech on it; when it is full, it will fall off. If possible, apply cupping to the area, as it is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, massage it, and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off and this is a discharge, soak a piece of linen in cold water.
Place it from above until the discharge stops, and if the blood flows excessively, sprinkle powdered zaj, or unripe figs, or similar constrictives until the bleeding stops, or apply peeled split fennel to the area.
Leave it until the fennel sticks to the area, for the blood will stop. If leeches need to be reapplied, do not use the same leech if possible to use another one. If the leech refuses to attach, then wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or pierce a needle in the area until some blood comes out, then place [the leech]. For if it senses some blood, it will stick to its place. When you want to remove it, sprinkle some onion, salt, or ash on it, for it will fall off its place.
The Third Chapter.
On Fractures.
This chapter also contains useful information needed in the practice of medicine, and it is about setting fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, my children, that there are ignorant physicians and laypeople who may claim this chapter, those who have never browsed through the ancients' books on it or read a single letter from them.
Because of this reason, this branch of knowledge has become non-existent in our country, and I have never come across anyone who is proficient in it at all. I have gained what I have from it through prolonged reading of the books of the predecessors and my insistence on understanding them until I extracted that knowledge from them, then I dedicated myself to practice and experience throughout my life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience with it has passed after I brought it close to you, freed it from the pitfalls of lengthiness, condensed it as much as possible, clarified it to the utmost, and presented to you many illustrations of the tools used in it.
For it is an addition to the clarification as I did in the previous two chapters, and there is no power except by Allah the Most High, the Great.
The first chapter.
Since there are summaries and essentials regarding the matter of broken bones that should be presented, before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated limbs one by one, it is appropriate to mention some sayings and sections at the beginning of this chapter. They first require you to understand them and grasp their true nature, you and whoever is eager to learn this noble craft besides you.
I say that whenever a fracture, dislocation, swelling, or fall occurs in someone, you should first quickly attend to their bloodletting or purging, or both. Unless there is a hindrance, such as weakness of strength, or if the one who experienced it is a child or an old feeble person, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, limit their diet to cold legumes, poultry, and goat meat, and avoid drinks, heavy meats, overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood until you are certain that there will be no swelling and no further accumulation of fluid in the affected area.
Then the patient should return to his initial regimen which he is accustomed to. When the broken bone begins to knit, it is appropriate that the patient be fed foods that are abundant, thick, strong, and adhesive in nature, such as dates, rice, heads of animals, shanks, and the stomachs of cattle. And eggs, soft fish, thick drinks, and the like, for by this regimen the setting of the fracture will be faster and better. Know that broken bones in strong men and the elderly cannot reconnect and heal to their original nature ever.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, they may not reconnect and heal. However, what is extremely flexible, like the bones of small children, can connect and heal. But nature grows something on the broken bone from all sides that resembles glue in thickness, sticking together and holding until they adhere to each other.
It binds them together until it reaches the utmost strength and security as it was initially, so that nothing hinders him in his actions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, adhesive, and thick, as we have said. Know that fractures vary in type according to the different parts of the body because a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the skull bone, and a fracture of the rib bone is different from a fracture of the spine.
And similarly, all other parts of the body differ from one another. We will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its own section separately. The type of bone fracture may also vary on its own.
For it may be that his fracture is a splitting without any shards occurring, and it may be that his fracture is along the bone, and its fracture has shards and protrusions, some cleansed and others not cleansed, and the fracture occurs with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
For each type has a specific method in setting it, as will be detailed in its place. Among the things by which bone fracture is recognized are its bending, confusion, appearance to the senses, and its crackling when you touch it with your hand.
When there is no apparent bending, no crackling, and you do not feel disturbance when you touch the bone, and the patient does not experience much pain, then there is no fracture; it could be a sprain or a slight bruise or a minor crack. Thus, it should not be moved by pulling or prodding at all.
Rather, apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later, which are suitable for the condition, then bind it gently. Know that if the bone is broken and split in two without any shards occurring, each part has moved away from its companion.
You should promptly set it and align it before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it as gently and skillfully as you can.
Know that setting and aligning it is easier than with a bone in which shards have occurred. Then, bind it as will be described later. If the bone has shards, it is necessary to pull the broken limb from both sides, whether it is an arm or a leg.
Either with your hands if the member is small, or with two ropes, or with the rope and hand. Place the member on an even surface in its natural form so that when a part of the broken bone extends, then trim those excesses to their places with all the cunning and gentleness you can manage.
Strive your best to not cause pain or suffering to the patient through your actions. Aim to align one of the bones with its counterpart in the best possible form, and during this time, touch and feel them with your hand. If you see anything amiss, correct and adjust it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and strong probing, as many unskilled people do. Often, their actions result in severe swelling or deformity of the member, which I have witnessed from them many times.
After alignment, perfection, and securing, keep that member still and at ease. Instruct the patient not to move it during their waking hours, sleep, turning, restlessness, bowel movements, and all other activities to the best of their ability, and ensure its position is one where pain is avoided.
This is because whenever he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the member, he should move it to another position where there is no pain. At the same time, ensure that its position is level and straight.
So that no deformity occurs in the member when it heals. As for how to secure a broken limb, I will describe it to you. Know that broken limbs differ in size and shape; those that are smaller include the arm, fingers, ankle, and the like.
It is necessary that the bandages be soft and gentle, and for those areas that are tough like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be broad and strong because the wide binding adheres to the large limb and tightens it evenly from all sides without any imbalance.
Then after you finish leveling, you begin to apply the corresponding paint. With a gentle flexing of the area of the fracture, you then begin to wrap the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, depending on what the limb requires, and you tighten the bandage slightly with your hand, then extend it to the upper part of the fractured area.
With less tension than you applied to the fractured area, you then move the wrapping slightly away from the fracture and gradually loosen the tension until you achieve a suitable position. Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap several times, then continue wrapping towards the lower side of the fracture. The tension and looseness of this wrap should be as described for the first upper wrap. Place some gentle flexing material or cloth between the wraps to straighten the deformity of the fracture.
