If there is a deformity, place something to correct it; otherwise, leave it empty. Then wrap another bandage over it. Adjust the tight bandages over these wraps immediately if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something to alleviate the swelling and reduce the inflammation. Leave it for several days, then secure the bandages之后应为：then secure the splints. The splints should be made of broad, wisely carved half-reeds or they can be made from colander wood.
Which is made from pine or palm fronds or reed or linen and the like that are available of these, and let the width of each splint be in this form and exactly this shape.
Unless the splint that is placed on the fracture itself is slightly thicker and wider than the other splints. As for the length of the splint, it is made according to what is appropriate for the size of the fractured limb, whether large or small. The form of the splint:
Then bind the splints with another bandage according to the same tightness as your first binding, then secure it from above with tight threads according to what we mentioned about the tightness, which is that your binding at the site of the fracture should be tighter. And the further away from the fracture, the looser the binding should be.
The threads should be of moderate thickness and fineness, and they should preferably be made of wet linen, because if the threads are too thick, as I have seen ignorant people do when they make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because binding with them falls outside moderation, and very thin threads are also unsuitable because you will not achieve the tightness needed. There should not be less than a finger's width between each splint.
If the patient is bothered by the edges of the splints after they have been correctly tightened, place soft wool or loose cotton under them so that nothing causes discomfort. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and there is a break in the skin, we will address this separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be bandaged with splints from the first day. For if the limb is large, splints should not be applied to it until after five or seven days or more, according to your assurance against the occurrence of hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages that were used by the early practitioners for fractures, dislocations, and contusions. A general brief bandaging method that treats fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, as it does not tend to be excessively hot or cold.
It involves taking the flour dust from the millstone, which adheres to the walls of the mill when the mill is in motion. Knead it as it is without sifting it, using egg whites, and make its consistency neither too thick nor too thin, then apply it.
Description of another bandaging method for setting fractures, dislocations, and contusions: take marsh mallow, resin, aqqaqiya, rasan, mugwast, and sugar—ten drachms of each; also take vinegar and ginger—five drachms of each; from althaea, twenty drachms; and from Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Grind all the ingredients and mix them with althaea water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot. Use this bandage, for it is well-composed, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people due to its balance.
Description of another bandage that is also beneficial for fractures and contusions: take mugwast, marsh mallow, and white chaste tree—ten drachms of each; vinegar and ginger—five drachms of each; aqqaqiya—six drachms; Armenian clay—twenty drachms. Grind all finely, sift, knead with water or egg white, and use it.
A description of a bandage for joints and bones dislocated from their places, which alleviates the pain associated with them, as well as bone fractures, is taken: white wool is dipped in vinegar and cooked oil, then applied to the area. This bandage does not have healing power.
However, it is excellent in reducing hot swelling and alleviating pains particularly. A description of another bandage that mends the broken bone: take dark fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind them all together, and apply them moistly. A description of another concise bandage used when setting a fractured bone.
And when you want to reduce the remaining swelling, take equal parts of the root of chicory, camomile, purple foxglove, and ground celery seeds. Grind all together and knead with honey if the limb is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, knead with coriander water or plain water and use it.
A description of another bandage that is stronger in reducing swelling than the first one, used when solid swelling occurs after setting a bone: take equal parts of chicory root, flaxseed, fenugreek, marigold, mastic, purple foxglove, and camomile.
Grind all together and knead with decoction water or fresh water or honey, each according to the heat of the limb and its cooling. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that anything should be placed on the broken limb when setting it.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil alone, it should be described as moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he suggested placing things that are drying with some heat, such as salt, myrrh, frankincense, and the like, on the broken limb when setting it.
A description of a bandage that is beneficial for weakness and pain: take wood ash, chickpeas, human hair or bird feathers, colocynth, and salt in equal parts. Grind them, sieve, knead, and apply as a bandage. As for the amount that the binding should remain.
Then it is undressed, which means you observe; if the injured does not experience pain or itching and the broken bone does not move from its place, do not undress it for many days. If there is severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling in the area, then it should be undressed.
Hasten to undress it at the time and do not delay this at all, remove the bandage, then take a soft cloth or a seaweed sponge that is moist, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside, then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply clean wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat, bind it for a night to prevent inflammation and to subdue the swelling and pain of the limb. Afterwards, return it to gentle binding with light bandaging without tightening it as you did initially.
Tenderly care for it until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and all other symptoms have completely disappeared and you need bandaging and binding again, repeat as you did initially. If none of the mentioned issues occur in the limb.
Do not remove it until three, four, five, or seven days have passed; it may even be left for twenty days. All of this depends on the condition of the limb as you observe it, as we have said, until the fracture is secure and the flesh around it is nearly healed.
Also increase the tension at that time more than your first tension altogether, and also increase the thickness of the patient's diet as previously mentioned. If you see that the site of the fracture has dried out and become thin more than it should, then know that the nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Wash it with lukewarm water every time you change the dressing, and let this be done every three days. Slightly reduce the tension, as this action allows nourishment to flow to the limb, promoting quick healing. As for what the ignorant among the splitters do, which is to break the limb again.
If it does not set properly the first time and sets crooked, then this is an error in their action and a great deception. If it were correct, the predecessors would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, but I have not seen any trace of it from any of them at all. And the correct approach is not to follow it.
The Second Chapter.
On incidental fractures in the head.
Incidental fractures in the head are varied, their forms are different, and their causes are diverse. Some fractures result from a sword blow and may involve the complete splitting of the bone until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
For this reason, this type of fracture is called an axe fracture. Alternatively, the sword may cut part of the bone and only split its surface without penetrating to the end, and this type of fracture is called a partial extraction. The wounds of these fractures can be either large or small.
And there are fractures that are comminuted or contused, and their cause is a blow with a stone or falling onto a stone or the like. This type of fracture can either be penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may occur on the surface of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures can also be either large or small, and there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone in the thinness of the hair, which is a slight crack.
For this reason, this type of fracture is called hairline. Among them is a fracture caused by a fall or impact with a stone or the like, where the surface of the bone indents inward, making the area concave, similar to what happens to a copper pot when it is struck and part of it indents inward.
This is most common in the soft skull bones, such as the skulls of children. All these types of fractures may have detached fragments that are exposed or not exposed, and we will discuss the treatment of all this in its proper place. We identify all these types of fractures through examination.
And examine it with probes and remove the rotten flesh from it. As for the hairline type, it is identified by examining the bone, rubbing it, and涂上墨水，骨折处会显现出黑色。至于骨折的治疗，首先要观察病人的症状。
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of consciousness, cessation of speech, fainting, acute fever, protruding and red eyes, and other similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient or treat them, for death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then at that time begin his treatment. That is, if the wounded person comes to you right after being wounded and this happened in the winter days.
It is necessary to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. And if it is in the summer days, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day so that the membrane under the bone does not spoil, thus causing the symptoms we described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is fragmentation and crushing, it is necessary to cut off the crushed and fragmented part as I describe to you, which is to shave the head of the injured patient.
And expose the bone in whatever way is feasible for you, according to the shape of the wound and what is easier on the patient. If bleeding or a hot swelling appears when exposing the bone, deal with it as appropriate.
That is, fill the area with cloth soaked in a mixture of wine and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is controlled. Then proceed to set and extract the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a fine, narrow-bladed cutter, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter after this one, slightly wider than the previous one, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter, even wider than the second one. It is necessary to have a set of different cutters, some wider than others and some shorter than others, with extremely sharp edges. They should be made of Indian iron or good steel.
Use gentleness when tapping on the fracture to prevent the head from being shaken and thus harmed. If the bone is strong and solid, it should be drilled around before using the saws with drills that are called non-penetrating drills, and they are named non-penetrating drills.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the skull bone to what is beyond it, for the drill has a round edge below its sharp point, similar to a collar or small circle that prevents it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should have a variety of these drills, with each one suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have a drill whose sharp point length and shortness match the thickness of that skull. This is the image of three types. Of the drills, there is a large, medium, and small:
As for the manner of drilling around the broken bone, you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the drill to another location and ensure that there is a distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a thread or similar.
Then, you cut with the saw between each two holes and do this with utmost gentleness as we said until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other tool from the instruments you have prepared for this purpose.
Like glue and delicate forceps, you should be extremely careful that the drill or saw does not touch any part of the membrane. If you have removed the bone and it has healed from the membrane, which was attached to it, and you have extracted it, you should then clean it thoroughly.
And smooth out the roughness that remains in the bone with another tool similar to a cutter, except it should be thinner and gentler than other cutters. If any small bones or splinters remain, take them out gently with the tools you have.
Then treat the wound with bandages and ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other aspect of the work, it is extremely simple and far from danger, as Galen mentioned and greatly praised. This is his statement. You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent. Once you have exposed that part, place the end of this lens-shaped cutter underneath it, and here is its shape:
The lens part of it should be smooth and not cut anything, while the sharp part is on its long sides as you see, so that the lens part rests against the membrane and the sharp face of the cutter is against the bone. Then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small mallet until you cut all the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from any fearful accident occurring to the membrane, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and timid of people. Yes, even if he were drowsy.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the edge of the lens-shaped cutter itself and remove it gently, for it can be removed without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane and only the surface of the bone is broken, leaving roughness and fine splinters, you should remove that roughness and extract those splinters with delicate chisels that have been prepared in various sizes so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate one for each place according to what the work guides you to.
The nature of the broken bone and its shape, and it is necessary that you first use the widest saw for the bone in your set, then use a finer one after it, and continue doing this gradually until you use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for other small incisions and delicate fractures, it is necessary to treat each one according to what leads to its healing, which is something not hidden from anyone who has the slightest skill in this craft and understands what we have written and explained about larger fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, it is advisable to take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound, dip it in rose oil, and place it on the mouth of the wound. Then take another folded or triangular cloth. Dip it in a mixture of rose oil and place it on the first cloth. Do this as gently as possible so as not to weigh down the membrane. Then apply a wide bandage on top, but do not tighten it beyond what is necessary to hold the wound in place.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain that the heat and swelling have subsided, then remove it. Afterwards, use medications that help dryness, such as hawthorn root, crushed cornsilk, powdered barley, zeraunja, and the like. You should make a powder from these medications.
Sprinkle the dry powder on the wound. In general, use a medication that cleanses without stinging. Strive to keep the wound clean during treatment, free from any grease or dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate at all.
For pus, when it gathers on the brain membrane, corrupts and spoils it, thus causing great harm to the patient; and it may occur in the skull lining when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If there is a blackness on its surface, and you find that the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, then know that he will surely perish. If the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied to it, and if that medicine had the power to cause such blackness.
You should take a portion of honey and three portions of rose oil, mix them well, soak a cloth in the mixture, then apply it to the wound. Treat it with various appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of the nose if it is broken.
Know that only the upper parts of the nose, which are bones, can break. The lower part is made of cartilage and does not break, but may suffer from swelling, distortion, and bruising.
If one side of the nose is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nasal opening and align the fracture from inside while using your index finger and thumb to guide it from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this gently to ensure that you do not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, you should align it with a slightly thickened blunt object. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. It is advisable to set it within the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert into the nostril's hole a wick of linen cloth if the fracture is on one side or insert two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril's hole, and some of the early fixators mentioned that the wick should be soaked in fat and changed every day. I do not agree with that; rather, the wick should be soaked in egg white mixed with millstone dust.
Then leave the wick until the bone heals and the cartilage hardens. You may insert quill tubes of duck feathers into the nose, wrapped in soft cloth, to better hold the nasal fracture in place without obstructing breathing.
This is not necessary if you prefer to make the wicks instead. If a hot swelling appears on the nose during your treatment, treat it with kerotie or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some ointment of diachylon.
If no hot swelling appears, it is advisable to dress it externally with semolina and ground barley mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it without binding the nose at all. If the nasal bones are fractured in small breaks.
Or if they are shattered, it is advisable to incise and remove them with a suitable instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and closing of wounds. If there is a wound inside the nose, it should be treated with wicks and the use of lead tubes is recommended. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of a broken lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound, examine it. If the break is only from the outside and not split into two and has not caved inwards, then its treatment is easier.
If the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently raise the bony prominence of the fracture from inside to outside.
Your other hand should control the alignment from outside the bone. If the jaw fracture has split into two, it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until proper alignment is achieved. If there is any displacement of the teeth, it must be corrected.
Or if they are separated, sew them together with a thread of gold, silver, or silk as much as you hope will remain. Then place turpentine on the broken jaw, and put a folded cloth over it. Place a large, secure bandage or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw over the cloth. Then bind it from above according to what seems appropriate for securing it and keeping it together so that it does not come undone. Instruct the patient to rest and remain still, and provide them with soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect any change in shape has occurred, promptly address it on the third day.
Then bind it from above according to what seems appropriate for securing it and keeping it together so that it does not come undone. Instruct the patient to rest and remain still, and provide them with soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect any change in shape has occurred, promptly address it on the third day. Then, prepare what you bandage with mill dust and egg white or with semolina flour after removing the linen from it, and place a soft cloth over the bandage. As long as that bandage sticks to it and no change occurs in the bone, leave it.
Do not remove it until it heals and the fracture strengthens. This fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during this time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to alleviate it until the swelling disappears.
And if the fracture is with a wound, examine it. If a fragment or fragments of bone have become detached, gently extract those fragments using whatever method you find suitable for their removal from the site. If the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, after you have extracted those fragments and none remain, suture the opening of the wound if it is wide. Otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. Suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of a broken rib.
Ribs most often break from the front, near the end of the shoulder blade. The fracture can occur in one of three ways: it may break and snap into two without causing fragments, which is the easiest to treat.
Or fragments may be caused by the fracture, which is more difficult to treat. Or the fracture may occur with a wound. If the fracture does not involve a wound, prepare two assistants: one to hold the arm adjacent to the broken rib, and the other to extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, without any protrusion or indentation. If more extension is needed, you should place a ball of cloth or wool under the armpit of the patient.
The bone should be of the size you need, extend and lift the splint, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is aligned as it should be. If you cannot pull the end of the splint out because it has gone deep, then the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately firm pillow is placed under his armpit, and the servant presses his shoulder downward until the end of the splint that has gone deep rises up. Then, adjust and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the bone has broken off and is moving, you should make an incision over it and gently remove that splinter. If the splinter is trapped within the bone, you must use one of the cutting instruments prepared for this purpose to extract it. After placing under the splint the device that protects the intestines, which is made of wood or iron, and this is its shape:
It resembles a spoon without any indentation, and its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. Its length should also be suitable for the task at hand. It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the incision you made to remove the bone fragment is wide and you are not concerned about a hot swelling, then sew the edges of the wound together. If the incision is small or you fear a hot swelling, then pack the wound with cloth and bandages according to the size of the cut.
If a hot tumor appears, then soak the dressing in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting the bone without any cut or incision, it is done by applying to the bone a poultice made from mill powder with egg white. Place the soft bandage on it, then put a ball under his armpit if needed. Then take a turban that is very long and its width is about a finger's breadth or so. Then apply a folded pad on the bandage and dressing. Then take a splint from a thin board. Its width should be three fingers and the same in length. Then insert it into a cloth, then place that cloth with the splint in the part of the turban that falls over the fracture site, then tie the turban tightly around the fracture as you wrap it. And wrap it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his affected armpit, and fold it several times on each side, as you see fit for the tension to secure the fracture firmly, which is not unknown to you.
The goal of all this is to ensure that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day. Whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on their back and place a small pillow under their armpit when they sleep at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, raising the clavicle fracture with the shoulder's elevation, or bind his arm to his neck. Do not remove the bandage unless an event such as itching or swelling occurs at the site, for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see fit to do so. Apply traction and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it strengthens more in twenty-eight days. And it may be less than that for some people.
Chapter Six.
On the healing of a shoulder fracture. The shoulder rarely breaks at its wide part, but rather at its narrow sections. Whenever it breaks at a spot or in the middle, it can be identified by touch. The treatment should correspond to the shape and form of the fracture.
Straighten and return it to its natural shape by whatever means possible, then apply the dust of the millstone with egg whites and soft cloth, and place a folded cloth pad on top. Then secure it with a wide board splint.
Make it thin enough to cover the entire shoulder or slightly wider. If there is an indentation under the splint in any part of the shoulder, level that indentation with soft cloth until the splint lies flat. Then secure it from above with a long bandage.
Tie it firmly and ensure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever it loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved. The patient should lie on their healthy side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
It may take twenty or twenty-five days. Once this period is complete, remove the bandage safely, for it is one of the bones that does not fear fragmentation or collapse. If a bone shard protrudes and pierces under the skin, make an incision over it and extract it. And make what I mentioned for the fracture of the clavicle from soothing the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of chest fractures.
The chest may fracture in its center, though this is rare. However, its sides are more frequently fractured and shattered. Its symptoms when its center fractures include tilting downwards, severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And sometimes blood may be discharged, and the depression in the fractured bone becomes apparent and is not hidden to the touch. Its treatment involves having the patient lie on their back with a pillow placed between their shoulders. Then press their shoulder blades and gather the ribs from both sides with the hands, carefully aligning them as much as possible.
Continue until the bone returns to its proper shape, then apply a dressing and pressure, and place a splint made from a light plank of poplar or willow or something similar above it after wrapping it in cloth. Then carefully tie it to the fractured bone so that it does not move, and wrap the binding around in a circular manner towards the back several times, securing it tightly. Then check the binding at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity calls for its removal due to infection, pain, or swelling in the area, then promptly remove it, take off the dressing, and treat whatever has occurred with appropriate treatment, then reapply the dressing if you deem it necessary. Bind it and keep it tightly bandaged until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
On the setting of ribs when broken, know that the breakage occurs in the thick parts adjacent to the back, and their ends from the front are subject to compression because they are cartilaginous. The knowledge of this is not hidden when examined by the fingers.
Set it by aligning the fracture with the fingers in the most effective manner until the shape is as it should be, then bandage it and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the rib fracture leans inward, it will cause severe pain to the patient.
And it swells like the swelling that occurs in those with pleurisy, because the bone swells the membrane, and difficulty in breathing, coughing, and expelling much blood also occur. This is difficult to treat, and early practitioners devised many remedies for it.
Some have said that the patient's diet should include foods that cause inflation and gases so that the belly swells, expands, and pushes the fracture outward. We dislike this so as not to reinforce the occurrence of a hot swelling if it has not already happened; if it has occurred, it will increase and confirm it.
Some have said that a leech should be placed on the area and then suctioned strongly, which seems more appropriate; however, there is concern that the leech might draw too much from the weakened area. Some have also said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
And the padding is placed between the ribs until it fills, so that the bandage lies flat when wrapped around, then treat the patient with the treatment for wounds from food and medicine, if the patient suffers severe distress that he cannot endure. And the bone was piercing the membrane painfully, and we feared for the patient, so it should be that we incise at the location and expose the broken rib, then place under it the device which protects the peritoneum, which has been previously described, and cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring the edges of the wound together with stitches and treat it with ointments until it heals. If during this time a hot swelling appears, act quickly, soak the padding in rose oil, apply it to the area, and treat the patient internally with what alleviates the swelling as well.
And he should lie on the side that makes sleeping easier until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of spinal and neck fractures.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, which is rare, they more often suffer from contusion. The same applies to the vertebrae of the back. If this happens to someone and you want to know whether they will heal or not. Then observe, if you see that his hands have become weak, numb, and lifeless, and he is unable to move them, stretch them, or clench them, and when you pinch them or prick them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, know that he will not heal in most cases, and he is doomed.
And if he moves them and feels biting and pricking in them, know that the bone marrow is safe and the patient will recover with treatment. If the same affects the spinal thorn and you want to know if it will also recover or not.
Look at his legs, if you see that they have relaxed and what happened to the hands has occurred in them, then when he lies on his back, gas and feces are expelled involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate but is unable to do so, know that he is terminal, so do not bother with his treatment. If none of these symptoms appear, the condition is milder. The treatment for what has occurred is to aim to relieve the hot swelling.
Place on the affected vertebra rose oil alone or mixed with roasted egg yolks. Apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening, drying poultices to the area, bind it tightly with a bandage, and order him to rest and stay still.
He should only sleep on the side that does not cause pain until he recovers. If a fragment of bone or something similar has separated within the fracture, it is necessary to cut the skin and extract that bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, then treat it with unguents that promote healing until it recovers. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, breaks, you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus.
And the broken bone is aligned with the other hand according to what can be managed and the alignment is achieved, then apply the bandage and splint if necessary, then secure it.
If you feel a fractured splinter in it, incise over it and extract it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of hip fracture.
The vertebrae rarely break, but if they do break, the fracture occurs at their ends, they split lengthwise, and lean inward. The patient will experience pain in the affected area, swelling, and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is to run your hand over it until you locate the fracture and understand its shape. If the fracture is only at its ends, then align that fracture according to what you can manage in terms of alignment until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has leaned inward, then lay the patient on their stomach so that you can set the fracture. Once aligned, apply a bandage, then place a splint made of wood or leather over it and secure it tightly.
Do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the shifting of the splint. Fill in the concavities of the ribs to ensure even tension. Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side. If a hot swelling occurs, address it accordingly.
Cease extending and setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply what is necessary to alleviate it as previously described, then return to setting and securing it properly. If there are splinters or fragmentation at the ends of the bone, they should not be removed.
And it is not touched but smoothed from the outside as we said and its tension is left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a humerus fracture.
The humerus is what lies between the elbow and the shoulder. If it fractures, it should be set in one of two ways. The first is to take a smooth, moderately thick curved stick in this manner:
And you tie two straps at its ends, then hang it from a high place. The patient sits on a chair, then throw his fractured arm over the stick until his armpit is attached to the center of the curvature of the stick. Then suspend something heavy above it.
Or a servant extends his arm downward, then the doctor aligns the fracture with both hands until it is set properly. The other method is for the patient to lie on his back and have his hand suspended from his neck with a strap.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with both hands and the other to hold below it, each pulling in their direction. If you want the pull to be stronger, tie a strap under the fracture and another above it, and have each servant pull them in their respective directions.
If the fracture is close to the shoulder tip, it is appropriate for the middle of the strap to be under the armpit and the other under the fracture toward the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, it is appropriate for the strap to be in that position and on the elbow itself.
Then align the fracture gently without force until the fracture is properly aligned and well united. Then bind it if there is no appearance of hot swelling; if hot swelling appears, then leave binding it until the seventh day and apply a clean cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bind it. The method of binding is to place a bandage on the fracture, then wrap a strip of new cloth over the bandage, gather the arm onto itself, and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And place the cloth and binding on the arm and forearm so that the arm serves as a splint if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes in the fractured bone. If you fear that something might change due to this, then use splints.
It involves placing on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, with each splint separated by the width of a finger. The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an additional three fingers' length from each side.
Then bind the splints with the binding method I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
It is that your binding on the site of the fracture should be tighter.
The binding should be less tight as you move away from the fracture. If you see that placing the splints and binding as we described at the time of setting the limb during your treatment is appropriate, then do so.
And if you fear hot swelling, then leave the binding and splints until the seventh day as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days to prevent itching or swelling or hindrance of nutrition reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, and adjust all of this as mentioned.
If you are certain that none of this will happen, do not loosen the bandage except after many days, and the patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a cushion filled with wool should be placed under his armpit at a moderate level.
And check at all times, whether night or day, to ensure that the shape of the broken limb does not deteriorate or the bandage loosens; correct everything with your effort. Make the patient's diet as we have mentioned previously, which is to make the food mild initially until the bone begins to strengthen.
It is advisable to make his diet more substantial, for it is common for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days. At that point, the bandage should be removed, and he may use the bath and be treated with ointments suitable for this purpose. If the fracture is severe and inflamed.
Do not remove the bandage from him until fifty days or two months have passed.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of an arm fracture.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the forearm bones. One is smaller, which is next to the thumb, and the other is larger, located below the smaller one. The ulna, the larger one, may break alone, or the radius, the smaller one, may break alone, or both may break together.
When the upper smaller bone (radius) is fractured, setting it is easier and healing occurs faster. When the lower bone (ulna) is fractured, the fracture is worse and healing is more difficult. The worst case is when both bones are broken together. If the fractured bone is the upper smaller bone (radius).
The physician, when setting it, should make the traction mild gently until he straightens it. If the large splint is the one broken, he should make the traction stronger. If both splints are broken, he should make the traction very strong.
It is appropriate that the form of the hand be extended on a pillow when setting and traction are applied, with the thumb of the hand raised above all the fingers, and the little finger below the other fingers, and the patient sitting cross-legged.
And let the pillow be at his level in height so that the patient does not undergo difficulty.
Then, a servant stretches the arm from below, either by hand or with a band, and another servant stretches from above in the same manner. Then, the doctor aligns the bone until he restores it to the best possible form. If there are splinters in the broken bone, they should be removed.
Place each splinter in its position to the best of your ability. If a splinter protrudes and pierces the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, incise over it and extract it as described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have dedicated a separate section for it.
Seek the treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of setting, apply a cloth soaked in kerotyi made with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thick and thin. Bind the cloth gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the kerotyi and apply a dressing prepared from mill powder mixed with egg white. Then, apply bandages, and let the bandage placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger. Know that the number of bandages for the arm is usually six.
The fracture is in one molar or in both molars together, then make your bandaging at the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and every time you rotate the bandage upwards or downwards, make the bandaging slightly looser as was mentioned earlier in the chapter.
Let the bandage that is wrapped around the fracture be a soft, moist one and not too hard. Let the thread used for binding be of special cotton, intermediate between thinness and thickness as we described, and examine the limb and tendon after some days.
If something needs to be corrected, such as an itch that occurs in the limb, it should be washed with warm water until the itch subsides. The limb should be left unbound for a night to rest, then binding is resumed.
If the binding has become loose or the bone has shifted, correct all of that to the best of your ability. Also, if the nourishment is unable to reach the limb due to excessive tightness, you should loosen it slightly and leave it for a few days until the nourishment can flow through, then bind it again.
If nothing mentioned occurs to the patient, it should not be loosened until after twenty days or so. Then suspend the patient's arm to his neck and let his arm be in a balanced position. He should avoid restless movements and sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days or thirty-two, and it may heal in twenty-eight days. All of this depends on the conditions of the humors and the strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of fractures in the hand and fingers.
The palm's knuckle and the fingertips rarely suffer fractures, but they often experience contusions. Whenever a fracture or contusion occurs in the palm.
It is advisable for the patient to sit cross-legged with a chair straight in front of him.
Then he should place his hand on it, outstretched. The bonesman then stretches the fractured limb, and the doctor aligns it until it is well set. At this point, bandaging and pressure should be applied unless there is a warm swelling; then a splint should be placed above according to the site.
And it should be inserted into a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm, make a ball-like shape from cloth and have the patient grasp it with their fractured hand, then secure it with a long strip of cloth.
Let the splint be made of leather that has flexibility so that it conforms to the bending of the entire palm and secure it as needed. If the fracture is on the outside, a splint should be placed from above and another from below in the palm so that the hand remains open and straight.
Then secure the binding as the hand is rotated and interlock the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture occurs in one of the fingertips, and it is the thumb, align it properly and then bind it with the palm. If you prefer, you may make a small upright splint to support the fracture.
And if it does not move, then if the fracture is for all the fingers such as the middle finger and the index or little or ring finger, they should be aligned and bandaged with the healthy finger next to them or all of them are bandaged on a splint, which is better, or a small upright splint is placed on it.
As we have said about the thumb and examine during and after setting it, if it is away from the hot swelling, counter it as appropriate whenever something of this sort occurs, as has been described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On setting the femur fracture.
The thigh bone often fractures and can be detected by sensation because it shifts forward and backward. Setting it involves placing a bandage above the fracture and another below it while the patient lies face down.
Then, each supporting bandage is stretched to its side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. If it is close to the base of the thigh, it is advisable to tighten a soft bandage, either from wool or similar material.
At the base of the thigh toward the groin, the stretching should be upward and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee so that the stretching is downward. Then, the doctor aligns the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural form.
The bone will heal well. At that point, it is advisable to keep the dressing and binding in place if there is no hot swelling in the limb. If hot swelling does occur, leave it for days until the swelling subsides, then return to treating it.
And as for binding it, you should fold a solid and wide bandage around the fracture two or three times and leave what remains. Then bind the leg until the heel is at the base of the ankle, and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, and let the ends of the thread be above on both sides.
Then wrap what remains of the bandage around the thigh and leg. Then apply splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and make one splint for the shin bone. Fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft cloths so that the binding is even.
Then start with a strong binding from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns, and as you move the bandage away from the site of the fracture, make your binding less tight, softer, and looser.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, tie them to the splints above, then pass the ends of the thread down until they reach the ankle, and also tighten them with the ends of the splints on the other side.
To ensure the bandage does not move from its place, then leave it tied as long as there is no pain, swelling, or inflammation in the limb. If any of these occur, immediately loosen it and correct whatever has happened as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, you should level it if possible; otherwise, cut into it and remove it. Treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This fracture of the thigh can sometimes be bound without adding the leg with splints.
As we have mentioned regarding the arm and forearm, except that setting it this way does not cause the patient to limp. However, if you set it alone without attaching the leg to it, the person will inevitably limp forever.
And know that the thigh strengthens in fifty days, or slightly more or less, all of which depends on the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the knee joint.
The knee joint rarely fractures, but it often suffers from contusions. If a fracture does occur, it would either be a split or a comminuted fracture in its parts, and this can happen with or without a laceration.
All of this can be determined by touch, and setting it involves aligning the dispersed parts with the fingers until they come together and unite as much as possible with precision, gentleness, and skill. Then apply a dressing, and if necessary, secure it with a circular splint.
Tighten above with the appropriate bandage for that, then attend to all the conditions we described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, by addressing each symptom with what is suitable until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On setting the fracture of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the fibula. Both are subject to various types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. For this reason, its setting is like the setting of the arm, and the procedure is the same. If both bones are broken, the leg turns in all directions; if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns forward; if the thicker bone is broken and this happens below, then it is not unknown to you.
Use stretching and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly. However, if the fracture of the leg is severe with many fragments, the stretching should be less and gentler, and you should take care in setting it.
And in the leg, there is an additional task compared to the arm: when you have aligned the splints and completed all your work, take two brooms made of pine wood, which are used for flattening rooms where strips are placed between the cracks of the boards, or they can be made from palm leaves or something similar.
Choose those that are slightly thick and not too thin, and let their length be the same as the leg from the knee down. Then wrap a cloth around each one twice along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let it extend from the knee to the foot. Then bind the two brooms in three places at the ends and middle, for with this restraint, the leg will not tilt to the right or left and will be set well. Sometimes a wooden gutter of the length of the leg is also used.
It is placed there to prevent movement, and it is most necessary to do this in the case of a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially. Then examine the leg every day and pay close attention to any swelling or inflammation or other such conditions. And when any of that occurs, counter it with what is appropriate until it heals, and the bone of the leg will heal in about thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fractures of the leg and fingers.
As for the heel, it is not subject to fracture at all. As for the bones of the leg, they may be subject to fracture, and fingers rarely suffer fractures; rather, they are more often subjected to contusions in most cases.
If the leg bones are fractured and you see that those bones are approaching each other, let the patient stand with his foot on the ground as if walking. Then you place your foot on the raised part of those bones and press down to align them.
Until they return to their places, then apply the dressing and splint. Place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, then firmly bind the foot after wrapping it with cloths and anything else needed.
If three or four days have passed, loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones aligned whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as you see. As for if one of the fingers is broken, set and align it as described for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along its length, but let it be slightly wider. Place under the foot the board I described and bind it firmly. If two or three or more of the bones are broken.
Make on each finger a splint of appropriate size enclosed in a soft cloth and bind the board at the bottom of the foot and let each end of the board protrude from under the sole of the foot to secure it well.
You should not forget to attend to all the symptoms we have mentioned for all kinds of fractures and address each symptom as appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When the woman's vagina is fractured, sit her in a squatting position, then gently bend her towards her back little by little. Let her hold onto something behind her. Then the midwife should stuff her vagina with cotton until it is full and forms like a ball inside her vagina.
Then the woman should be shaken and her body lifted little by little. The cotton will come out until it forms like a ball at the entrance of the vagina, and the bone fracture will be realigned. Then place a pad on her back. When she needs to urinate, gently remove the cotton so she can urinate.
Return it in the same manner as it was first inserted, then go back to her initial padding. Do this for seven days or so until it heals. If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, attach a reed tube to its mouth, and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, as this will realign the fracture. Then stuff it with cotton and keep it in place as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it should be treated similarly.
And its adjustment is as we described in the hip bone, and it will not be hidden from you the correctness in these rare fractures that seldom occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself guides you in most cases.
On the right way in setting and binding it, understand. As for when a man's thigh is fractured like a goose's throat, insert the phallic object into it, then place a wrap of cloth on it and bind it, leaving it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the setting of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is necessary for one who has sustained a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the thigh or the arm bone or the like, to promptly perform trepanning at that time if the conditions for trepanning assist you, as we have mentioned earlier.
If the wound is bleeding, you should promptly cauterize it by applying powdered zinc if nothing else is available. Then proceed to set the fracture on that very day and do not delay it.
If a hot swelling does not occur, then set the fracture immediately. If a hot swelling does occur, delay setting it until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides. Do not touch it at all on the third and fourth days, as it may result in不良反应。请注意，我提供的翻译需符合原始文本的意思，若存在任何不准确或需要更正的地方，请告知。但根据您的要求，这里没有出现需要特别注意的部分，直接进行了翻译。如果您期望得到更加精确的医学历史文献翻译，可能需要参考专业的医学历史文献翻译资源。不过，基于您之前的指示，我不会添加任何额外注释或解释。
If the broken bone protrudes and is exposed on the skin, it should be aimed to return it and set it with your hands gently and a slight pull. If you cannot return it and set it with your hands, then use this tool which is made of iron, its length being about seven or eight fingers. And its width should be according to the wound.
For this reason, the doctor should have three or four of them according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be round with a slight thickness so they do not bend when pressed upon during use.
It should be sharp at the end, have a bend at its tip, with the top being thicker and from the middle downwards very thin. This is its shape:.
It is called "peron" in Greek, meaning a small lever. It should be used to place its sharp bent end on the protruding bone and push it until the bone returns and becomes somewhat aligned, thus helping to set the ends of the fracture together.
If the broken end of the bone is thin and the tool does not grasp it well, you should cut that end of the bone until the tool can grasp it. If you cannot return the bone as described at all, then cut it with a similar cutter from those we have mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws as best you can, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone. If after returning the bone, the patient feels severe painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural place, then do so, for you greatly benefit the patient. Once you have completed setting the bone, dip a cloth in a black binding liquid, especially if you are in summer, and do not apply kerotis or anything fatty to the wound.
To prevent rot and corruption from occurring, then use bandages after you have finished setting the bone and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole with scissors in the bandage according to the size of the wound.
Beware, above all, of tightening the wound with the fracture, for ignorant physicians have often done this and caused either death or gangrene or inflammation in their patients. Let your binding be soft and loose, contrary to the tightness used for other fractures.
If the wound is poor or it is a large wound and you fear some of the不良症状我们描述过的，且他在伤口处感到令人不安的疼痛，你不应该在上面放置夹板，而是用坚固的布料制成卷带，在夹板的位置固定它。
If it has been a day or two and you see that the wound has begun to produce pus, remove the cloth soaked in the drink that was placed on it. Then use bandages and ointments which we customarily use to treat wounds such as the fourfold ointment and the like, and you should loosen the binding.
You should examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it heals and closes. It is advisable to position the limb in such a way that pus can flow down easily. If many days have passed and the wound has not healed nor stopped discharging pus, know that there are likely small bone splinters present.
You should probe the wound with a probe. Remove and extract any of those splinters that are loose. As for those that are not loose and are causing irritation to the limb and causing pain, cut and remove them in whatever way you can manage.
If the wound is exposed to inflammation, putrefaction, or any other type of corruption and decay, you should treat each condition with a corresponding treatment as previously described in its respective section.
Among the things you should pay attention to and keep in mind when a large bone is broken, such as the thigh or the upper arm and other similar large limbs, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For often such actions can lead to death. So leave it until it decays; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see an opportunity for treatment; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the swelling that occurs after some fractures.
This swelling often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially near the joints, which can distort the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from performing its natural functions.
If the swelling is soft, use medications that constrict, such as frankincense, myrrh, aloes, alum, and the like. Take some or all of these, and mix them with a constricting liquid or with egg white or vinegar.
Apply it to the swelling and bind it tightly, leaving it bound for many days. Then release it and repeat with another binding until the swelling goes away, if God wills, or bind a sheet of lead securely over it.
For lead has a property that can reduce all that swells in the limbs. If the swelling has hardened and become severe, and the necessity arises to remove it, cut into it from above and remove the excess growth or pare it down with a knife until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On treating fractures that have healed but left the limb weakened and different from its original nature.
If a bone fracture has healed but the limb remains weak and thin, this can be due to many reasons. One of them is either due to the excessive loosening and tying of bandages inappropriately, or due to over-tightening the bandages so that nourishment cannot reach the limb, or due to excessive and undue prolongation, or due to excessive movements at inappropriate times, or due to the scarcity of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment for that is to nourish the patient and fatten his body until blood increases in it, using a bath, bringing joy and happiness to him, and so on. Then apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch attracts much nutrition or continuously prolong with warm water. Until nourishment flows through it and returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of fractured bones that have healed crookedly and prevented their proper function.
If a deformity, protrusion in the fractured bone, or complexity arises in a limb after it has been set and healed, causing the appearance of the limb to be unsightly. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, it should not be accepted for one to claim that they will break the limb again. Many ignorant physicians and bone setters in our country have often done this.
This action is highly condemned and can lead to great harm, the least of which is disability. However, if the deformity and complexity are pliable, it should be treated with water in which relaxing herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves and root of the willow and marigold, and the like.
And it is dressed with soft bandages like well-made diachylon, or the sap of the root of the colocynth is taken and mixed with chicken fat and cerise oil and used to dress it, or take the juicy fig and pound it with pigeon dung and similar medicines which are called dispersers of adhesion.
And the complexity is resolved by constant gentle massage performed by hand and using movement of the limb in all directions at all times. If the deformity has progressed, become severe, and hardened, and it becomes necessary to treat it with iron.
It is necessary to incise above it, release the bone connection, and carefully remove any remaining complexity or bone with fine cuts, performing this with gentleness, effort, and attention. Then treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocation of one of the joints from its place, which hinders movement, uglifies the appearance of the limb, and causes severe pain to the patient. Whenever someone suffers from a jaw dislocation, it should be immediately attended to and reduced.
It should not be delayed at all, for if it is delayed, the area will swell and make it difficult to reduce the jaw. Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor should it be moved or stretched while swollen, as this often causes spasms and painful distress to the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should immediately begin to treat the patient. Then leave it until the swelling subsides slightly, then foment the limb with hot water and oil, then gently reduce it. Each part should be treated as described in its respective place.
And I have arranged the chapters on dislocation in accordance with what was previously mentioned regarding fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the Treatment of the Lower Jaw Dislocation.
It is rare for the jaws to dislocate except in cases of scarcity, and their dislocation can occur in two ways: either they move slightly from their places and become somewhat relaxed, or they dislocate completely until they are fully relaxed. Until it reaches toward the chest so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot hold it back nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. If the dislocation is slight, it often returns on its own with minimal intervention.
And if the dislocation is complete, it should be corrected immediately without delay. This is done by having an attendant hold the patient's head while the doctor inserts the thumb of one hand at the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side.
Or if the dislocation is on both sides, he should insert both thumbs and use the rest of his fingers from the outside to align them. The doctor instructs the patient to relax his jaw and let it go in every direction while the doctor aligns and pushes the jaw back into its proper place.
If it is difficult to reduce the dislocation, especially if both jaws are dislocated, use a compress with hot water and oil until their reduction becomes easy. Do not delay their reduction at all as mentioned. Once they are returned to their proper place and the patient's mouth closes without relaxation, then apply bandages with cerotes made from wax and ointment.
Then it is gently bandaged with a loose bandage, and the patient should sleep on his back with his head pillowed between two pillows so that he does not move to the right or left nor chew anything but rather make his food liquid and easy.
Until the pain disappears and the jaw heals, let him eat what he desires, but he should do so gently and not strain to open his mouth while eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the jaws do not close properly when they separate at some point and do not return to their places, inflammation and constant headaches often occur, and the patient may experience bloating or vomit bitter bile. If you see this, know that it has become corrupted.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the Reduction of the Collarbone and Shoulder Joint.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest, but it may dislocate outward, which can be determined by touch. Its reduction involves the patient lying on his back and extending his arms.
Then press the area with your palm forcefully, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and secure it tightly. As for the end that adjoins the shoulder joint, it rarely dislocates. If it ever does dislocate, it should be reduced and aligned as mentioned, and in whatever way you deem appropriate.
Then apply the dressing, bandages, and binding, and instruct the patient to maintain rest and tranquility until recovery. With this exact treatment, you also realign a dislocated shoulder joint.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the realignment of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint may dislocate in three ways: one is when it dislocates towards the armpit downwards, the second is when it dislocates towards the chest, and sometimes it may dislocate upwards from the shoulder, though this is rare.
It does not dislocate backwards due to the presence of the shoulder bone, nor does it dislocate forwards due to the presence of the nerves. It most frequently dislocates and moves downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with less muscle mass because it dislocates and relocates quickly in such individuals.
As for those who have a lot of muscle mass, the opposite is true; that is, it dislocates with difficulty and relocates with difficulty. Sometimes, some people may receive a blow or fall, causing swelling in the shoulder and heat, leading them to assume it has dislocated. It should be examined thoroughly to confirm its actual condition.
Then proceed with its treatment. The dislocation is identified if it is downwards towards the armpit; when you align the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, you will notice a clear difference. You will find the head of the dislocated shoulder indented and, upon touching under the armpit, the head of the shoulder feels like an egg.
The patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear or move it in all its normal movements. Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will clearly feel this upon touch, and it cannot be concealed.
This jaw may be easily replaced if it is soft or the patient is a child. Its replacement is done by having an assistant raise his hand upwards, then you place your thumbs under his armpit and lift the joint forcefully upwards to its position while the assistant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then apply it downwards, for it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned and the jaw has been like this for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use laxatives that relax and soften, such as cooking the root of colocynth, fenugreek, and marigold in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and a ball of wool, which is balanced between softness and firmness, is placed under his armpit. Then the doctor places his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand in his hand and pulls it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, for it will return to its place. If you wish, you can replace it this way: have a taller person than the patient stand beside him, and have his shoulder enter under the patient's armpit and lift it upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another assistant should pull the patient's hand downwards toward his abdomen. If the patient is light, it is advisable to hang something heavy on him to increase his weight, for the jaw will return immediately. It can also be replaced in another way.
This involves driving a long stick into the ground, with its head being round like the pestle of a mortar, neither too thick nor too thin. Then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft cloth on top of the stick, with the patient standing along the length of the stick.
Then he extends his hand downward from the other side and also stretches his body from the other side with force, then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we mentioned, use this treatment: take a stick about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers and its thickness is about two fingers, it should have a rounded head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:.
Then tie a soft cloth around the rounded head so that the stick does not harm the patient, then place it under the armpit and extend the entire hand and arm downward on the stick, and bind the stick to the upper arm, forearm, and wrist.
Then the arm is placed on a bar. Give it width and extend the hand downward, leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side, then the joint will be reduced immediately. Once it is reduced in any possible way, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the dressing prepared from flour dust mixed with frankincense and egg white is applied all over the shoulder, circling it from above. Then the ball is tightly secured under the armpit and wrapped with a bandage over the dressing, and his hand is suspended around his neck.
It should be left不动七天，患者的饮食应少量，以便该部位能更快恢复 strength. Seven days later, or even five days, it can be untied and trained with movement. If it is stable and does not relax, then it has healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture affecting it or another ailment, then cauterization should be employed using three pieces of brass as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. If you do all this and release the ligament after seven days and the joint does not stabilize.
I replaced the dressing and tightened it several times but it did not stabilize, fell, and relaxed, and you were unable to raise it upwards, know then that the nerve at the top of the shoulder has been severed or stretched or relaxed. Know then that the joint will never stabilize in its place.
As for the jaw that is directed towards the chest and breast and to the back, its realignment is done by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns to place. All kinds of binding and treatment are applied until it heals. If stiffness occurs in the joint and movement slows down after healing, this may present.
Let the patient use the bath frequently until that stiffness is softened and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of the elbow joint.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns to place with difficulty. It can dislocate in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is clearly visible and palpable in whatever form it has dislocated.
When you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, it will be clearly evident due to the deformity of the joint. The arm cannot be bent, nor can it touch the shoulder. You should act promptly and realign the dislocation immediately before inflammation sets in.
For if a hot swelling afflicts him, it is difficult to reduce and sometimes it does not heal at all, especially if the jaw is set back, for this is the worst kind of jaw and the most painful, and death often comes with it.
If it can be reduced, a servant should extend his arm with both hands and stretch out his arm while the doctor places his hands above and below the elbow, pushing the joint back into place with the thumbs of both hands or the base of his palm until it returns to its position.
And if the jaw is dislocated forward, it can be reduced by bending the arm until the base of the palm strikes the corresponding elbow. If the jaw does not respond to being put back, use very strong extension, which requires two servants to stretch the arm.
Two servants also hold the patient to prevent movement during the extension. Then, rotate the arm in all directions after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around his hand. When the doctor handles the joint with both hands, smear it with oil so that the joint can slide easily.
Then, push the joint forcefully until it returns to place. After it is reduced, it should be bandaged with a wrapping that includes binding and drying with egg white, tightly secured, and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then, if the joint remains in place, remove the binding. If you see that the joint has not healed properly, reapply the bandage and binding for several days until it strengthens, then remove it. If swelling occurs after the joint is reduced and the bindings are removed.
Difficulty in movement, so use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and kneading until it becomes flexible or make a splint like a fat ram on the joint then tie it and leave it for a day and night then remove it and enter him into the bath, if he sweats then rub the joint moderately.
Then apply the splint again a second and third time while entering the bath until it becomes flexible, and if you wish to put moistened light ox-plaster with fat on it and bind it tightly, do this several times for it will become flexible and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of dislocating the wrist.
The wrist of the hand often dislocates and reducing its dislocation is easier compared to other joints. However, it is necessary to quickly reduce its dislocation at the moment it occurs before the area swells or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce its dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, have an assistant stretch the hand, and the doctor should put his palm on the joint protrusion and push until it returns. However, it is necessary to check if the dislocation has moved to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the outer side of his hand on the board during extension and flexion. If the dislocation protrudes to the outer side of the hand, then let the inner side of his hand be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls on the same joint protrusion. If it returns immediately, otherwise bind it with a bandage to reduce swelling.
Leave it alone and do not repeat it, for it cannot bear it, and you will not be able to correct it after days have passed unless the joint remains out of place, but it does not harm the patient except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been cut or damaged, and there is no remedy for it except to cauterize it, which may be helpful or may not make any difference. Once the wrist is realigned, apply the dressing we described, then bind it and leave it for five days.
Then it is loosened and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult or some stiffness appears, soften it with warm water and rubbing repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Treatment of Separating the Fingers.
Fingers may separate in all directions. When a finger separates to the palm's outer or inner side, extend the finger and push the separation with your thumb until it returns, then bandage the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction it separated from.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight on that day. If it is at night, bind it as described. Continue to release it during the day, exercise it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it separates to the inner side of the hand, binding it towards the same direction and following the initial treatment until it heals. Do likewise if it separates in any other directions.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of spinal hernia.
When a vertebra of the spine or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae have moved from their places, there is no treatment because death quickly comes to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's feces are discharged without will, unable to hold it back, and often some of his limbs become weakened, either his legs or his arms or one of them. As for if a single vertebra moves from its place, it often returns to its position.
Its movement can be in four directions. The one that moves backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should consider if the hump occurred in youth, then there is no treatment and no cure at all. As for those that occur due to a fall or blow or something similar.
The earlier authorities have mentioned various treatments at great length, most of which do not prove beneficial. I have condensed what they said so that a little of my explanation suffices instead of much of what they provided, by my approximation of the meaning and my clarification thereof. I have also depicted the instrument differently from how they explained and illustrated it.
I say that the hump which occurs in front on the chest cannot be treated and has no cure. The same applies to those occurring on the sides. Only the one that appears on the back specifically can be treated as I am describing, and that is by laying the patient face down.
On a flat surface near a wall, place a wet cloth underneath him to prevent his chest from being harmed. Then place a vertical board fixed in a hole in the ground at one end of the surface near his head, and another board near his feet at the other end of the surface.
A servant holds the board, and it should not be secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other one in that manner, then wrap it around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the board at his head and tie it there.
Then secure with another bandage over his hips, above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bindings and tie them to the other board at his feet. Then each servant pulls the board in his direction with the binding, and the two boards do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not secured as we said, and the doctor places his hands on the hernia with force until it returns, or he places a board on it then leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board that is about three cubits long.
And dig in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place where one end of the board can fit. Then place the center of the board on the bulge and have the doctor put his legs on the other end and pull tightly until it presses on the hernia.
And it returns to its place. If you wish to make it with a hand-turned winch, then drive into the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the bench two boards that are an arm's length long, and at his feet at the other end of the bench also two boards.
