And let there be a cubit between each board, and make a hole in each board through which the winch can pass. Secure all the boards firmly in the ground so that they do not move at all, and insert a round rod, which is the winch where the rope is wound, into the holes of the two boards.
And at its end is a hole in which a stick, a cubit long, is tightly secured with what is coiled. And on the other two boards something similar to that. Then tie the strap which you tightened at the chest of the patient to the spindle which is at his head and the straps which you tightened at his legs to the spindle which is at his feet.
Then, a servant stands at each spindle, turning his hand with the crank that coils the spindle, and the doctor adjusts the hump as mentioned. This is an image of the spindle, the board, and the patient:.
Then, after the vertebra returns and the area is straightened, it is necessary to apply a bandage dried with egg white, then padding. Then place on top of the bandage a splint made of a board that is about three fingers wide or so, and its length should be enough to cover the area of the hump.
And on some of the correct beads, then it is tied with the appropriate strap, and the patient should use mild food until healed. If some protrusion remains in the area at the end of healing, it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with using the board we described for a long time.
A sheet of lead may be used for that. Sometimes a protrusion appears at the end of the spine beads, and it may be mistaken for dislocation, but it is actually an extra bone that has protruded. Do not treat this with such treatment, as death might occur.
The Thirty-First Chapter.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and they do not suffer what other joints suffer from such as slight displacement and concavity. The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways.
That is, it can be dislocated inward, outward, forward, and backward. It most often dislocates inward and rarely dislocates forward or backward. The sign of its dislocation inward is that if you align the healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg will appear longer.
The knee will protrude more than normal, and the affected individual will not be able to bend their leg at the joint. The area adjacent to the joint will show明显肿胀，因为股骨的头部已经移位到了那里。
The signs of outward dislocation are the opposite of these symptoms. For forward dislocation, he will fully extend his leg, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee. If he attempts to walk, he will not be able to move forward.
He will retain urine, his ankle will droop, and when walking, he will step on his heel. For backward dislocation, he cannot extend the knee and cannot bend it before bending the ankle, and his leg will also be shorter than the other.
The ankle will be relaxed, and the head of the femur will be明显位于髋部。对此类脱位的复位，你需要观察；如果脱位已久，伴随者没有尝试复位且保持原状，则完全无法治疗。
It should not be exposed to it, and as for the one that was recently dislocated, whether it is any of the four types of jaw dislocation, you should immediately twist the joint and extend it inward and outward and move it right and left, perhaps.
If it returns without needing other treatment, then if it does not return this way, you should prepare a servant as a strong aid to extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee on his leg, and another servant to pull him from above.
By inserting his hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage at the base of the thigh, and another third servant holds the end of the bandage, extending it either from the front, from the side of the armpit to the side of the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
They should all extend simultaneously until the patient is lifted off the ground and remains suspended. This type of extension is common to all four types. If the jaw returns with what we have said, otherwise each type requires the specific treatment I will mention.
As for its specific reduction if the jaw is dislocated inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, then a bandage should be placed at the base of the thigh between the head of the femur and the area below the armpit.
Then extend the bandage upward from the armpit to the upper body towards the clavicle. Then another strong servant should take hold with his arms, embrace the thick part of the patient's thigh, and pull outward strongly, then it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment used to replace this organ. If it is difficult for you and does not respond to this type of treatment at all, you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong flexible bandage around the ankles and the knees.
There should be a space of four fingers between each one and the other, and the affected leg should be stretched out more than the other by two fingers. Then, suspend the patient from a beam in the house, being two arms' lengths above the ground.
Then, you order a strong servant to hold the thigh and another servant to hold the patient. The other servant holding the thigh should push with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly. As for the specific realignment if the joint is dislocated outward.
The patient should lie on the bench as described for the hunchback case, and a bandage should be tied specifically to his affected leg and chest. Then, place two boards, one at his feet and the other at his head. Place an additional board in the middle of the bench and secure it very well.
It should be wrapped with moist cloths to prevent injury to the patient, so that the board is between his thighs to prevent it from moving downward during stretching. Then, each servant pulls in his direction and the doctor aligns the hip with his hands, if it responds to being returned.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it as mentioned in the case of the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. If the dislocation is forward, you should stretch the patient’s leg once while he lies in this manner on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the diseased hip, then presses it with the other hand, and thus extends the pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, it should not be extended downward.
It should be elevated from the ground; it is preferable to place it on something solid. It should also be positioned outward as we mentioned regarding his posture on the bench, lying on his stomach with the bandages tightly secured.
As we said before, it is also advisable to use pressure with a board on the place to which the joint has moved. If the hip joint returns completely as it should, its return will not be hidden from you, and that is when you can extend the patient's legs.
When you see them level and the patient can bend and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the part has returned as it should. At this point, join the thighs, place the dressing, and bind with a turban tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then loosen the bandages and dressing. Compare the leg with the other one; if you see them equal in measure, know that the setting has held, and allow the patient to walk. If you notice any laxity, however.
Return and dress it, binding it as you did initially. Leave it for another three days, then loosen it. Walk slowly on it for a few days until it strengthens well, if God wills.
The Second and Thirty-Third Chapter.
On treating the dislocation of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three directions: inward, outward, and downward, which means backward. It never dislocates forward at all. The sign of its dislocation is when you order the patient to draw his leg towards his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. The method to correct all its dislocations is to make the patient sit with his legs stretched out if he is able to do so. Have a servant sit behind him to hold his middle and bend him slightly backward. Then you sit on his thighs.
You adhere your back to his front, place his leg between your legs, then grasp his knee with both hands and interlock your fingers around it. Then you press the sides of his knee firmly with both hands, and another servant stretches his leg until the knee returns to its proper place.
The sign that it has returned is when the leg adheres to the thigh in a relaxed manner, not forced. Then dress its wound and adhere the leg to the thigh, then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. Only light walking should be done for several days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it as described, then use strong stretching with bandages, which I have previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On treating the dislocation of the ankle.
The heel may be dislocated slightly or completely. Its dislocation can either be inward or outward, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight dislocation, it is easily reduced by gently stretching with both hands and aligning until it returns to place. As for the treatment if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit down and a strong servant should hold him from behind in the middle of his back.
Then grasp his foot with your right hand from the top and with your left hand at the ankle. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then do it again with your left hand twice, then stretch it with your left hand and push the sole of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as described, then on the third time, push the sole of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the ankle. If it returns in one or two tries and you see the foot is aligned, otherwise repeat the procedure as it will return to place.
If it does not return as described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground and drive a peg into the ground very securely between his thighs, wrapped with cloths so as not to harm the patient, then have an attendant hold his thigh steady.
Then another attendant stretches the leg, either with his hands or by using a strap tied around the ankle. Each attendant then alternates in stretching while the peg remains between the patient's thighs to prevent his body from being pulled downward during the stretch. The doctor then aligns the dislocation with his hands.
Another servant holds the healthy leg downward, then the dislocation returns quickly. Once the dislocation has returned and you are certain of its proper return, apply the dressing and splint and secure it with tight bandages and immobilize the foot with a bandage downward. You should be cautious of the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight and does not harm him, then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage becomes loose, tighten it again and release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, do not be assured that the dislocation will not recur and become spoiled, and it will not accept treatment afterward. If a hot swelling occurs, you should use what we have previously described in another section of the treatment and prolonging until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
It should be aligned gently without force where it has become dislocated, and this is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints on the top of the foot, the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
or on a board while standing as if walking. Then you place your foot on top of the dislocated joints and press them with your foot firmly until it returns and you see that it is aligned and no swelling appears in the area.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that covers the entire foot, which has two ends. Then secure it tightly for three days, then release it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are assured it will not recur, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of jaw dislocation that occurs with a wound or with a fracture or both together.
When any of that occurs and you treat it and set it, it is often followed by death.
Therefore, none but a skilled, experienced, tender-hearted, compassionate, patient, and not reckless or bold person should undertake such treatment. At the beginning, only use medicines that relieve hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
Except for what you hope will be safe from damage with the lightness of the disease and some hope appears to you, then do not delay treating it at the first moment before a hot swelling occurs.
If the part returns as you desired, then use treatments that relieve hot swellings and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments. If the jaw is fractured and fragments of bone are detached, then extract them. Follow in this what we have mentioned regarding simple diseases as presented earlier in their respective sections.
Exert your best effort and refrain from entering paths of risk as previously advised. That will preserve your reputation and keep you safe, if God wills.
